THE RED PAGE.
SERENADE.
(After Kurner.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Cradled in the silent night,
Life is sweetly sleeping ;
Longing makes the darkness bright,
Love his watch is keeping.
Bound about me come and go,<j|
Ghosts and spirits lonely,
Yet I linger here below,
Faithful to thee only.
Gracious maiden, hearest thou ?
Why so long delaying ?
Liest thou in slumber now,
Deep in dreamland straying ?
Nay, thou movest not in dreams,
For my spirit, yearning,
Thro’ thy curtain sees the gleams
Of thy lamp, still burning.
Ah ! so glance thou, child most kind,
From thy window bending ;
Softly ! how the evening wind
Sweet with song is wending.
And the wind shall truly hear,
Bear my thoughts above me,
And shall whisper in thine ear,
Where thou art—“ I love thee.”
When the earnest lover sings
True love listens, waking ;
But the night to morning wings,
I your rest am breaking.
Slumber till the morning light
Breaks in day the clearest;
Until morning, then—Good night 1
Good-night, thou, my dearest!
AHAU.
A Hundred Years After.
JULY 24tli was the centenary of Alexander
Dumas pere ; born at Villers-Cotterets, on
July 24, 1802. The French are fond of
celebrating centenaries. A few months
ago they solemnly made obeisance to the memory
of Victor Hugo. To-day it is the turn of Dumas.
Next year George Sand will be exhumed to be
extolled. These occasions are useful for stock-
taking ; and, when an author is not only remem-
bered but seriously feted a century .after coming
“out of the everywhere into here,” it goes far to
warrant the conclusion that he really is a fixed
point in the sky, not a meteor blazing for a
moment between nothingness and oblivion, nor a
mere rocket, ineffectually shooting towards star-
hood.
The best way of honouring an author is to read
him. Another way is to imitate him. Dumas is
both read and imitated. Millions have enjoyed
his romances; scores have copied his methods.
If he has kept many readers delightfully awake,
he has also enabled many authors comfortably to
sleep. What are the Stanley Weymans, what
were the Harrison Ainsworths, but Dumas-
ketiers ?
The genius of Dumas was the consequence of a
singular blend of bloods. His grandfather married
a negress, and his father was born in San Domingo.
He was never wholly civilised. There is a bar-
baric note in all his books and plays, whilst over
some of them the trail of the splendid savage is
deeply impressed. The Titanic energy with which
he produced is without a parallel; this, with his
utter lack of artistic conscience, his Bacchic scorn
of the conventions, his reckless prodigality and
his deep-hearted joyousness, were endowments
that, like the fluid within his veins, came of his
Equatorial ancestry. The soul of the man was
sun-burnt.
; His father was like a character from one of
Dumas’ own romances. He was a General in the
Republican army which, in face of immense odds,
magnificently held France for the French against
all Europe in the days that followed the Revolu-
tion and prepared the way for Napoleon. Promo-
tion was by merit in those days, and father
Dumas was a General 20 months after he had
enlisted as a private. At the age of 30 he com-
manded a French army in the Tyrol. His feats
as a soldier are only casually mentioned by
Alison (“History of Europe,” 11., 495), though
he acknowledges that by the successes of General
Dumas in capturing the Mont Cenispasses “ the
whole ridge of the Alps separating Piedmont from
Savoy fell into the possession of the Republican
Generals, and the keys of Italy were placed in the
hands of the French Government.'’ But D’Ar-
tagnan never did finer things in battle than did
the father of the novelist—or accQinplished mar-
vels by more novel means. He took Mont Cenis
by assault. Some defences had to be surmounted.
“Arrived at the palisades, the soldiers began to
clamber up;. but,” records the son, “ in} 7 father,
thanks to his Herculean strength, found a simpler
and less noisy plan, which was to take each man
by the seat of his trousers and by his shirt-collar,
and to throw him over the stockade.” One cannot
imagine a modern British general doing a thing
like that; it is not provided for in the Army regu-
lations. Single-handed at Clausen, the Creole
Horatius held the bridge against an Austrian
regiment.
Dumas tried hard to be a classic, and succeeded
in spite of his own efforts. That sounds like a
paradox, but it is no more than the truth. He
wanted to write tragedies like Corneille. He
might as well have endeavored to write another
“Paradise Lost.” Had Dumas attained his
ambition, and produced pieces true to pattern, he
would not have been the Dumas we know
—the original, swift, headlong man of genius,
whose pages are cataracts of incidents whipped
into the froth of passion. To imitate the
classics is an excellent occupation for second-
rate men; it is essential to fail in that
object, and to realise an original self in the
attempt, in order to be a first-rate man. M.
Hippolyte Parigot, the latest French critic of
Dumas, aptly cites a story. A monk, on a fast-
day, when fish was the orthodox fare, had a plump
pullet placed before him. It was savoury; he
was hungry. “In the name of Bacchus, Comus
and Momus, I baptise thee carp,” said he and
fell-to. The earlier works of Dumas are romantic
pullets, though he might baptise them classical
carp. He even tried to give a religious tinge to
some of his plays—he, Dumas, did ! The critics
condemned “ Caligule.” He said they did so
“ because it contained a great deal too much piety
for the rogues.” He introduced Mary Magdalene
as miraculously converting one of the young ladies
of his tragedy. That from Dumas, who, had he
known Mary, would have so injured her reputa-
tion as to put it beyond reparation, even by a
cart-load of Papal benedictions ! Dumas’ pose as
an apostle tickled Thackeray, who said: “We
have people in England who write for bread, like
Dumas and Sand, and are paid so much for their
lines ; but they don’t set up for prophets. Mrs.
Trollope has never declared that her words are
inspired by Heaven ; Mr. Buckstone has written
a great number of farces and not talked about the
altar and the tabernacle. Even Sir Edward
Bulwer . . . never ventured to say that he had
received a Divine mission and was uttering five-
act revelations.” But Dumas was capable of all
that, while his life was a prolonged orgy. In
France, it was not usual to start new religions, or
he would have done so ; and ho might even have
claimed that the D’Artagnan romances were
revealed to him by the Virgin Mary.
But the plays ol Dumas, if still read, -are no
longer a force in the theatre. They had their
vogue as part of the romantic movement in which
Hugo and Gautier figured. They fill 25 volumes,
and are full of entertainment, adultery, assigna-
tion, assassination and machination. He kills six
people in the first act of one play, after which
there is a fatal duel, an agonised death by poison-
ing, and a resurrection of the ghosts of numerous
slaughtered victims. It is all very lively, though
Thackeray found the dramas of Dumas “pro-
foundly immoral and absurd.” His plots are
marvels of ingenuity. The carpentering is su-
perbly done, but the characters are nearly all
villains. The man with a Divine mission never
converted his own puppets. Somebody once said
that Dickens never created a gentleman. Did
Dumas, whether in play or novel, ever create a
character who was not more or less of a black-
guard ? Some of them do gentlemanly things
occasionally, but that is another matter. One
admires the delicate tact of Henry of Navarre
(“Marguerite de Valois”) in pretending not to
notice anything when he knows that his wife has
a lover—M. de Mole, a fascinating rascal —con-
cealed in a cabinet in her bed-chamber. But then
Marguerite knew that he had a key to the apart-
ments of the fair Madame De Sauve on the
next floor of the Louvre, and was kind enough not
to mention that-either. There must be love and
let love in these matters.
In Ins fiction Dumas essayed to do for France
what Scott—whom he acknowledged as his master
—had done for Britain, to depict the past in a
series of vivid romances, Certainly he had imagi-
nation enough, but he had absolutely no concern
for historical veracity. Scott’s romances are the
works of an antiquary and a scholar. He knew the
periods in which he worked. It is true that in a
note to “ Ivanhoe” Scott denies that “ the author
of a modern antique romance is obliged to confine
himself to the introduction of those manners only
which, it can be proved to him, absolutely existed in
the times he is depicting, so.that lie restrain him-
self to such as are plausible and natural, and con-
tain no obvious anachronism.” But Dumas cared
no more for anachronism than a polar bear cares
for the Sphinx, whilst Scott’s departures from his-
tory are generally warranted on the ground of
giving verisimilitude to the story or the breath of
actuality to the character—just as a painter might,
in a historical canvas, represent a leading figure in
a blue cloak instead of a red one to suit the colour-
scheme of his composition. Undoubtedly Dumas
took pains to inform himself, both by original
research, and by paying—or owing—others to
search for him. He freely stole from scandalous
memoirs, aud cribbed from the chroniclers. But
all he wanted was a few pegs upon which to hang
the fabric which he spun from his own teeming
brain. Rarely does he arrest the flow of his romance
to describe, as Scott does, with such fulness of
knowledge and fond enthusiasm, a historical
scene and its accessories. Bishop Spratt admirably
said of the learning of Cowley that “it was not
embossed on his mind but enamelled.” So was
Soott’s ; but that of Dumas was just “ washed in,”
as the artists say.
In “The Three Musketeers” he makes Milady
speculate that D’Artagnan “ is going to transport
her to some loathsome Botany Bay, some infamous
Tyburn of the Indian Ocean.” As the action of
the novel takes place before the middle of the
seventeenth century —it opens in 1625 —and Cook
did not discover Botany Bay till 1770, Milady was
unduly anticipating an infliction for this country,
which has produced enough evil females of its
own. To Botany Bay likewise, in hi# play of
“ Kean,” Dumas represents the Prince of Wales
as about to transport the eminent tragedian, who
gallantly rescues an innocent maiden whom Lord
Melbourne endeavors to abduct. There is a great
quarrel between the Prince and Kean in the
theatrical dressing-room of the latter. Kean
insults the Prince, who exiles him as aforesaid,
but graciously commutes the decree to one of
banishment to New York, where the actor marries
the lady himself. Perhaps the most audacious
thing Dumas ever did in the way of turning history
Upside down, was in his account of the overthrow of
Charles I. by Cromwell, in “Twenty Years After.”
It is a delightfully hopeless mix-up; one of the
wildest pieces of invention that ever romance set
to paper. It is delirium tremens history—but it is
an exciting story. Those who want the actual
facts can get them from Gardiner, but they will
be more entertained with “Twenty Years After.”
For downright wickedness the women of Dumas
are unmatched. He did paint a few decent
women, but his gallery includes a collection of
females the most cruel, ferocious, deceitful,
lying, lustful, lewd and luxurious who ever wore
petticoats and fondled lovers. Take the women
of his “Marguerite de Navarre.” Except the
King’s mistress, Marie Touchet, who is a tender,
harmless, little figure, all the rest —queens,
princesses, court ladies and waiting wenches —
are strumpets to a woman. Shakespeare’s worst
woman. Lady Macbeth, is a saint in comparison
with some of them; and Dumas’ worst, the Queen-
mother, is a loathsome ogre, to whom torture is a
joy, murder a relaxation, and adultery a tame
device for passing away the time.
But say the worst of Dumas, and he still re-
mains the most fascinating of story-tellers. His
very recklessness is part of his charm. If one
wants sober history, there are the serious his-
torians, in their conflicting schools, eaeh accusing
the other of doing insidiously what Dumas did
without a blush. His practices were dubious. He
employed a corps of “ gentlemen ”to write scenes
which he invented, just as some of the great
artists of antiquity, like Titian, employed others
to paint in portions of their canvases. He was
the architect —why should he not have his hod-
carriers and his stone-masons? Thus, presum-
ably, he would have argued. So that the story
rushed through the volume, dodging in and out
among the side-plots, or so that the rivulets that
he started flowing in his opening chapters met
and gathered in the confluence of the narrative at
the proper point, the style could take care of it-
self. He must have enjoyed his work as he en-
joyed his life—and that was as reckless as his
work. “ They say I have been extravagant, he
said to his son in his dying hours. “ Yet 1 came
to Paris with only a Napoleon in my pocket, and
see,” pointing to the shelf, “I have one still!
With all his faults we could spare many a great
teller of tales before we could spare him who told
of Monte Cristo, and who sent swaggering across
the world the clanking figures of Athos, Porthos,
Aramis, and D’Artagnan.
D.B.
Philosophy from Schiller.
The Highest.— Seek you the highest, the
greatest ? The plant can teach you the secret.
It is this: Be with will, what the plant is,
without!
The True Ideal.—All men may know what you
think ; your own is what lies in your feeling.
Is He to be as your own ? Feel then the God
of your thought.
The Key. —Would you yourself know, observe
the evolving of others But, would you
understand them, then you must scan your
own heart.
My Faith. —“ What’s my religion ? ” you ask me.
None of the many mentioned by you. And
why none ? My religion forbids it!
The Best State.— Do you ask how I know
the best state ? By even the method of
knowing good women. Friend, nobody talks
of these two.
Fbiend and Foe. —Precious to me is my friend ;
and also my foe is of value. My friend points
me out what I can ; my enemy what I should,
do.
Science. —Science to one is holy, a heavenly
goddess; to another, only an excellent cow
existing to butter his bread.
Goodness and Greatness.— Two great virtues
there &*©. O, were they at all times united !
Alwayi'the Good with the Great, always the
Great with the Good.
The Bond of Union.— How does Nature proceed
in welding the greatest and meanest of man-
kind ? She sets vanity only between.
—*.
“The true test of art is sincerity,” saith John
Ruskin, and proceeds to glorify some very crude
paintings and some very naive literature. Well
if sincerity is art, welcome to the ranks of men of
letters George Bradley, of Half Ounce, Grey
Valley, M.L., whose book of poems, “Richard,
the King of New Zealand,” is obviously thoroughly
sincere, full of the serene sincerity of the ancient
anchorites who lived on a locust or two a day and
a sprinkle of water once a lifetime, in the trust
that such things were for blessedness and Eternal
Life. What man, if he were not sincere (so sincere
as to have that absolute atrophy of the sense of
humor which is the test of genuine, 24-carat
sincerity) could write and publish this ;
PRAYER.
Oh, God guide and guard all those that roam,
Guard and guide our Premier home ;
Guide o’er our mighty main
To rule o’er once again.
Guard him back to New Zealand ;
Rule him with Thy Mighty hand ;
Watch o’er him where e’er he goes,
And banish all his bitter foes,
Amen!
And this again of Kingßichard’s visit to England
on the occasion of the Diamond Jubilee of Queen
Victoria ( verb, et lit.) :
Ohobas— Oh, that knelt-before the throne,
And saw the Pope of Rome
We can thank our Dick
That’s now landed home.
They talk about the Rulers—
We let them talk away,
I say we have the champion
Ruler of the day.
When I read in the papers
All seems like a dream,
That our noble Dick
Should kneel before the Queen.
Ohobas —Oh, should kneel before the Queen
It seems like a dream,
That our noble Dick
Should appear before the Queen.
“The Prayer” printed above, was sung by the
composer at a banquet given to King Seddon at
Greymouth; the poem from which we have
quoted was written to the tune of ‘ ‘ The Wearing
of the Green ”by the bard—a simply pathetic cir-
cumstance, since “The Wearing of the Green” is
the only song of which Mr. Seddon knows the
music, and he is therefore able, without the trouble
of adding to his musical education, to sing the
noble paean to himself in moments of relaxation.
This bard of Greymouth inspires us with great
hopes of poetry in Maoriland ; he has the first
essentials of great writing—sincerity and a simple
purpose. Will not the rest come in time ?
* :
Ambrose Pratt (who is just now in Sydney) has
secured through his literary agent in London a
very good contract for a series of short stories.
“A.D.”: “Jef’s” experience with “Henry V.”
(B. 12/7/’O2) recalls a somewhat similar experience
during my Adelaide Matric. The Odes of Horace
were among the Latin text books. Our unco guid
preceptors (following the virtuous example of Mr.
Gladstone) religiously ignored the last stanzas of
the first ode (Bk, iv.). In the exam, we were set
the complete ode for translation —but we did not
share Jef’s discomfort. I think every candidate,
male and female, gave a better translation of the
last portion than of the first, so that out of evil
came good.
Henry Lawson arrived in Sydney last week,
having hastened his departure from London on
account of illness. Lawson is suffering much from
nerve trouble,- and is now under medical treatment;
but the bright skies of sunny Sydney should soon
set him up again.
Some Correspondents—
F.J.B. writes: “A.P. (Red Page, 5/7/’O2) says that
Nietzsche, in * Also spraeh Zarathustra,' evidently bor-
rowed largely from Blake, but that he had not Blake’s
sane physiological basis, so landed himself in a lunatic
asylum. What is this cant about the breakdown of
Nietzsche’s intellect? He paid the penalty of intense
over-work, just as Swift or Scott, or Paschal, not to men-
tion others, paid it before him. Blake, on the contrary,
was never sane. He was born mad. What is the mental
condition of a man who draws the picture of the soul of a
flea, and who plays at Adam and Eve with his wife in a
summer-house in ‘ the altogether’ ? But the whole thing
is absurd. Blake is hardly known to his own countrymen,
and he is certainly less known to cultivated Germany than
is Nietzsche to. cultivated English speakingdom. Lamb
was the first to make literary allusion bo Blake, and folk
treated Lamb’s appreciation of him as something not
altogether to his own intellectual credit. Swinburne
made Blake known in more recent times ; but even Swin-
burne’s wild worship has not succeeded in making Blake
known to more than curious students of the by-ways
and back-alleys of literature. Nietzsche’s attitude towards
English literature and English thought is one of pro-
nounced contempt. Also he was very honest. Of all to whom
he owed an intellectual debt he made full acknowledg-
ment. He never spoke of Blake, and probably would have
treated the mention of his name as referring to a noted
English admiral of a long-past day. When ‘ A.P.’ wants
to class Blake with any other writer, let him take
M‘Don aid Clarke, the mad poet of America."
Christopher Crisp (returning to the subject of “Im-
perialism and Democracy ”): The “ big American Repub-
lic ” is a despotism plus rings, and has no real national
existence or dignity. The “little Swiss Republic is
merely a municipalised Edinburgh: The “ great French
Republic” is at the mercy of any Boulanger on a black
horse, and it lives on its Napoleonic glories, its Legion of
Honour, and its ancient nobility. Its feverish adulation
of the Russian alliance shows how keenly it feels its
plebeian forlornness as a bar sinister Republic. Great
Britain, in spite of the banalities (and worse) of many of
its monarchs, is at the head of civilisation now, distinctly
and solely because of the beneficent and magnificent
potentiality in every corner of the world of its system of
responsible government under the Crown.
(Which is an excellent illustration of the fact that one
can prove anything—even the non-absurdity of a monarchy
—if one is allowed tfo ignore all facts. Certainly, if the
American Republic has “no real national existence or
dignity ; ” if the Swiss Republic (which has rolled back
from its borders time and again the armed hosts of
Empire, and has given to the world priceless examples in
the preservation of Liberty, and the designing of
Federal Government) is a mere municipality ; if the
French Republic (which did not fall before Boulanger
on a black horse, nor yet before all the forces
of priests and soldiers conspiring together for its
destruction) lives on its ancient nobility—its proscribed,
dispossessed and absolutely worthless ancient nobility ;
then certainly Republicanism is a failure, and hereditary
monarchy the One true principle of Democracy : also the
sun circles round the earth and is eaten up by a dragon
every night.]
To W. : None of the “ steals ” you cite has any present

W.G.W. : Not a single newspaper gave the late Brunton
Stephens’ birthplace correctly. There is no such place in
Scotland as Barrowstonness, but there is a well-known
dirty hole of a place called Bo’ness, or Borrowstonness,
on the Firth of Forth. There is also a neighboring shanty
village named Blackness, both contiguous to Lord Hope-
toun’s lordly domain.

A Lesson Worth Learning
Hsepatitis and Dyspeptic Misery:
11 A BLESSING TO WORK & LIVE,”
The Case of
Mrs. B. EAGAR.
(For the 'Redfern Times.)
It is impossible to read the following
statement of the condition of things de-
scribed by Mrs. Bertha Eagar, of No. 145
Walker-street, Redfern, without being
struck by certain phases and facts in her re-
cital. Carrying her memory back eleven
years, Mrs. Eagar said :
“ It is that length of time since I was re-
leased from a complaint of such great ob-
stinacy that there was every reason to think
I would never be the same woman again.
Why I say this is because my sufferings ex-
tended over a period of six months or
more, and half that time I was under the
care of a doctor.”
“ What complaint was he treating you
for?” asked our reporter.
“ My ailments were as numerous as they
were difficult to cope with, but I am of
opinion that they were occasioned by a dis-
eased state of my liver. Be that as it may,
I know that indigestion was one of the prin-
cipal features of my illness, and I became so
seriously affected by it that before long I
was a completely helpless invalid. When I
had been taking medicines prescribed by my
physician till I knew it was useless to drink
any more I did not know what to do next,
for I was so feeble that I could not walk
across the floor. It seemed a cruel thing to
throw so much money away, and waste so
much time for nothing, but when people are
ill, you know, they will spend their last
penny, if necessary, in-buying things which
they expect to do them good. And that is
the very reason why I consider it my duty
to divulge to all mankind the fact that it
was only through using Clements Tonic at
the eleventh hour that, my awful sufferings
were checked. Through reading a kindly-
disposed person’s testimonial I started tak-
ing if, and ever since those w-ords appeared
before me I have regarded them with as
much respect as though they had consti-
tuted a message from an angel above. When
I come to consider how much agony I en-
dured I am devoutly thankful that the right
remedy came at last.”
“ Was it after eating that you felt so
ill ? ”
“ Well, if you like to call it eating, it
was, but my appetite was so very quickly
appeased that I could not take even a
moderate meal. Two or three mouthfuls of
light diet were always enough, and then the
most unmerciful pains in the chest started.
Had you beheld my anguish you would have
been moved, as the suffocating sensations in-
duced such desperate efforts to get my
breath that an onlooker would have feared
I was going to choke. A fearful state of
weakness was brought about, and I was
compelled to lie down the greater part of
the day, my household duties, of course,
being out of the question so far as I was
concerned. A dreadful aching pain settled
across my brow, as if its mission was to
destroy all the pleasures in life, and it cer-
tainly succeeded, for I felt cheerless and
careworn beyond expression. This state of
things was bad enough in the day time, but
when I hid to go through the same horrors
it night, for the want of sleep, my sorrowful
condition can be imagined. Gradually the
tlesh wasted off my bones, and in the mean-
time my skin changed to a pallid hue, while
ilia weaker I got the more my limbs ached.
My lips and hands were as white as if my
flood had turned to water, and when I
•aised my head I became quite giddy. If I
tried te walk upstairs I felt so dazed that I
•ould not go two steps alone, a strange mist
flso appearing before my eyes, which nearly
lways felt swollen and weak, as if I were
offering from a severe cold. Acute pains
truck me between my shoulder-blades, too,
while the aches across my loins were as pain-
ful as they could be. My mouth was never
free from a taste as bitter as gall, and there
was a sticky substance over my tongue
which I could never wash off, these two ad-
verse elements combined making me con-
tinually feel sick.”
“How did your nervous system bear up
against all this ? ”
“Very well for a time, and then my
nerves gave way quite suddenly. The least
excitement produced a fit of trembling, my
mind also being agitated on the slightest
provocation. But, so far as that goes, my
brain always seemed to be in a restless state,
for if I got an hour or two’s doze, it in-
variably meant that I had some kind of ugly
dream, the recollection of which frightened
me on awaking.”
“ You certainly had very little comfort in
life at that time, Mrs. Eagar.”
“ There was really nothing left for me to
live for, and the melancholy thoughts with
which I was seized through my heart pal-
pitating very badly made me heartily sick
and tired of everything ; but when I read
about Clements Tonic I thought I might at
le <st get a little better if I tried it, and I
think my present appearance speaks for it-
self. When my recovery began it was by
acquiring an appetite through the use of
Clements Tonic, and already that medicine
had done me more service than all the other
decoctions put together, because there was
an inward sense of comfort after my food
which told me that digestion was being pro-
perly performed. Ido not wish you to be-
lieve that this wonderful amount of relief
came instantly, because such a thing, con-
sidering the plight I was in, would be im-
possible, but it was certainly the speedy and
direct result of taking Clements Tonic, and,
moreover, I was soon sufficiently eased from
headaches and pains to admit of sound
sleep helping me towards the goal I desired
to reach. The medicine I was taking suited
my complaints splendidly, as the fearful
pains in my chest diminished as each dose
of Clements Tonic was taken, while flatu-
lence and giddiness were remedied quite
mysteriously. Drowsiness and despondency
were wafted away by the medicine, which
was likewise bracing together my shattered
nerves, and its regulating powers had a
beneficial effect which I have enjoyed up to
the present day. My skin soon became
clear and bright, which that
Clements Tonic had a more powerful in-
fluence over my Llood than any physic I had
hitherto purchased, and the unpleasant taste
was altogether removed from my mouth. It
did not take a great deal of Clements Tonic
to rid me of those wearying aches which had
come upon me through excessive weakuess,
and when they were gone there was nothing
tb stop me from doing my housework with
as much activity and cheerfulness as ev r.
Indeed, it was a blessing to work and live,
for before my last bottle of Clements Tonic
was finished I was well and sound in every
respect, and not one of my old ailments has
troubled me since. A lesson like mine
should be taught all invalids, so you can
publish these words in any way you
choose,”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Bertha Eagar, of 145 Walker-street, Kean v.
in the State of New South Wales, Com
monwealth of Australia, do solemnly ami
sincerely declare that I have carefully read
the annexed document, consisting of four
folios, and consecutively numbered from one
to four, and that it contains and is a true
and faithful account of my illness and
cure by Clements Tonic: and also contains
my full permission to publish in any way my
statements—which I give voluntarily, withom
receiving any payment; and I make this solemn
declaration conscientiously believing the tame to lie
true, and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths
Act, 3900.
Subsoribed and declared at Redfern, this twenty-
ninth day of March, one thousand nine hundred
and two, before me,
A. MOLES, J.P.

Strength restored
after Illness
A mother’s interesting letter about
her little daughter
Here is a case of a little child who had
bean suffering with fever and a complication
of ailments which had reduced her system
to a state of extreme weakness. The way
she was restored to health and strength is
told in the following letter: —
“ Fire Station, Brooke Road,
“ Stoke Newington, London, England,
“ October 21st, 1900.
“Gentlemen, —I have great pleasure in
writing to thank you for the great benefit
our little daughter of four years old has
derived from taking Scott’s Emulsion. In
February last she was stricken down with
fever, followed by congestion of the lungs,
pneumonia and abscesses, which held her
between life and death for some days. A
4m
I
f
MARJORIE SARGENT.
(From a photograph.)
friend recommended your Scott’s Emulsi n.
which she has been taking for some mouths,
and it has certainly been the means of
restoring her to health and strength. She
is now looking well and strong.—l am, yours
truly,
“ (Signed) Agnes Sargent.”
Scoit’s Emulsion certainly deserves the
praise it receives from those who take it.
It is a standard preparation of cod-liver oil,
and has more us s in the human system
than any other remedy which is offered to
the public. Scott’s Emulsion has overcome
all of the objections formerly urged against
cod-liver oil. It is perfectly palatable, so
that it is not objectionable to the most
sensitive palate, and it does not tax the
organs of digestion.
Scott’s Emulsion gives the system strength
and vitality. It is a specific for coughs,
colds, and all ailments of throat and lungs,
and in addition to the properties of a
specific, it offers the most vital principles
of food in the easiest form for absorption
into the blood. The result of taking Scott’s
Emulsion is an increased appetite, better
digestion of food, renewed strength, and
vigour in all the vital organs.
Scott’s Emulsion must not be confounded
with other forms of cod-liver oil, for it is
peculiar to itself, and differs from any other
preparation. The only way to derive the
benefits which we are revealing from day to
day by the publication of letters from those
who have tried our preparation, is to insist
upon having the genuine Scott’s Emulsion,
which is easily distinguished by our trade
mark of a man holding a big fish over his
shoulder. You will certainly be well paid if
you will be careful to buy only the genuine
Scott’s Emulsion, for the merits of this
preparation are well known both to the
medical profession and to the public.
You can obtain a sample of Scott’s Emulsion
by sending fourpence to Scott & Bowne, Ltd.,
483 Kent-street, Sydney, and mentioning this
paper. All chemists sell Scott’s Emulsion.

Bards of the Backblocks
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
World-Makers.
Hear the whisper of hopeless things in this time
of the twilight pale,
Told by the tongues that cling to the wings of the
drowsy eastern gale,
Sobbing the story of broken life, beaten agains*
the dust,
Of eyes that dry in the lonely years, and lips set
hard to the crust.
Come out, oh, heart, from your drowsiness, and
know of the hearts that beat,
The march of the dead in the milking-shed, to the
clatter of creaking feet.
’Tis a long, long way in a cruel day over a hope
less track—
And this is the word of the driving wind, the crj
of the man out-back.
There came a lie in his golden youth ; full-blooded '
and fair he went,
And drove his strength on the eastern hills, and
dreamed in the midnight tent.
The whistling gums fell back at the stroke, ti e
slab roof dared the sun,
Till the strong soul smiled in the summer bush for
a weary work well done ; <
He took her away from the olden home to the roo f
on the chestnut rise,
And side by side thro’ the hard day tide they
labored with streaking eyes. i
Oh, the load is sore to their shoulders now, the I
hot brain heaves a wrack—
God keep a place in His halls of grace for the soul) '
of the folk out-back !
When the red cows died at the black well-side
and the locusts clanged in the ferns, i
She lay face fair to the steaming roof and gave tr
the world her bairns.
With never the soothe of a tender hand, but his
for the wrenching pain,—
Who cares that the brown mouth smiles no more ’
—for the hand’s eternal stain ?
She eats her sup with a bended head, and sips the
dregs set-faced,
And the bitterness bites her heart to dust, the late
bairn cries at her waist.
Oh, the children come and the children stay, the
dam goes black and black—
But the sword of the Lord is hard to the necks of
the folk in the toils out-back !
The gulch yawns black to the purple sky, no sign
of the dawn-stain yet,
The candle gleams from the misty panes where
the meagre meal is set.
The red chains clank in the early dark, and dry
eyes blink and stare
To the East and curse for the laggard flush to
waken the dawn out there.
And shuffling feet in a weariness go out ’neath the
morning star,
That winks white lights on the looming ridge, to
the deeps where the cattle are.
Stifle and sweat, come dawn and dark, no matter
what life may lack,
From the reeking sheds to the hungry fields go
the makers of worlds out-back !
They ravish the shrines of the olden land till the
hidden ways are plain,
Bare-breasted and clean to the summer sheen, and
wait as they may for rain ;
While the fever-limbs of a drought that swims in
the ring of a merciless sun
Writhe down and choke in scourging smoke the
last blades, one by one.
The old man hopes in a frowning Fate, and plans
in the steaming dark ;
And the old wife turns in a restless dream, ashake
from the dingo-bark.
Oh ! a twisting wrench tears out his heart, still
brave, tho’ the lean lips crack,
As he lists the sigh of the bairns near by, worn
old in the years out-back.
This is the whisper of hopeless things, of strong
souls shadow-pale—
The sorrow of people that fashion a world, told by
the driving, gale.
What would you have for the axe that led straight
in the pathless ways
By giving the best of the upland plains a gift tc
the future days ?
What for the women of faith, bowed, brown, worn
lean in the merciless years,
Who willed their lives for the sake of him and the
children they bore in tears ?
)h ! the praise is slow on the way they go, yet
the Lord God knoweth the track ;
Vtayhap He keeps in His halls of grace a rest fo>
the folks out-back.
Vic.
C. L. RIVERS.
If there is any shred of truth in Queens-
land Premier Philp’s pathetic yarn about the
danger of landing the returned Kanaka on his
native island, and the necessity for bringing him
back to Queensland to save him from his savage
acquaintances, it would seem that many years of
missionary effort in Polynesia have been a failure,
and that it is time to cut off the stipend of the
useless and inefficient preacher. Australia has
suffered from vast quantities of information about
the good that was being done in bringing the
brown man to a knowledge of the desirability of
putting his cocoa-nut in the plate on Sunday.
To be told now that he is such a murderous
wretch that his own returned brother often dare
not land is a surprise; and Premier Philp’s demand
that the anti-Kanaka law be set aside in order
that the time-expired brother, after one affrighted
look at his family waiting for him on the shore
with the cooking-pot in its hand, may come
straight back to the Queensland plantations, is a
shock. Somebody must be lying. The idyllic
picture of the church with its thatch of palm-
leaves, and the native preacher, and the glossy
I brown choir, told us nothing of this.

publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceed-
ing two columns in length, or, say, 2000 words, are speci-
ally acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper
clippings. All communications will be regarded as
strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient

for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, AUGUST 2, 1902.
A Lesson from Argentina.
The Argentine spectre was roused again from
its sleep, if it ever does sleep, by Sydney
Telegraph’s article of 21st July, entitled
“ A Comparison.” Argentina is, of all the
world, the one new country which presents the
closest resemblance to Australia ; therefore
it has become customary for Tory papers here
to seize themselves violently by the collar
and ask themselves in a terrible voice ivhy
the Commonwealth is almost stationary while
the Confederation is rapidly going ahead ?
And then they answer their own question
by stating that the malign influence of the
Labor party is responsible, and that our only
hope lies in developing our “national
resources”—by which the Tory paper always
means growing wheat in desert land, and
growing sheep where sheep only die of thirst,
and making railways with borrowed money
to enable people to go into the desert where
nobody w T ants to go, and to carry out there
the goods the handful of local inhabitants
can’t buy, and to bring away from there the
things they haven’t got to sell. As Sydney
Telegraph sorrowfully puts it:
Jin Australia we possess many advantages, real and
assured, over our growing South American rival. We are
far richer in minerals ; our wool is the finest in the world.
We have facilities for borrowing money and importing
capital such as Argentina cannot rival. Yet, strange to
say, while our Governments continue to borrow, the
influx of British capital is checked as far as private enter-
prise is concerned. Since the crisis of 1893 the amount of
British capital employed in Australian banking, and in
the development of the resources of the land, has been
reduced by very many millions, because the openings for
it have been restricted, and the hazards much increased.
There was a considerable advance in agriculture amongst
us after the crisis, for the reason that the JGovernments
were not in a position to compete in the labor market,
and because the country offered more attractions than
the towns. Now, Australia has once again succeeded in
making the towns more attractive than the country, and
production is on the down grade. Yet, are we differ-
ently situated from Argentina, where the President
comes forward and points _ out to the people that
they must “always bear in mind that agriculture
and the pastoral industry are their main sources
of wealth?” True, we can add mining to the list, and
mining appears to be the only point at which Bntisn
capital can directly take the field in Australia, though
there is a pause just now, and the tariff will further
check it. But where is the opening either for the immi-
grant or for imported capital at the present time . if
there is any important work to be executed, the dominant
party in the Legislatures resents anything outside
Government construction. Does that benefit labor, or
starve employment? It evidently does not offer any
inducement to the immigrant, because he does not
come ; and the present exodus to South Africa does not
suggest that the Australian himself derives much benefit
from the condition of affairs.
* • * •
“ Where is the opening for the immigrant
in Australia 1 ” It is a fair question. If the
British farmer, driven out by his country’s
Foreign-trade policy, wants to find a new
home, he might go to Germany, for instance,
where, even if he had to pay away three-fourths
of his wheat crop in rent and taxes, he would
have as much left for himself as he would in
Australia, taking the average return of all
the wheat lands of this country, even if he
got his land here fornothing. He could goto a
comparatively barren land like Sweden, and
if he paid away more than three-quarters of
his wheat crop in rent and taxes, he would
still have as much left for himself as he would
have in Australia, taking the average crop in
each instance, if l(e got his land here free
and paid no taxes whatever. He could go
almost anywhere and get better returns than
he can get in Australia —the only reason why
he doesn’t go almost anywhere more fre-
quently than he does is probably his ignorance
of foreign languages. Under these circum-
stances why should he come to Australia ?
If he wants to raise sheep and cattle—this is
the only sheep and cattle country in the
world whose flocks and herds have perished
wholesale, and which, even in good average
years, receives less than 20 inches of rain
over nearly three-fourths of its area, and less
than 10 inches over nearly half its area. In
such circumstances why should the pastoral
immigrant want to come to Australia, any
more than the agricultural immigrant '(
Even on these dry lands the agriculturist
might scrape out a decent living if he was
producing merely to supply a great Austra-
lian manufacturing population at his own
doors, but when a large portion of his scanty
crop has to pay freight and middlemen’s
charges to a market 16,000 miles away,
where is the inducement ?
* * * 9
As to why artisans don’t come here —the
workers in steel, iron, brass, tinplates,
woollens, cottons, linens, silks, machinery,
and so forth—the reason is obvious enough.
There are no great industries to employ
them, and there can’t be while the Common-
wealth States import £41,000,000 worth of
goods annually from outside, and while their
narrow cult of Foreign-traders persuade the
people that the greatness of a nation is
measured by the greatness of the amount it
imports, and, consequently, the smallness of
the amount it produces for itself. While
Australia takes pride in the sneers of its
ignorant G. Reid and its uncouth Pulsford
and Pumpkin Smith at the obscurity of the
first beginnings of its new industries, it
isn’t likely to offer any inducements that
will attract an influx of artisans. They are
no more likely than agricultural immigrants
to come here, except by the same foolish
mistake that took the New Australians to
Paraguay. As a matter of fact, Australia
never had any great genuine immigration,
except in the first flush of the gold
boom ; and in those glorious later years when
it was floating the first big loans and finding
employment for almost everybody on public
works. Here is the real record for the six
Commonwealth States:—
Immigrants
whose passages iSelf-
were paid Supporting
by the State Immigrants. Total,
(mostly out
of Loans).
1851 to 1860 .. 225,153 343,676 568,828
1861 to 1870 .. 82,005 91,272 173,277
1871 to 1880 .. 107,520 95,502 203,022
1881 to 1890 .. 151,805 224,760 376,565
1891 to 1900 .. 7,750 18,765 26,515
Thegold-boom broughtthegreatinflux of 1851
to 1860 ; also, in these days, the supply of
good land was still far from being exhausted,
and much land was still being given away to
whoever asked for it. But even in the best
of the gold days Australia bought an immense
proportion of its immigrants. The influx of
after years was mostly based on a flashy and
artificial prosperity produced by the influx
of borrowed money. Here is the beautiful
record for the last three decades :
Annual influx
Self-sup- Influx of borrowed of borrowed
porting money (State and money per
Immigra- other loans, bank head of

population.

1891-1900.. 18,765 60,000,000 1 14 7
. * * * *
Immigration practically ceased when the
alluvial gold boom ended, and when Aus-
tralia ceased to pay the passages of its
immigrants, and to present them with a
respectable estate on arrival, and when the
influx of loans wore off a little, and they were
no longer sufficient, as in 1881-1890, to sup-
port half the population. Yet all this influx of
outside money might perhaps have done more
good than harm if it had been invested in
self-supporting industries that would have
obviated the necessity for importing. If a
foreign capitalist came here with £1,000,000,
and took away £250,000 a year in dividends,
still, if his industry enabled the country to
supply itself with some much-needed article
of which it used to import £1,000,000 worth
per annum, the country would be £750,000
a year better off. But of the loans, about
£60,000,000 went merely to avert the ne-
cessity for taxation, and £150,000,000 went
in works that don’t pay interest on their
cost, and almost the whole lot dribbled back
to Europe in payment for imports, and after
all this debt had been acquired the country
was nearly as dependent on imports, and
quite as devoid of any permanent employ-
ment that could possibly attract immigrants,
as before. The insane pride of the cult to
which Pulsford and Reid and M‘Millan
and Pumpkin Smith belong, in the scarcity
of local industries, and the consequent
necessity for immense importations, blocked
progress everywhere. Even the States
which made a half-hearted attempt at Pro-
tection had the same superstition in a less
degree —the superstition that people should
be driven out into the desert at any cost to
pursue the natural industry of growing wheat
on the hobs of Gehenna, and that a huge
trade—the sending away of raw material
that we can’t manufacture for ourselves for
want of big industries to employ capital, and
the importation of manufactured goods that
we can’t produce ourselves for lack of works
to supply work and wages to labor—is pros-
perity.
* 9 * *
Capital doesn’t emigrate here now any
more than labor does, and the reason why it
should be so is very easy to find. The Free-
trade paper has a comforting illusion that
it is because the Labor party insists on
having the £5OOO job of making a new road
done by day-labor, and won’t allow private
enterprise to build a certain lone and
solitary bridge—that over Sydney Harbor
—that great enterprising millionaires don’t
settle in Australia, but, as a matter of
fact, large capitalists never go to any
country that takes such an insane joy in
imports, unless it is a country with a tem-
porary gold boom on hand. The only reason
why they should want to come here and
stay here is to start large manufacturing
industries ; there is nothing else for them to
do that holds out any prospect of permanent
profit. They can’t do that unless they sup-
ply locally some of the demand that is now
filled by imports and thus reduce importa-
tion, and when they see the Senate cutting
down one duty after another on the ground
that it will reduce importation they under-
stand that this is a country which doesn’t
want local industry—that importation is a
disease. Canada, which helps on its indus-
tries with almost prohibitive tariffs, has
between 600,000 and 700,000 workers in its
factories against the Commonwealth’s less
than 200,000, and it certainly does attract
immigrants. It doesn’t mind if it knocks
“importation” endways so long as it sup-
plies abundance of local employment. Argen-
tina began its boom with the Protective tariff
of 1878, and the factories of the city of
Buenos Ayres alone turn out as much value
in finished goods, according to Mulhall’s
estimate, as the factories of Australia,
Tasmania and Maoriland put together ! ! And
about the flow of immigrants into Argentina
there can be no doubt whatever. Argentina
supplied the work by artificial means and
the people came. Australia started at the
other end. It artificially acquired 650,000
people, dating from the beginning of assisted
immigration (they have since, counting in
their children, increased to one or two
millions) by buying them, largely with loan
moneys, under the assisted-immigration
system, but having conscientious scruples
about taking any artificial means to
supply them with sufficient permanent
employment, it keeps a great portion
of them alive out of loans. And its futile
Freetrade press finds comfort in assuring
itself that the whole trouble arises somehow
out of the conduct of the Labor party, or is
owing to the machinations of the Jesuits, or
something equally shadowy.
Local Mis-Government.
It is just as much a maxim of scientific democracy
that no constituency Bhould be wider than the
area governed as it is that no constituency
should be narrower. If in N.S.W. only
one-fourth of the adult population is allowed
a voice in governing the State, it is bad govern-
ment. Also, if in N.S.W., the city of Sydney
is governed as to many of its purely local matters
by the constituency of the whole State, it is again
bad government. Manhood suffrage (with its
coming complement of womanhood suffrage) has
now put the State Government on a fair demo-
cratic basis. But there is as yet hardly a serious
suggestion that local government should be ad-
ministered on the same sound lines. The people of
Sydney are not allowed to govern themselves as
citizens in purely civic relations, but have to sub-
mit to be ruled in part by the people of the whole
State as regards local matters ; and the people of
Bourke, and Booligal, and Bathurst and Bega are
in the same or worse plight. It is inevitable that,
under such a system, the cities and towns suffer,
and the whole State suffers. If purely local
affairs are administered by purely local bodies,
then the consequences of mal-administration and
error, having purely local consequence, will be
promptly felt and rectified ; or if not rectified the
local community continues to suffer, and deserves
to. But if Bathurst is partly governed as to its
local affairs by N.S.W., and mistakes are made,
the consequences are felt by Bathurst, but
not by all N.S.W. and the chance of prompt re-
dress is slight. Bathurst continues to suffer,
but doesn’t necessarily deserve to. The wrong,
the responsibility, the redress should be, in all
matters of Government, intimately connected.
When they are not, when one party can inflict
the wrong, which another party has to suffer,
misgovernment must follow.
* * * *
The case of the city of Sydney is just now a
good example of this. Sydney is allowed to
govern itself to the extent of sweeping its streets
and collecting its garbage. In most other matters
it is governed by the State Cabinet through
Boards, or by private monopolies through
Acts of Parliament. Its water supply, for
instance, is in charge of a Board of Water
Supply and Sewerage, which is to all intents
and purposes a creature of the Government,
and is ruled by a derelict politician of the Parkes
days, who was made its President after the electors
had found him unfitted for further public life.
And this Board, lest the process of the education
of its members by their professional servants
should in time make it a fairly effective body, is
constantly hampered and stringhalted by the
State Cabinet. The Board, even in minor
matters, merely reigns while the Owe’Sullivan
governs; and the eye of the Owe’Sullivan is
naturally fixed more on the electors of Queanbeyan
and the Labor Caucus and the chance of getting
the Premiership than on the necessities of
the city of Sydney. Hence, in part, the pre-
sent threatened water famine, which, should
it come (and there seems every prospect of
its coming), will almost ruin the city. If the
people of the metropolitan district at next munici-
pal elections could deal directly with the persons
responsible for ,the fact that a citizen can’t now
take a plunge bath or water his pet roses, and has
to think seriously of the time when he will not be
able to even flush his drains, probably some-
thing notable would happen. But they can’t.
The people responsible are Mr. G. H. Reid, who
appointed Mr. Jacob Garrard as President of
the Water and Sewerage Board, and Mr. Owe-
Sullivan and Mr. John See who add to the
inefficiency of that Board by hobbling it; and a
hundred and one other persons to whom the
municipal electors are as nothing. Similarly the
people of Bathurst suffer annual typhoid epi-
demics because the State Government persisted in
selling them a water supply pumped from a mud-
hole, and the State Board of Health insisted that
there was no harm in allowing piggeries to be
kept in the close vicinity of that mud-hole ; and
these wrongs were inflicted, not by persons who
are responsible to them and can he punished by
them, but by persons who are responsible to the
whole of N.S.W. and can only be punished by
N.S.W.
* * * *
Local Government would not cure all the abuses
of municipal government. Aldermen would still
be aldermen, and mistakes and scandals would
still occur. But this material step forward would
be gained : That those who did the wrong would
have to answer for it to those who suffered the
wrong. At present the people responsible for the
Sydney water scarcity and the Bathurst water
dirtiness are just as much responsible to
the voters of Hay and Armidale as to
the people of Sydney and Bathurst respectively.
Naturally, the man at Hay can’t work up much
enthusiasm regarding the Sydney man’s bath
limitations or the Armidale citizen about the
germs in the Bathurst water supply. So scan-
dalous misgovernment and maladministration go
unchecked and unpunished.
* * * *
N.S.W. Government will never become decently
efficient or decently honest until a comprehensive
Local Government measure has been made law.
The present system makes the citizen the victim
in his local affairs of the State’s muddling, and at
the same time makes the State treasury the prey of
every dishonest local member with a “pull.” It
is as expensive as it is inefficient, and secures the
least amount of good Government at the greatest
possible cost. The Labor party, when it comes to
recognise its duty, will have to link Local Govern-
ment with Financial Reform as the first plank of
its platform.
The Iron Bonus Bill.
Arthur Rae writes on the other side :
Under the heading of “ The Federal Anti-Labor Party ”
(12/7/’O2), you slate that party for moving the amendment
in the Bill giving a bonus on iron production. That
amendment confines the bonus to iron and steel produced
in State-owned works and completely bars the “ private-
enterprise ” syndicator. You base your denunciation of
the Labor party’s action on the grounds that the N.S.W.
State Govt, “did not intend to start iron works,” and
that the Party was well aware of the fact. That The
Bulletin is consistent in advocating Protection I admit,
but in barracking for protection to private enter-
prise, in opposition to State-owned industry, is most
amazingly inconsistent, in my opinion. Admittedly
a mere resolution, or even an amendment, in a
Bill will not create State iron-works ; but the action
of the Federal Labor Party paves the way for further
.action by the State Labor Party in the desired direction.
Apart from the constitutional point as to the power of
the Commonwealth to itself undertake the iron industry,
the fact that neither lands nor minerals are in possession
of the Federal authorities, while the States possess both,
compels the Labor Party to look to the latter to do the
work. The alleged fact that the State Government
“ does not intend ” to start ironworks need not trouble
us, if those who believe that the State should do so get
to work and force the hand of the State Government by
all possible means. The history of N.S.W. during the last
10 or 11 years is full of instances of things the State
Government “did not intend ” to do, but did all the same,
when compelled by democratic pressure to do or go. Old
Age Pensions, Arbitration Acts, and many other measures
on our statute books were the wild socialistic dreams of
a few years back, and one of the latest instances of the
things the State Government, “did not intend” to do,
but is now getting ready to do, is the establishment of a
State clothing factory. There are dozens of privately-
owned clothing factories against which the State factory
must compete in quality and cost, or be condemned as a
spoon-fed pauper industry. There are no privately-
owned iron-works producing the finished article from the
native ores. If the manufacture of uniforms worn by
State officials is a sufficient reason for running a State
clothing factory, then the tens of thousands of tons of
iron and steel required annually for our railways, tram-
ways, rolling-stock, bridges, and public buildings is an
equally good or better reason for establishing State iron-
works. If the enormous cost of the machinery and plant
of an up-to-date iron and steel works capable of supplying
the public needs of Australia, or even of this State, is
taken into account, it .rrfeans that the first syndicate to
commence, at once creates a powerful monopoly. Mono-
polies are admittedly dangerous alike to the political and
economic freedom of the people. By no Australian
journal has this been so ably and so persistently preached
as by The Bulletin.
* * * *
If State iron-works are good to possess, which is the
easier and the safer way to obtain them ? To create on
the one hand huge vested interests monopolising the
mineral wealth of the State, and subsidised by the whole
Australian people, in order to provide some labor for our-
selves and dividends for absentee shareholders, or to bend
our energies (even if the effort takes a few years to reach
maturity) in establishing State works which will give us
an equal amount of employment under probably better
conditions for the toilers, and retain the dividends in the
hands of the People, who create them ? Against this it
may be urged that to build State works by borrowed
money bearing interest is only paying dividends to
absentee money-lenders in another form, while the people
take the risk. This is only partially true, because no
syndicate will find the capital required unless it can see
its way to earn large dividends reaching probably 25 or
30 per cent., or even more. The State can secure the
money at 3£ or 4 per cent., or, allowing for all
costs and deductions, suppose we say 5 per
cent. That amounts to £IOO,OOO per annum on
a loan of two millions, which is admittedly
more than would be required to start the concern. A
syndicate starting with the same capital, obtained at the
same rate, and content with an additional dividend of a
modest 5 per cent., would drain the country of £200,000
per annum, and in spite of trades-unionism and arbitra-
tion courts, would probably pay lower wages, work longer
hours, and do much to imperil political liberty and pro-
gress by its far-reaching influence and immense wealth.
The only practical question at stake in determining which
is the better, State or private enterprise iron-works, is
whether there is a reasonable hope of getting State works,
established in the near future? I think there is. Mr.
Kingston says he will accept the Labor party’s amend-
ment and go on with the Bonus Bill. If made law, it
means that all the States will be assisting by the bonuses
paid, in bearing the risks undertaken by one State in
establishing a national industry on truly national lines.


By payment of the bonus all the Australian people will
practically become partners in the enterprise, and, through
the Commonwealth Parliament, will exercise a vigilant
interest in its management. The fact that the Taxdodgers
Union, the Chamber of Commerce, all the daily papers
and fat men, individually and collectively, denounce the
Labor amendment of the Bonus Bill, establishes a strong
prima facie case in its favor. Why The Bulletin joins
with this motley crew of Mammon-worshippers when its
weight on the other side would so powerfully assist the
party of true Australian nationalism I am at a loss to
conceive.
• • * •
To which The Bulletin replies in as few words
as possible:—
(1) N.S.W. and all Australia want some new indus-
tries very badly indeed. In N.S.W. it may be fairly
estimated that 12 per cent, of the population is just now
supported by the expenditure of loan-money, mostly on
non-payable works. The loan business, which was
originally for remunerative works, has degenerated into
a relief system which makes N.S.W. one of the boss
pauper countries of the earth.
(2) The Labor Party has held the balance of power,
and had a great say in the direction of affairs in N.S.W.,
for 10 or 12 years. that time its chief idea
in the way of finding more employment for the people
seems to have been loans and public works —more of the
old relief business.
(3) During most of that time it has been the friend and
agent of the rich absentee importing firms who seek to
restrict Australian industry because it would reduce the
demand for foreign goods.
(4) During all that time it has done its full share to
make this country the slave of the foreign money-lender.
It has been fugleman for the rich money-lending sharks
as well as for the importing sharks. It has been the
agent of foreign sweaters and slave-drivers inasmuch as
it was largely responsible for throwing open N.S.W.’s
ports to the goods of the sweater and finding him a
market here.
(5) It passed an Old Age Pensions Bill certainly, which
was a good and noble action—and it didn’t provide r
penny to pay the pensions with. It was instrumental in
procuring better wages for the railway employees—and it
left any deficit that might arise therefrom to be added to
the floating debt in the good old way. It allowed the
Reid Government to shamefully fake the public accounts,
and kept the Reid Government in power. It allows the
See Government to annex trust-funds (the rents of the
unpaid-for properties in the resumed area) as revenue,
and keeps the See Government in power. It doesn’t even
protest against these swindles. It helped Reid to cut
down the already insufficient revenue, for the benefit, in
a great measure, of a wealthy class, and winked at all the
deficits that followed. In finance it has been a Tax-
dodgers party pure and simple. And, to put the matter
shortly, The Bulletin has pretty well lost faith in the
N.S.W. Labor party as an organisation that will, under
its present platform and leadership, exert itself to run
any public enterprise on a honest basis. It hasn’t origi-
nated any swindles that this paper is aware of, but,, for
giving its support to the swindles of both parties, it is
unique.
(6) Further, The Bulletin is losing any faith it ever had
in the N.S.W. Labor party as a constructive body. It has
been, to a considerable extent, in power for the last 10 or
12 years, and the State Bank and the State Insurance
Office are where they were ; and the State Land Bank is
too small and obscure to amount to anything, and Local
Government is apparently no nearer, and so are most
other things. The idea of the Labor party doing any-
thing serious in the way of establishing great State iron-
works looked too remote to be worth gambling on. And
the chance of such works paying their way unless the
Labor party uses its influence to have them run a great
deal more honestly than the Sydney land resumptions
have been, looks still more remote. The See-Owe’Sullivan
Government would probably treat the receipts from the
works as revenue and charge most of the expenses to
loans, and the Labor party would look on.
(7) There is no proposal whatever to give any company
a monopoly in the iron business. Canada supports seven
or eight large iron and steel works. The idea of Aus-
tralia, with two-thirds of Canada’s population, being
able to support only one is ridiculous. As for the theory
that the proposed company would make 20 or 30 per
cent, dividends that is mere theory, and very improbable
theory, too.
(8) The proposed works might easily save the payment
of £l,ooo,ooo—perhaps £2,000,000—a year for imports.
If tile co. took away even £250,000 a year in dividends
(25 per cent, on a capital of £1,000,000) it would still
save Australia something between £750*000 and
£1,750,000 in payment for imports. A Commonwealth
that is borrowing about £1,000,000 a month to
keep the pot boiling, and to pay for its imports,
and to find work for its unemployed, wants to make some
saving of this kind, and to do it very promptly. N.S.W. has
waited nearly a dozen years for the Labor party to do
something towards finding work for the people in self-
supporting industries, and has found it voting most of
the time with the crowd that keeps down N.S.W. indus-
tries in the interests of English and German and
American manufacturers and foreign importers—with the
Reid Cash Basis fraud, and the See resumed area rents
fraud—and with the party that is selling Australia to
foreign money-lenders. And that party carried its point
on behalf of subsidised State ironworks by joining votes
with the importing gang which wanted to kill the
measure altogether—which is against every new industry
because it will interfere with its business as agent for
outside sweaters.
(9) As to why the Labor party should hang on to the
motley gang of mammon-worshippers which sells this
country to Cohen, the London money-lender, and sup-
presses national industries for the benefit of M'Arthur
and Co., of London and elsewhere, The Bulletin is at a
loss to conceive. For years The Bulletin has urged that
an effort should be made to shift the foreign banks, in-
surance offices, and loan corporations that take large
profits out of Australia without supplying any new
industry or new employment; to get a graduated land-
tax and an absentee-tax that would remove the A. A.
Co. and other great foreign land syndicates that have
made 30 per cent, dividends out of Australia without
furnishing work or wages for the people; to get a
State bank and a State note issue ; to make a beginning
at lifting the blighting mortgage of the foreign Cohen off
our railways, telegraphs, ’phones, water works, sewage
works, roads, harbors—our everything, in fact ;to estab-
lish our own industries and cease supporting the blood-
stained Carnegie in the U.S., and the softgoods sweaters
of London —these foreign importers who make far larger
dividends out of this country than the manufacturing
“ monopolists” of the most-protected Australian State.
And perhaps it was the N.S.W. Labor party’s blank
apathy in practice, if not in theory, as regards every
great national idea of the kind that prevented this
paper taking its State ironworks seriously. The first idea
that presented itself was that the party had gone back to
its old importing idol, G. Reid, and was killing the pro-
posed Australian industry in a devious fashion to oblige
him and the rich foreign importers who are behind him.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
M.‘Billion the Freetrader—again.
Following in the footsteps of Dry-Dog Reid, that
bounding importer, William M'Millan, has
been delivering an oration on the fiscal question at
Euroa (Victoria). The report states that Willtam
the Importer “compared the relative progress of
N.S.W. under Freetrade and Victoria under
Protection,” which is just what the cheeky little
man probably would do. M‘Millan was once an
ardent Freetrade Treasurer in N.S.W. He
heavily protected newspapers, bricks, beer,
apples, timber and woodwork of almost every
kind, bacon, hams, tongues, biscuits, blasting
powder, butter, candied fruits, candles, cement,
cheese, cider, cigars, tobacco, confectionery, corn-
flour, galvanised iron of all kinds, wire, jams,
jellies, lead-piping, preserved milk, sugar,
molasses, paints, salt, wine, and a number of
other things. He preached Freetrade, and he
gave a little Protection to almost every industry
that was going. He denounced the Victorian
protective duties, and his own protective duties
were, in some cases, heavier than those then
existing in Victoria. When his own theories
and his practice were set against each other
he was a shameless political hypocrite ; and the
fair assumption is that, either he didn’t believe
what he said when he declared Protection to be
wholly bad, or else he believed Protection was
bad, and he and Bruce Smith and others were en-
gaged in a villainous conspiracy to ruin, or at
least injure, N.S.W. In one sense he certainly
had his own reward. If he had advocated Pro-
tection he would have had to tax his own soft-
goods, and damage his importing business for the
good of local industry—and he didn’t advocate
Protection. If he had practised real Freetrade
he would have brought about such a wreck, and
brough t down on himself such unanimous execra-
tion from the heavily-protected Freetrade papers,
that he would have gone out of office, and a
Government that would have taxed his softgoocls
would have come in—and so he didn’t practise
Freetrade. He took care to have Freetrade as re-
gards his own imported softgoods, but.that was the
exception rather than the rule. Politically he was
a slanderer as well as a shuffler, inasmuch as he
denounced, scoffed at, and repudiated the policy
by which he surreptitiously made his political
living. Politically, he was also a thief, for he
stole the credit of the prosperity which attached
to his protected industries, and pointed to it as a
proof of the advantages of Freetrade. No one
man or one Government could have worked this
monstrous imposture. But the Freetrade Sydney
papers, in consideration of heavy protection for
themselves, backed up the political fraud. Thesemi-
Protective tariff, to which M'MiLLAN and Bruce
Smith lent themselves, was in force in N.S.W.
for very many years, and some of its Pro-
tective items were in force until the Federal tariff
superseded them. Many N.S.W. industries owed
their existence to that tariff.. Therefore, anybody
who points to the prosperity of N.S.W. under
Freetrade is just a plain, ordinary liar, for N.S.W.
never had pure Freetrade. The method of dishing
their opponents pursued by the N.S.W. Freetrade
leaders may have been smart politics, but it
wasn’t honorable, and the person who does such
things isn’t calculated to ornament the church to
which he belongs—if he belongs to one. Next
time M ‘Milt.an addresses a Victorian audience on
the blessings of Freetrade that Victorian audience
should ask him why he didn’t take his own
blessing when he had the chance.
