A Hysterical Bishop.
Bishop Stretch of Newcastle (N.S.W.), has been
up in Northern Queensland, and has apparently
interviewed the class of people with whom bishops
and other large church dignitaries mostly asso-
ciate. He has come back full of fury at the
proposed abolition of Kanaka labor. For some
reason the Church is almost solid on the side of
cheap nigger labor. Probably its unconscious bias
nigger-wards arises from the fact that the brown
man is almost the only person nowadays to whom
the story of Jonah and the fish can be told in its
entirety without exciting laughter. He is almost
the only surviving receptacle for the complete
narrative of the ark and the deluge. He is—when
he finds a certain amount of “ religion ” —almost
the only person left who regards the preacher
man as a superior being, and who can be
patronised as my “ pore, humble brother.” At
all events, for some reason or other, the Church
has a decided cant in the brown man’s direction,
and is frantically illogical on his account. This,
for instance, is Bishop Stretch :
The nationalities of Cairns are decidedly mixed. The
Federal Government, with that rare courage which marks
truly great men, have fastened upon the most harmless
and (en parenth&se) the most unprotected for their special
fury. Chinese are there by thousands, and singularly
useful they are j Japanese also. But it is the Kanaka
that must go. These last seem singularly inoffensive.
They work hard at work no white man should be asked to
undertake (for it is deadly monotonous), are law-abiding,
quiet people, who spend their money where they earn it.
And many of them come under very distinct Christian
influence,' and live simple, pious lives. These men, many
of whom have wives and families who are really good-
living people, the enlightened Government insists upon
deporting to heathen islands where the chances are that
they will be murdered by their still-heathen countrymen.
A ‘more curious and cowardly proceeding it would be
hard to imagine. I trust that in some way Australia
may be spared the scandal of it. If not, a White Aus-
tralia will have to blush a pretty high-colored red
for very shame. It is curious that no plans for “ de-
porting ” Chinese or Japanese have yet been put forward.
It is not hard to see the reason. When we wish to play
the bully, we naturally choose the smallest boy. The
Kanaka only trashes and cuts cane, and loads it on to
the trucks. After that the work provides employment
for a large number of white men. Practically black labor
is little more than white man’s machinery, till something
directly mechanical can be invented and substituted.
Whether under these circumstances it is worth harassing
an important industry for the sake of a theory is surely
worth considering.
Bishop Stretch’s statement that he found Chinese
“by thousands” in a town which has less than
3000 persons suggests the usual unreliability of
the Church in matters of fact. His professed
inability to understand why the Kanakas were
fixed upon for expulsion great ignorance
of the subject, and a fixed intention not to study it
except from the sloppy standpoint of alleged Chris-
tian charity. The Kanaka is the most numerous
variety of colored objectionable—more numerous
than the Chinaman, and more than four times as
numerous as the Japanese —which is one objection.
He is about three times as criminal as the white
population, judged by the gaol statistics, which is
also a point against him. Also, he is much more
criminal than the Jap. Further, he came to Aus-
tralia under an agreement which provided for his
return to his native islands at the end of his term
of labor, and the Federal Government simply
proposes carrying out the agreement. It can’t
carry out any such agreement as regards the
Chinaman and the Japanese, for there is none.
The Kanaka doesn’t “only trash and cut cane
and load it on the trucks ” —not by any means.
That is an old yarn and a played-out Stretching of
the truth. But the most curious feature of the
story is that the Kanaka, when it is a question of
his remaining in Queensland to rear a half-caste
population and dilute the white race with a strain
of savagery, is “a singularly inoffensive indivi-
dual.” And almost in the next sentence it seems
that the Kanaka is a gory and howling savage, to
whom his own relatives dare not return for fear
of butchery. This is a large discrepancy. The
mere fact that the Queensland Kanaka may, or
may not, have heard, in a perfunctory way, about
Jonah, and learned to haunt the white man’s
public-house, doesn’t account for it. The Kanaka
on the islands has heard of Jonah also from the
missionary man. And there are many thousands
of Kanakas on the islands who have gone through
the Christianising process in the canefields and
public-houses of Queensland, and taken the mild
influence of the Gospel back with them. They
are all over the islands—these singularly inoffen-
sive Christians, and are now alleged to be scream-
ing on the beach, with an axe and a cook-
ing-pot, waiting for the return of their relatives.
Either Bishop Stretch wants Queensland to
take in a race of savages, whose influence must be
detrimental to the white inhabitants, or else it is
a case of sending the naturally mild, inoffensive
islander back to his mild, inoffensive friends
according to the terms of the original agreement.
Bishop Stretch can’t have both kinds of Kanaka.
Which Kanaka is he going to have ?
Tasmania’s Troubles.
A pressman on the spot (so far as there is any
spot) sums up the situation :
Tas. Treasurer Bird had not a very pleasant song to
trill in the local Assembly the other night. His deficiency
was £90,000 at the end of 1901, and is estimated at
£122,000 for 1902. Apart from the loss of customs revenue
through Federation, railway earnings have fallen off
£lO,OOO in the year as compared with 1900, producing
only o'Bs per cent, interest oncostof construction—and the
money to build the lines was borrowed at over 3£ per cent.
The West Coast lines, constructed at a cost of £350,000,
earned a profit of £13,000 in 1900, and only £2500 in 1901.
This is an eloquent testimony to the stupendous slump in
the silver-mining industry. There is also an indirect loss
through the partial squelching of “ Tattersall’s ” sweeps.
However, Treasurer Bird proposes to grasp the trouble
with both hands and square things up somehow. Expen-
diture is to be cut down by £33,000; the chief items
being the reduction of the Legislature to one House (or
two Houses sitting together), saving £4OOO a year ; the
lopping of the vote to charitable institutions by another
£4OOO ; compelling local bodies to either build their own
roads and bridges or go without, saving £13,000 ; and
“ miscellaneous ” expenditure to be reduced by £12,000.
Last year deadhead letters and telegrams cost a full
seventh (£9500) of the total postal revenue. Legitimate
departmental postages should not cost more than £5500.
So it appears that in a country where there is
no adequate payment of members the legislators
pay themselves by saving' stamps to the value of
£4OOO annually. This game, according to Mr. Bird,
has to stop. A new assessment of land is to yield £4OOO,
an increase of the land-tax on a graduated scale, £7OOO ;
railway charges are to be increased by £25,000 ; probate
duty, now collected on personalty only, is to apply to
realty and will produce £lO,OOO ; a new income-tax is to
gather in £50,000 ; a stamp duty, £5OOO. New revenue
from these sources is estimated at £134,000. With the
expenditure reduced as above by £33,000, the melancholy
Bird hopes to pull through without breaking up the
happy home to any great extent.
Bird’s proposals for future squaring of the Tas. ledger
are of a woefullv gimcrack nature. His income-tax is
really a house-tax, for, allegedly to obviate complaints
concerning the “inquisitorial” operations of the old
style of income-tax, the rental value of a man’s house is
to be taken as an index of his income. So if a citizen
with a £5OOO income likes to live in a four-roomed cottage
he will be practically exempt. How this will operate on
the building trade and allied occupations is sufficiently
obvious. The graduated land-tax is not likely to burst
up big estates or cheapen land to any extent worth men-
tioning, for nothing under £lO,OOO value is to be touched.
The scale runs: on estates valued at over £lO,OOO and
under £20,000, fd. in the £ ; over £20,000 and under
£50,000, fd. in the £; and over £50,000, Id. in the £.
As the bulk of Tas. unearned increment is in blocks of
land under £lO,OOO in value, and as the highest rate
proposed (Id. plus the present id. tax) is ridiculously
inadequate, and as the whole sum proposed to be raised
in this way only amounts to £7OOO, which a co. like the
Y.D.L. might pay all on its lonesome without a murmur,
Bird’s graduated land tax looks very small and weak.
The producer pays £25,000 a year in extra railway rates ;
the big landlord pays £7OOO ; and the absentee receives
no special attention whatever. Considering that the
landed proprietors have, within the last year or so, been
relieved of the old police rate to the extent of some
£26,000 per annum, they owe the Treasury a lot more
than the proposed £7OOO a year.
Nobody takes the Tas. Govt.’s proposal to abolish the
Upper House very seriously. The total amount of
the proposed saving by the adoption of the unicameral
system is only £4OOO a year, and as economy is the only
score on which any Treasurer would dare to advocate such
a thing in Tasmania, the chances are that the House of
Landlords will continue to flourish for an indefinite
period. To shift it there would need to be a strong
public agitation, for the Assembly is so built that it finds
it extremely handy to retain the House of Refuse as a
“ kind person to adopt undesirable infants.” The
Assembly will not, of its own motion, fight the “ other
place” to a finish, and the average Tas. citizen can
see no points in “proceshing” and agitating for
the removal of an ancient tradition when the
direct monetary saving will only amount to £4OOO a
year. If King O’Malley’s present crusade among the
Western miners extends to the cities and the apple
orchards and the hop-gardens, it may be possible to
organise an agitation for the abolition of the Upper House
on the ground that it is a useless excrescence and an
unmitigated nuisance. Failing such agitation, the House
of Adye Douglas looks like a permanency. At present it
is snorting so loudly at the proposal to incorporate it with
a reduced Lower House that the Govt, will, in all pro-
bability, be afraid to mention even that mild reform above
a whisper.
Medical Advertising 1 .
The British Medical Association (N.S.W. branch)
came to no definite decision at the meeting
called to protest against doctors giving inter-
views on matters of medicine to the “lay”
the non-medical) press, holding that
such interviews constitute “advertising” in
an indirect fashion. Those of the public who can
take an impartial and dispassionate view of the
matter will thoroughly sympathise with any
honest effort to restrict medical advertising. The
present rule against doctors advertising is
both for the benefit of the profession and for
the benefit of the public. Were it to be
abrogated no one would gain except the
newspaper proprietaries; the struggling young
doctor would find it harder than ever to get a
footing, competing against rich and well-estab-
lished men able to advertise freely ; and the
general public would ultimately have to pay the
bill for the advertising in the shape of increased
fees. Those are the practical considerations,
apart from the sentimental one of the loss of
dignity to the medical profession, and the very
substantial medical one that the better-educated
patients would have their confidence in doctors
who competed for the public’s guinea on hoardings
and in newspapers very seriously diminished (and
that confidence is the best drug in any ill-
ness). The efforts of the association to dis-
countenance advertising are good in their
aim ; whether their method is irreproachable is
another issue. The N.S.W. Branch B.M. A. has,
rightly or wrongly, got the reputation of
cliqueism and favoritism, of persecuting some
doctors with frivolous charges and of letting other
offenders do almost as they please. It hardly has
the full confidence of either the profession or the
public; and its actions, however good their object,
are sometimes met with antagonism for that
reason. In the matter of what may be called
“ indirect advertising ” much must necessarily be
left to individual good taste. At times of great
public interest in a medical point it seems too
stringent a rule to forbid doctors of high repute to
give information to the lay press. There was one
particular press incident in connection with the
King’s late illness which was altogether indefen-
sible; if the B.M. A. were to confine its censure to
that it would have the general support of the
public.
Industrial Arbitration in N.S.W.
There have been during the past week two
important industrial developments in N.S.W.
arising out of the lately-passed Industrial Arbitra-
tion Act. A deputation from the Tramway
Employees’ Union waited upon the Railway Com-
missioners to ask for the official “ recognition ” of
their union as a preliminary to the discussion of
grievances. This the Commissioners refused, and,
the point being insisted upon by the men, a
serious position threatens. It seems to be an
undignified quarrel over a word on the part of the
Railway Commissioners. Under the Arbi-
tration Act the tramway employees can
register as an industrial union, and thereby gain
a legal standing which no refusal of recogni-
tion by the Railway Commissioners can affect.
The Commissioners may say that the union doesn’t
exist; they may also say that Australia
doesn’t exist. Neither assertion will have any
material effect. If. then, the Union has any
grievance with the Commissioners it can ask
redress and, failing to get it, can bring the matter
before the Arbitration Court. What the Unions
as yet fail to recognise in full is that the Arbitra-
tion Act relieves them of all necessity of threaten-
ing unpleasantness or of “demanding recog-
nition”; they can simply formulate their require-
ments, confer about them if the other side is
agreeable, ;and, in case of final dispute, send the
matter to a Court of Justice, just as a contention
about the ownership of a piece of land or the
rights and wrongs of an alleged slander case can be
referred to judicial arbitrament. Another develop-
ment in connection with the Act was a labor depu-
tation to the Premier and the Attorney-General to
complain of the recent decision in the A.W.U. case,
and to ask for an amendment of the Arbitration
Act —(1) to prevent lawyers taking part in its
proceedings; (2) to prevent the multiplicity of
unions in any one calling, and to ensure that each
union should be a “good faith” one. The first
request opens up a very big question ; there is no
more reason, and no less, why lawyers should be
barred out of the Arbitration Court than out of
any other. Possibly, one day they will be barred
out of all Courts. As to the second request, the
Act makes ample provision in the direction indi-
cated : the A.W.U. failed in its application to
squash a rival union only because certain A.W.U.
rules made it impossible that a Court of Justice
should force all shearers and shed-hands into its
ranks. Labor advocates would be well-advised if
they waited a little while before rushing to com-
plain of the Arbitration Act. Mr. J ustice Cohen
and his assistants seem to be administering it
with an admirable impartiality, and, so adminis-
tered, the Act is certainly wide enough and well-
considered enough to confer very great benefits on
the community, and on the workers particularly.

“Pace Pry” on a white-labor experiment in
N.Q.: —
The Mosman sugar district is a very large area of
country north of Townsville. There are thousands of
acres of the finest sugar lands in Australia awaiting
settlement. Mr. Woods, M.P. for Woothakata, a practical
man and a believer in White Australia, took up the
challenge of the local farmers before the last election ana
guaranteed that he would get a gang of reliable white
men to take the place of colored labor to cut the cane
this season, and afterwards become settlers on the un-
occupied sugar lands of the Mosman. Woods, M.P.,
won his election, and kept his word. He produced the
men, and white men are doing the work previously done
by black labor. They are doing it successfully and earn-
ing good wages. The “trashing”—“the awful, soul-
killing work that would be too much for white men, in
Pastor Bevan’s opinion—is being done by white labor.
And white labor is cane-cutting, and loading cane-
trucks, and laying portable tram-lines. And this
particular white labor, according to Woods, M.f.,
reckons it the softest job it ever struck. Smith s
—(lmmortal Smith’s)— gang consists of 22 field men
and a cook. When they started they elected a leader
to have sole control of the job, and to be the medium of
communication between the Mosman Mill Co. and the
gang. And the manager of the Mosman Mill Co. seems
anxious that no obstacle should be thrown in the way of
the success of the experiment. The men work eight
hours a dav, six days a week, with the result that sugar
farmers are so satisfied that they are employing white
men to do the stripping in the fields. The men receive
4s 6d. per ton for cutting and loading cane and laying
portable tram lines, and 15s. per acre for trashing cane.
At these rates the men average £2 and £3 per week

The Bulletin notes with satisfaction that a jury
in Armidale (inquest on Trooper Edgar, acci-
dentally shot dead) has had the courage to with-
stand bounce from a coroner. Daily paper
report: —
The foreman announced that the jury wished to inspect
the ground. The Coroner declined to adjourn the Court.
The foreman insisted, and said the verdict of the jury
might be materially affected by an inspection of the
ground at the present stage of the inquiry. The Coroner
said he would not waste the time of the Court, and was
proceeding to examine another witness when the foreman
again renewed his application, and said the jury intended
to report the matter to the Department of Justice. He
considered that the jury had been insulted by the Coroner.
The Coroner, with great warmth, said the foreman could
do as he pleased. He knew his duty, and the jury would
leave the Court at their peril. He considered he had been
subjected to insult. The case was then proceeded with.
After another witness had been examined, Senior-
Sergeant Sharp renewed the application on behalf of the
jury for a visit fto the ground, whereupon the Coroner
consented to adjourn the Court till Wednesday.
When the same attitude is taken up by all juries,
each time Magistrate, District Court Judge, or
Supreme Court *Tudge attempts “ bounce, 5 there
will be fewer scandals in the administration of
justice. If, in the Mount Rennie case, a juryman
had had the courage to firmly protest against
“ all-night sittings” of the Court; if, in scores of
other cases which might be cited, jurymen had
recollected that their privileges and dignities m
Court are equal to those of the presiding Judge,
many a blood stain would have been spared to
our history.

“ Arthur Glint ” writes from Victoria: —
The Marnoo Estate, near Horsham, comprising 9000
acres, has been offered for sale to Vic. Government, and
now that farmers are leaving the district to become
tenants of Southern landlords, it will probably be
bought by said Govt, as a site suitable for a rabbit-
warren. The land monopolist knows his business.
Marnoo Estate' is about in the same position as the
pelican in the wilderness; when there are signs of
a general exodus of the people to more favored parts, the
owner offers his no longer profitable monopoly to Govt.
Transactions of this sort are bringing Vic. Govt, into bad
repute as a land-buyer, for these deals, instead of improv-
ing the condition of the people, only become burdens,
like the national drunks. The fact that there is no com-
pulsory-resumption clause in Vic. Land Act makes collu-
sion between a prospective seller and the Govt, agents
possible. As things stand, Govt, is apt to lie
victimised or the taxpayers robbed. The purchases
undertaken now can only be with the consent of
owners, and we well know what that means. In fact,
without a compulsory resumption clause in the
Land Act, Govt, is driven to buy only those estates that
owners want to sell, probably because of their partial
worthlessness ; and estates which the people want Govt,
to buy, because of their fertility and general productive-
ness, the Govt, cannot buy, for the very good reason that
owners won’t sell. Hence the Northern landlord is able
to hand his bad asset over to the State, to become the
bad asset of the people ; also, the Southern landlord is
able to gather the fruits of monopoly unmolested. If
legislators cannot be found who have no respect for the
traditions of the local squattocracy, Victoria will soon be
in a bad way. Therefore, Victorian democrats should
fight tooth-and-nail for the insertion in the Land Act of
a compulsory resumption clause, even if, in the struggle
to obtain that result, the Leg. Council has to go.

A most worthy clause in the Federal Electoral
Bill is that which restricts the election expenses
of Representatives to £lOO and Senators to £250.
A poor man can’t be expected to spend more than
that, and a rich man shouldn’t be allowed to.
The country may want to choose its politicians on
what it believes to be their merits, but it is always
liable to have its judgment unconsciously biassed
by cash expenditure,<sor to be led astray by thi
panegyrics of newspapers bought up by the rich
candidate, and disinterested admirers hired by tin
rich candidate; and the only way to give a fail
deal is by restriction all round. By-and-bye, ii
will be necessary to go further, and make it un-

Truman-Leumane Australian-made opera
‘‘ Mathis ” was presented, for first time, last
Saturday,. to an audience —principally musicians
and musician-lovers—at Sydney Criterion Theatre.
Leumane, who held up the heavy name-part on
the stage in melodious, strong-man style, is respon-
sible for the libretto, a piece of work which has
scholarly and literary attributes. “Mathis” is
founded upon Erckmann-Chatrian’s romance, “The
Polish Jew”—same story that made the corner-
stone and foundation for “The Bells,” in which
Irving played so famously as Matthias. An inn-
keeper, miller and limebumer has promised his
daughter Annette, who is betrothed to a young
soldier, the liberal dowry of 30,000 francs. The
bridal day is at hand but the dowry isn’t. The
till is empty and Matthias distractedly wonders
how he shall fill it. Sleighbells are heard across
the snow and enter atravelling diamond merchant,
a Polish Jew. He leaves his belt filled with jewels
on the table while he attends to his horse. Of
this means to do ill deeds Innkeeper Mathis takes
full advantage, drugs the Jew’s wine, burns his
body in the lime-kiln, and later settles the price
of the stolen jewels on his Annette. Rest of the
story (as told in the opera) opens a year after,
and Truman, the composer, has set forth in
music the misery a conscience may bring
its possessor, for Mathis neglected to cremate
his conscience along with the Jew’s body. His
visions, remorse, fear of hell and gradual mental
decay are also set forth in solo, choral and or-
chestral music. Grand Opera writing is an am-
bitious undertaking, and if Mr. Truman hasn’t
quite succeeded in hitching his score to a star he
—well, he can console himself that neither has
any other composer—those who did were demigods
in music. The ruby eye of Mathis is the
Intermezzo which follows the first act. In it is
expressed the delight of the betrothed pair, as
well as prevision of the fate of the conscience-
stricken man. The murder is re-enacted for the
benefit of the audience in a dream of Mathis’s.
He dies immediately after awakening from
the horror, surrounded by the wedding-guests,
whose joyous bridal chorus turns to a funereal
hymn extolling sleep and rest. Audience mostly
agreed with Slapoffski and Leon Caron’s senti-
ments as printed on the programmes, namely, that
“ Mathis ” was musically and dramatically excel-
lent and. had “fire.” Miss Annie Perry, who ap-
peared in the prima-donna rdle, gave a capital
account of herself in song, and short skirts, as the
bride with the stolen dowry an! the small feet.
Miss Bertha Paning was a too-young mother
who sings a sweet, sad song as she spins at a
wheel. Her voice, though, has sounded better
on many a concert platform. Mr. Rainford, as the
Polish Jew, intoned his small part and looked
like a not very wild wild-beast. Mr. H. Huxham,
with a big black (stage) mo. and a small, light
tenor voice, was Christian, the soldier-lover.
Baritone Edward Farley, as Heinrich, completed
the caste. The mounting was everything that the
best innkeeping society round the blue Alsatian
mountains might desire.

Sheridan (John F.) takes possession of the
Criterion next Saturday, with a new musical
comedy, “The Lady Slavey.”
&* * *
According to the Brough programme,
“Niobe” is to be revived at Sydney Royal on
Wednesday, Thursday, and Friday of this week.
The tearful Greek lady who was recalled to life
on the London premises of Peter Amos Dunn,
through having electric shocks applied to her
ancient Greek feet, has always been an immense
favorite, and her farewell ought to be a function
of great size and tonnage. After that, a new
Pinero comedy, “Iris,” follows on Saturday;
and the fact that Pinero is always worth listen-
ing to is too well-known to require repetition.
Under these arrangements, “When We Were
Twenty-one” departs before Sydney had made
up its mind whether to like it or not. Per-
haps, if it had remained a while longer,
so as to have time to soak in, it might have been a
favorite, but it didn’t get the time. The most
frequent question asked about it is how Brough
proposed to become notorious along with the red
lady with the bad temper. When Brough and
his friends discovered that their adopted Lout was
going to marry that highly-purchasable person
Brough decided to buy her himself. .He offered
her £lOOO to become such a public scandal with
him that the Lout must needs hear of it and be
too shocked to marry her. But when he handed
over the money he made it a condition that he
and the red lady were never to meet after
that moment, and how he was going to be-
come a public scandal and a shock with a
woman who was never seen along with him
passes understanding. There are women whose
characters are so white that they can be
soiled by post-card, or breathed upon through the
telephone, but the Firefly’s reputation had seen
hard service up and down Europe, and it would be
necessary to hug her in broad daylight in front of
the Bank of England to work up a really service-
able scandal. The cry of an ordinary-sized scandal
wouldn’t be heard above the roar of her past
performances. This discrepancy has been com-
mented on in nearly every hotel in Sydney ; and it,
combined with the unpleasant signs of temper
shown by the allegedly nice girl, and the Lout’s
resolve to redeem his character by going to the
war and killing somebody, were serious drawbacks.
The last item was especially behind-hand and
antiquated. The war is over now, and the Jingo
joss is dead. An up-to-date Lout would have
declared his fixed determination to become a ping-
pong champion, and thus retrieve his wasted
youth.
#* # *
The “Great Ruby ” discontinues its varied
career at Sydney Lyceum this week. It is to be
succeeded by “With Flying Colors” —said to
be something about the British navy. The
chance to insert a few remarks to the
effect that Australia should also have a navy,
instead of paying an annual contribution for
the loan of a few Ark-bottomed samples of
the British fleet, over which samples it has no
control whatever, will doubtless be lost. The
British tar is a noble soul, and we should admire
him tremendously, but if we try to imitate him it
is a suspicious circumstance which ought to be put
down.
#* * #
“A Little Ray of Sunshine,” at Sydney
Palace, has improved considerably since the first
night as regards the playing, the actors having
“ settled down ” to their parts. Bright acting
by Miss Eva Macdonald, as a little girl,
stands out now among the feminine charac-
ters. The play, however, still depends abso-
lutely on Mr. Thornton’s pourtrayal of Lord
Markham, the eccentric old individual whose
schemes are much aided by his luck in falling
among a nest of very clumsy fibbers, headed by
Mr. Carlton, “of the Diplomatic Service.” If
British diplomacy has to depend on such poor
rolled-gold brummagem liars as Mr. Carlton, its
failure when it tries to diplomatise against the
patient pig-tailed Chinaman is quite explainable.
The Thornton season closes on Aug. 8. After
that, Williamson’s crowd is due with “ San Toy.”
** * *
Sydney Tivoli rejoices just now in a very
strong bill. Cinquevalli, with his nimble muscles
and his almost as nimble wits, amuses as much as
he astonishes, and thus fulfills all the require-
ments of a star turn. Tom Wootwell seems to
have an inexhaustible budget of good songs ; one
of the latest, sung in “Pecksniff” character, with
its catchy refrain beginning “Oh, my bretherin,
join together in,” threatens to be whistled every-
where. Mark Anthony is another entirely amus-
ing person, and Roberti (a basso with a most
remarkable phiz), Sullivan (tenor), Miss Jennie
Opie, and Miss Ray Jones all contribute good
turns.
** * *
“The Circus Girl,” at Her Majesty’s
(Melb.), shows a commendable desire to profit
by experience, and work upwards in the estima-
tion of a kindly-disposed public. Scenes that
were heaviest on first night have been relieved
of unnecessary ballast ; the orchestra puts more
color and sparkle into the music ; the performers,
here and there, are making slight improvements
in their character-sketches. Even the Terrible
Turk, whose mountainous amateurishness was
pathetic when he first took the floor, begins to
look less unhappy. If the stage-manager could
but teach ex-Constable Kelly to appear Terrible
at the outset and intoxicated later on—if he were
sufficiently advanced in histrionics to fall with a
convincing thud when the audience was
looking—if he _ could only seem to express
the disastrous influence of 8 quarts of circus
champagne, Kelly Pasha would be a gigantic
success. The human items in Signor Drivelli’s
Circus awaken slumbering memories of “The
Grasshopper,” a drama that Jennie Lee brought
to Australia long ago. Hugh Ward introduces
his pale meditative clown to notice in much the
same way that the late Harwood used to trot out
Dismal Jimmy, or the Man with the Iron Jaw.
“The Circus Girl” is at least a notable thing as
regards make-up. Ward is a picture of an old-
fashioned showman, and if Pat Bathurst, as the
ringmaster, were to walk into Fitzgerald’s arena
and shake his whip the horses would unanimously
rise upon their hind legs and salute the splendid
vision. Lauri, on the other hand, is not at all a
striking suggestion of an American bar-tender.
He wants briskness. For no ostensible reason he
paints his face as though he had a cold in the
head. In fact, he tries to get laughs for Lauri
rather than smiles for the character he plays, and
the result is disappointing. Some pleasant allu-
sions to the ladies are held over for a week, but
meanwhile the present scribe wishes that Carrie
Moore would not act quite so effusively. Carrie
has all sorts of charms, except the charm of self-
restraint.
** * *
This is the last week of the mangle and
other foolishness at Melbourne Royal. On Saturday
a new and original, likewise a novel and realistic
drama, entitled “ The Worst Woman in London,”
will take the place of the present reckless outrage.
Miss Duggan is to reappear, after a few weeks
rest, in the libellous character of the Worst
Woman.

Certain events which had been announced
for last Saturday were postponed for different
reasons. Dolores the Delightful was to sing at
the Town Hall, and a lot of people came along to
buy the pleasure of listening to that graoious
warbler, but at the last moment mademoiselle
found herself unable to appear. Her throat was
k? suffering from a sudden attack
of Melbourne climate. At time of writing it is
hoped that Mdlle. Dolores will be able to accom-
plish her four farewell recitals during the week;
also it is known for certain that she will not take
any liberties with her valuable throat. Therefore
this journal leaves the Melbourne newspaper
advts. to state whether Dolores will appear to-
night (Thursday) and again on Saturday, and
whether the latter night will be the farewell
occasion or not.

Henry Lees production of “Cyrano de
Bergerac,” at the Bijou, was another matter-for
postponement. The play is a large, spectacular
show which must be well done if it is to go, and
as the rehearsals last week seemed short of work
-in a sporting way of speaking—“ Cyrano ” was
fixed for a week later. On Saturday (2nd), Henry
Lee will appear as the hero of 1000 duels and the
unfortunate possessor of a Nose.

** # *
At the Opera House the attraction of
Lydia Yeamans-Titus and the rest of the pro-
gramme is “overwhelming,” especially on Satur-
day nights. Ada Delroy and James Bell are
recent arrivals, whilst the Jacksons, the Harveys,
the Wiltons, and the eminent professor of the
saxophone retain their positions. Also Tom
Nawn is appearing in a new sketch, “The Poli-
tician.” Nawn, in a state of politics and alcohol,
is much the same Nawn as usual, but his elec-
tioneering tactics include some new ideas. He is
the first man who ever thought of forming a
Hebrew Hod-Carriers’ Association.
** * *
The second Saturday of Dan Barry and
co. at the Gaiety found a larger and better audi-
ence than before. Better because there were more
girls wearing nice hats and dreamy eyes, from
which it would appear thattheirthrifty Adolphuses
had discovered the financial advantages of dealing
at Dan’s Dramatic Supply Stores. The nice hats
and their young men were not critical. “A Ride
for Life ” was accepted with signs of gratitude.
Dan himself, in a white umbrella and a badly-
fitting wig, was an elderly person flitting about
amidst a whirl of red-shirted bushrangers and
other eruptions. Rege Rede was a welcome
gleam as the providential Australian lad who
talks about the Melbourne Cup and circumvents
the villain. So also was the prettiness of Miss
Lila Byford ; but Gladys Grant was not up to
sample as per “A Gilded Sin,” not having the
power to express violent mental agony for three
consecutive hours. Dan’s chances of survival at
cheap rates seem to be improving.
** * *
The comedy advertised as “The Wrong
Mrs. Wright ” and which duly broke out at the
Queen’s Theatre last Saturday, was nothing more
nor less than “Jane.” And the Queen’s Theatre,
when one turned into it, turned out to be the neat
little hall at the back of the busy pub. where
“The Red Stockings” co. high-kicked in days of
yore. Two of the Cosgrove brethren figured
capably in the forefront of the show that wasn’t
“The Wrong Mrs. Wright.”
* * * *
Pretty Ada Willoughby appeared as the
wrongly-used Jane, and her somewhat frisky per-
formance called for no disparaging remarks. The
charges for admission to witness “The Trans-
formation of Jane” were 35., 2s. and Is., but the
supply of patrons at even the humble shilling was
greatly exceeded by the demand.
** # #
The people—and they were numerous—
who said that Dani was a potent attraction to the
Amy Castles concerts, because he was the most
loudly-applauded performer of the crowd, are now
lying low and saying nothing. Dani has proved
to be a quite ineffectual “star” on his own
account, whereas the Amy Castles co., minus Una
Bourne and the plump Italian tenor (Neal M‘Kay
being substituted for liim) continues to do boom
business at 3,2, and 1 prices. From Tasmania the
co. returned to Victoria —Geelong and Ballarat —
thence to N.S.W., with Queensland in view. The
tour is expected to close in a fortnight from now—
but why should it? There can be no longer any
pretence of a necessity for the “Princess of Song”
to return to London or Paris within a certain
time, seeing that the original “ time limit ” for the
tour has already been exceeded by many weeks.
And, as for the nonsense talked about the danger
of over-taxing her voice, it is a fact that Miss
Castles’ voice has thriven on the moderate
amount of work demanded of it. At Ballarat the
prima-donna was a comparatively greater success
than she had been in Melbourne (judging success
by the all-round heartiness of the applause), and
those who had heard her before were the warmest
admirers. Another improved member of the co.
is Needham, the baritone. Needham sings fetch-
ingly,' and is calculated to thrill the same girls
with less effort than Dani lavished on his task.
The slim young man joins one of the William-
sonian opera co.’s at the conclusion of the Castles
concert campaign.
** * *
The Hawfcrey Co. has continued to occupy
Adelaide Royal, relying chiefly on the furniture-
moving gentleman from Mars. Winifred Austin,
of Sydney, who has much improved, now takes
Miss Ray’s part, and Adelaide’s own Heggie is
the Messenger. Holloway is announced to follow,
with something about Revenge. Manager Pol-
lock is seriously ill again. The Tivoli has got
strongly back to work, with a small but starlike
co. The Sidney Drews score heavily in a kind of
mixture of “The Taming of the Shrew ” with the
last act of “ Iris,” wherein the husband (always in
a gentlemanly way) wrecks a whole apartment in
search for a collar-stud that isn’t lost. Harris and
Walters are another American pair, of the
“ sketch ” order; Harcourt is a conjuror beyond
thecommon; and “Prof.” Macann shows a reason-
able claim to call himself the concertina “ king.”
And there isn’t a comic song in the programme !
The Wild Cat Column.
Extract from S.M. Herald report of Importer
M'Millan’s speech at Euroa (Vic.): “Out of 112 millions
of wealth actually produced per annum in Australia
manufactures represent 28 millions, and of the latter nine
millions were produced in New South Wales without one
farthing of Protection. But you may reasonably say that
I cannot expect to arouse much enthusiasm among the
people of Victoria if I cannot prove that this Freetrade
policy of ours, this ideal tariff at which we aim, is just as
good for Victoria as for the other States. If I could not
prove this I would not be here to-night. Victoria in 1899
produced or manufactured wealth amounting to about
31 millions. Of this 10 millions were produced by manji-
faoturers. But New South Wales, without any duties,
produced 9 millions through her manufactures. Unless
the people of Victoria are inferior to those of New South
Wales—a most absurd idea with people of a common race
—then out of the 10 millions produced by Victoria she
could have manufactured 9 millions without any duty
whatever. What, then, did she pay for the luxury of the
extra million ? Only one million in customs, from which
New South Wales was free.”
So M'Millan practically assures the Victorians
that, if they had followed his advice, and given up Pro-
tection, they would only have destroyed one-tenth of
their manufactures (equal to throwing 6600 people out
of work), and only sacrificed £1,000,000 of revenue, which
would have made the Treasury insolvent. And he calls
that an inducement to Freetrade ! Of course every person
of education (if M'Millan believes his own yarn, he is a
person without education) knows that N.S.W. didn’t
achieve even the results it did “ without one farthing of
Protection.” Even under Keid’s Dry-Dog,tariff, which was
the nearest it ever got to Freetrade, it heavily protected
wine, beer, sugar, newspapers, tobacco, cigars, molasses,r
jams, jellies, biscuits, confectionery, &c. And Victoria neves
collected £1,000,006 a year more than N.S.W. in customl
duties—in 1900 it collected £659,000 more. But al
the mendacity attaohed to his yarn doesn’t in any way
diminish the wild humor of M'Millan’s assurance that the
blessings of Freetrade would leave over 6000 Victorians
unemployed, and make the State bankrupt by depriving
it of £1,000,000 revenue.

These are prosperous-looking’ figures, and if the Ballarat
Trustees continues to progress at this rate it will yet he
one of the strongest institutions of its kind in Australasia.
The chief essentials arblhat it should adhere to its policy
of carrying about half its profits to reserve (even on this
basis it pays 10 per cent, dividends, which should be large
enough to satisfy the most rapacious shareholder), and
that it should invest its funds, as hitherto, mostly in good
Government securities. At present its principal invest-
ments are in Victorian State securities, and at a time
when a good many Australian State securities begin to
look rather ghastly, those of Victoria, as the least
embarrassed of the States, are probably about the best.
By way of a side issue, the fact that the Ballarat Banking
Co. and the Ballarat Trustees, Executors and Agency Co.
show such a material improvement this half-year, while
so many Melbourne and Sydney institutions do the reverse,
seems to suggest that the big cities are not bad places to
be out of, in a business sense, just at present.

After a long struggle the Equity Trustees, Executors and
Agency Co. appears to be at last building up a substantial
business. It would have been in a much more solid
position if it had not increased its dividend to 7£ per
cent, so soon, and, in view of the fluctuating character of
the trustee business, it remains to be seen if it hasn’t
raised it to 8 per cent, rather soon. Still, if the co.
doesn’t go beyond 8 per cent., which is a figure that
should satisfy anybody, and if the earnings remain at the
average rate of the last two years, progress will be
reasonably rapid. The investments are chiefly in State
and municipal securities and in the co.’s own premises,
which are unencumbered ; and if the risky mortgage
business is avoided (which it has been, to a great extent,
so far), things should go well. One fact worth noting is
that ±4501 of the assets consists of “ commissions, &c.,”
due to the co., but uncollected. This represents nearly
a whole year’s income, and suggests that the co. credits
itself with commission on an estate as soon as it comes
into its hands. A much wiser process would be to take
credit for commission only when actually collected. In
the boom days some trustee companies gaily took credit
for, and to a great extent distributed as dividends, com-
mission on estates supposed to be worth £lOO,OOO, which
shrunk to £50,000 or less before realisation. And, natur-
ally, the commission shrank likewise.

stock can be “ relied upon for some years ” with a pro-

world this would be for a lot of us if we could find “ re-

value.

If the Queensland Trustees can keep up the average rate
of progress of the last 18 months its prospects are good.
Its reserves are now growing on a respectable scale, and
it seems to have got over its former unwise habit of pay-
ing away practically everything in dividends, while the
urgent need to strengthen the institution was neglected.
Probably the recent boom arose partly out of the Tyson
estate —nearly every Queensland institution made a little
out of the winding up of James Tyson—and in that case
it may not be fully maintained. But a good general busi-
ness is being got together, and providing the co. doesn’t
give way fo exorbitant hunger after dividends, and the
management is reasonably careful, its future should be
prosperous.

Very extensive alterations and improvements
have just been completed at the engineering works of
Mephen Ferguson, Melbourne. The principal item of
interest is a new Electric Welding plant, which is now in
use welding the steel-plates required in the manufacture
of the patent spiral riveted pipes which are in such large
demand for water-supply, mining and other purposes.
The new method of welding is giving very satisfactory
results, and is well worth seeing.*

The antidote to cyanide poisoning, as supplied
by Ernest de Beer, 439 Bourke-street, Melbourne, has
been approved by the Victorian Mines Department as
being the only real antidote known. All who use
cyanide should keep one or two of these on hand. The
price is 10s. each.*

Wallaroo and Moonta meeting, on Monday,
lasted all day. The reform party were in a minority of
three bn the vote of shareholders, but, on number of
shares, they were beaten by two to one, the directors and
their friends having control of large parcels of shares held
in trust.

The Castlemaine Brewery Co., like many other institu-
tions, would do better if it traded to a greater extent on
its own capital, and less on borrowed money. It has a
paid-up capital of £125,000, otherwise it trades on £143,995
of borrowed money (5 per cent, debentures, £100,000;
and mortgages, £13,995) and an Overdraft of about £2600.
Interest on these liabilities swallows up about half the
profits, which, by reason of the cut-throat competition
which has raged for years among Melbourne beer-makers,
are not large at present. A Beer Trust is what Melbourne
brewers want. Melbourne wholesale beer prices are now
so very much below Sydney level, that the brewing busi-
ness is only a shadow of its old self.

* * • *
A Broken Hill M.P., talking wildly in the
Assembly on a labor-suspension matter, the other night,
said that the Pinnacles should be employing 3000 men.
The ore, it was said, was richer than that in the big
Broken Hill mines. No doubt it is rich, but it is even
inore refractory than the B.H. stuff, pyrrhotine being
present as well as sulphur and zinc. Attempt after
attempt to treat it has failed ; everybody who has tried
to work it has dropped money. Mines in the district,
containing equally good ore, though in smaller bodies,
have been open for years. Apropos labor-covenants,
much nonsense is talked. M’sL. A., in the debate referred
to, wanted to know what would happen at Broken Hill
if the Minister suspended the covenants of those leases ?
As a matter of fact, it would make very little difference ;
if the co’s kept on only the dozen or so men prescribed
in the bond and sacked the rest, Broken Hill would feel
just about as good over it as a man who has been
toasted all but his little finger.
• • * *
In the row over the reconstruction of the B.H.
South all the sympathy is with the holders of contribut-
ing shares. But that isn’t likely to save them. They
have struck the two ends of a piece of bad luck at once.
First, they are the minority ; second, the majority are
led by an uncommonly greedy crowd. Not the directors,
or anyway not all of them, but the greater than the
directors who pull the strings. The position may be
sized-up like this: About eight years ago the South,
having used up its carbonates in a silly attempt to be its
own smelter, faced a cold world with nothing but sulphides
to sell, and nobody would then take sulphides at a gift.
Its shares got down to a shilling or so, and there was talk
of winding up ; but that was saved by a reconstruction—
as a last hope a few believers put their half-crowns into it,
taking contributors’ shares. If they had had half the
greed and half the knack of driving a bargain that their
present opponents have, they would have stuck out for
preference shares and got them. Anyhow they took con-
tributors, and by the time their shares were 4s. 6d. paid
up, the next-to-hopeless mine, which had declared only two
or three divs. out of its carbonates, was actually paying
divs. out of its sulphides, and the promoters’ shares had
sailed up from a nominal value to past a pound. Since then
divs. have been regular and the value has gone up more.
Some people expected that the grateful promoters would
Want to distribute medals or something among the con-
tributors who had thus saved the situation ; but they
didn’t know these promoters who, instead, decided that
it would be a good idea to make the contributors pay for
new machinery while they (the promoters) alone took the
divs. And they did—for 18 months or so along with
every div. notice has gone to the contributor a call
notice. But that process didn’t suck the contributor fast
enough ; and, besides, there would come a time when he
would not be iiable to be sucked any more. The new scheme
is to overcomethatglaring defect. The managing director,
speaking for the directors, proposes that the contributors
(whose shares are now paid up to Bs. 6d.) should pay
is. 6d. a share and then get a 10s. share in a new co.,
while the promoters (£1 shares nominally) shall take two
10s. shares. Another 78,000 odd shares are to be
divided pro rata, not among the contributing shareholders
alone, but among all shareholders. The result of this
juggle will be that, instead of having rather more than
one-third the shares and being entitled to more than one-
third of the divs., the contributors will get less than one-
fourth of the shares, and be entitled to less than one-fourth
of the profits, even after they have paid the eighteen-
penny fine. And the co , it is to he remembered, is
not hard-up ; on the contrary, a div.-notice was issued
two days after this unspeakably greedy and cheeky circu-
lar was sent out with a snuffle to the shareholders. The
only good thing about the whole business is the purpose
for which the money (£45,000) is to be used—to sink a
new shaft and to increase the output from 3000 to 5000
tons a week. Even those ends, though, don’t justify this
bail-up. But do the directors really mean it ? Is it
bluff ? Are they waiting for contributors to say, “ Why,
we would rather pay up what is unpaid,” to get in with,
“ Well, pay up ! ”
The moral of the B.H. South “ situation ” is—
Never assume that any contributing shares are not
loaded. London Stock Exchange would not quote even
the B. 11. Prop, stock until a small liability on itscontribs.
was wiped off—which was done in ’B9 by making a call
and declaring a div. at the same time.
* * * •
B.H. Prop, to the end of its half-year (May 31)
had paid £7,408,000 in divs. and £BB,OOO in bonuses, apart
from cash and share distributions on the flotations of
“ pups.” This in 17 years, though it is less than 16 since
the first div. was paid. In the half-year when first it got
its furnaces in blast its ore was worth £l5 2s. sd.
a ton—at least it got that much out of the ore. Costs
were then £6 15s. 4d., and the profit was £8 7s. Id.
(Silver, fine, was 4s. 1J(1., and lead £l3 16s. 4d. then.)
Last half-year costs were £ll9s. l jd., and profit 3s. ljd.
The reduction of costs is in spite of the fact that the ore
cannot be now dumped into the smelter as it is broken,
but must be crushed, concentrated (for the removal of
zinc and gangue), roasted (for the removal of sulphur),
and briquetted (so that the fine concentrated stuff will
not cake in the furnace) before it gets to the smelter ;
and even then it is not a good smelting material. In silver
the co.’s most prolific year wa5’94 —14,054,3g30z., which
was between one-llth and one-12th of the world’s output
that year. Last 12 months’ was 5,238,1150 z., which is about
l-33rd. In lead the output was never before so great
as n0w —62,746 tons in the last 12 months, which is
between one-13th and oue-I4th of the world’s. A few other
figures for the past six months Products realised,
£652,005 (this includes bullion, &c., on hand); ore
treated, 308,838 tons(in ’94 it got up to 325,949 tons, which
is a six-months’ record) ; wages-sheets, approximately,
£331,830 ; freight and charges, £91,332 ; mine-timber,
£17,273 (in 1889 this item was over £lOO,OOO in six
months); coke and coal, £92,579 ; liquid assets in excess
of liabilities, £389,657 ; plant, £407,313 (after writing-off
over £560,000). The co. has an insurance fund of £6OOO,
and a reserve-fund of £l2s,ooo—counted, of course, in the
assets mentioned just now.
* m * *
B.H. North was “resting” last half-year; its
“working expenses” were £BO4, and its “expenses of
management” £64l—which seems to show that 1 ‘ resting”
pays the management better than it does the miner. The
co. has now a well-equipped and developed mine of
medium-grade ore, and an up-to-date plant waiting for
the lead market to pass £l2 before flring-up. When it
knocked off it could not pay its way at much under
£l3 10s.; but the new plant and other changes will
probably make 30s. a ton difference.
* * * *
Lake George Mines (N.S.W.) to be abandoned.
Co. has £5OOO in hand with which “ it is proposed to pur-
chase some other Australian property on which to re-
erect the machinery.” London people must have an idea
that Australian mines are going a-begging, when they
assume that for £5OOO they can get a mine on which to
re-erect the extensive plant at Captain’s Flat. There is
machinery enough there to equip the biggest copper-
mine in the State. Lake George mine has been done to
death by faddists. No practical man can say that it has
been proved conclusively that the mine is a duffer. It
has a lode which for size, and persistency in length and
depth, has no equal in Australia, and, comparatively
speaking, it has been only surface-scratched. It
is true that in the deepest workings zinc pre-
ponderates, and there is only enough copper to swear
by. Zinc has gradually increased in quantity as the lode
has been sunk upon. Here is some expert advice offered
to the co., without any expectation of a 1000-guinea fee
or other reward: Use the £5OOO in hand to sink the
shaft 500 ft. deeper, and see whether the zinc continues to
increase or decrease. If the first contingency occurred,
it might be a profitable zinc-mine. The consensus of
practical opinion is that the zinc zone will be passed
through at no great depth, but Lake George controllers
have never shown any appreciation of really practical
advice.
• • * *
J. H. Curie, of London Economist, who was
out here 12 months ago getting material for his book on
“ The Goldfields of the World,” has published it—nomi-
nally a new edition of an old book ; really a nearly-new
book. In their order he sets the world’s fields : Rand 1,
Cripple Greek 2, Kalgoorlie 3. The Rand is “ 10 times
greater than any other, nob necessarily because of its
greater present output, but because of its permanence.”
In ’9B, 75 Rand mines produced £15,141,376, and of these
40 paid £4,834,159 in divs. There are, he says, 124
co’s. on the main-reef banket beds, and most
of these will eventually give profits. “ For a
number of to come an average yield of
25 millions may be expected with reason, and it
is certain there is ore in sight to ensure a large
output for 30 years more.” Yet, “ in from five to ten
years from now a number of the richest Johannesburg
mines will be worked out,” and “ but few outside mines
will be added to the list of payable ventures.” Of
Rhodesia : “ It is a country of narrow reefs and short
ore-chutes.” Queensland disappointed him ; it “ needs
prospectors and capital.” Victoria has “ many hundreds
of small mines and no great ones.” Of Kalgoorlie : “ The
mines are getting both smaller and poorer in depth,” and
their London management is abominable. Murchison, on
the other hand, surprised him ; “ there are liberally hun-
dreds of reefs there, many of which carry gold.” Klondyke
has “ passed its zenith.” Mr. Curie doesn’t think much
of the average working miner, and he thinks less of the
average London director. As to the former, “ the
majority literally squander their wages on drink ahd
gambling, and live from hand to mouth all the time.” The
wages are so high that the miner should be able to save a
lot of money. Mr. Curie forgets that the metal-miner also
mostly lives where cost of living is highest, and that his
employment is erratic. And so far as it is true that the
miner is a drinker and a gambler (and in regard to
the Australian miner it is nob truer than it is of any
other big body of workmen) it might at least be con-
sidered whether his occupation does not predispose him
to it. Australia’s experience of the miner, and especially
of the gold-digger, is that he is just about as true and
plucky and staunch as anything that stands on legs.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin. — Let me call attention to the
congested state of the W.A. labor market, especially on
the goldfields. There are probably 800 unemployed in
Kalgoorlie-Boulder district, as a walk round the Golden
Mile at change of shift will prove. On the out-back
goldfields, strange to say, there is always a surplus of
labor, and yet arrivals of workers from the Eastern States
largely exceed departures for same. Proportionately to
population, there are more unemployed on the W.A.
goldfields than in Sydney or Melbourne. Yet the gold-
fields are mainly responsible for sending seven Foreign-
traders to the Federal Parliament.—O.
• * * *
That king of W.A. white elephants, Black Flag
Proprietary, is being set going again. A manager, with
the promising name of Solomon Israel, has arrived on the
mine, and intends, under orders from his English direc-
tors, to give the show a thorough test. This is what it
has never yet had. Although starting with the then
record capitalisation of £400,000, a large proportion of
which was actual cash, 9-10ths of the money was wasted
on the surface. A magnificent 50-head battery—the best
then on the fields—was put up. It was to have been 100
head, but the other 50 stamps were wisely counter-
nianded. Palatial residences were built for manager and
staff, brickmaking plant established, costly carriage-drive
made from the manager’s house to the mine, and—fitting
climax—a tennis-court was laid out, and a dark room for
photo-developing excavated in the main shaft. The first
crushing went either 5 or 7dwts., and the mine promptly
went bung. The main shaft collapsed shortly afterwards,
and completed the shareholders’ troubles. The exasper-
ating part of the affair is that those who know the
mine best say that it is all right—they have
endeavored to get hold of it on tribute, and have
continually opposed all labor ' suspensions. There
is a huge body of quartz outcropping for chains,
which has not, so far, been even faintly proved. The
huge share-capital that has been wasted will be a dead-
weight round the manager’s neck ; but writer will be sur-
prised if he doesn’t find something worth crushing.
Black Flag was the show which gave Henning—now a
leviathan in the London mining world—his start towards
fortune. What has become of Sam. Gifford, the Cornish
mining engineer, who reported so enthusiastically on the
property ? Has he realised that nothing fails like failure ?
Good money is being made in a quiet way by
small parties in W.A. Near Black Flag a party of four, in
12 months, raised and washed 1000 tons of alluvial, which
averaged 7dwts., the gold being worth a little over £4 an
oz. After paying ex.’s, this left a splendid return. The
party has another 1000 tons out, which, however, is not
expected to average over sdwts., and yet they are well
satisfied. The gold comes from what looks like the bed
of an ancient lake, about 100 ft. below the surface.
Vigorous prospecting has failed to pick up the lead out-
side this claim.
• * * *
Gleisberg, late mananger of Half Mile Reef,
Bardoc, and of Lady Loch, Coolgardie (W.A.), has ful-
filled the predictions of the prophets by taking charge of
the Associated. Until Gleisberg gets into the run of
things, Hewitson is to remain and act as wetnurse. As
the Associated is almost on its last legs, and all known
ore bodies will be pretty well worked out by the end of
the year, the question of management is of little moment.
No pay ore has been found below 600 ft. level, but strenu-
ous efforts are being made to locate the continuation of
the Brown Hill-Iron Duke-Oroya chute. Looks now
as if the Australia East is the same lode thrown eastwards
by the upheaval of the Kalgurli Australia hill. A small
pocket of ore has been got at the 1050 ft. level, and may
be an indication of a lens. Exploration at this point is
being pushed ahead feverishly.
Ten heads of Bayley’s (W.A.) mill have made a
start on stone accumulated from recent exploration, so
that this mine, for a time at least, will help the gold pro-
duction of the State. The reef at 1000 ft. level has been
driven on for nearly 200 ft., and averages about sdwt. for
a width of sft. This is not much good, but improvement
is anticipated.
* • * *
C. A. Saunders, new manager of Kanowna
Champion lode, is trying to make shareholders believe
that a leader, which yielded 22dwts. average, would pay,
if properly worked. As said leader runs nearly hori-
zontally between two duffer lodes, and entails shifting 10
tons of mullock for one of ore, and stone is hauled by
windlass, the expectation seems imaginative. Directors
blame late manager Don for crushing ldwt. ore, but the
facts are that, for two years Don refused to erect battery
bought from defunct wild-cat Carbine, and finally put it
up and crushed stone under directorial compulsion. So
far as proved, mine is an absolute cat, and was floated
to exploit mythical north lead lode.
* • * •
Manager Askin Nicholas, who retired from
Queen Margaret (W.A.), after the mine was practically
brought to a standstill, has quitted the Peak Hill, and
his place has been filled by R. Leslie Hall. Stacks of
“siller” have been spent in cabling ’Ome the great
potentialities of the mine, and this has managed to boost
scrip some 20s. The retirement of Nicholas should con-
vince the most sceptical that the time for handing over
the baby to one’s dearest friend has arrived.
• * * *
Sinking of the main shaft on the Associated
Northern (W.A.), will be resumed shortly. Developments
at No. 6 and 7 levels are still being pushed ahead, and
large bodies of rich ore are being opened up. The
accumulation of telluride ore now totals nearly 2500 tons,
and is worth at least £50,000. This lot is going to
Illawarra instead of Fremantle, as the local co. seems to
be in a comatose condition.
• * * *
Ex-Mayor Tom Doyle, of Kanowna(W. A.), has
treated another 10 tons from his leader for 1720 z., bring-
ing up the total, for about three months’ work, to 1060 oz.
from 22J tons. This same leader put Doyle on his feet
some eight years ago, but he threw it up under the belief
that it had cut out, and only re-started when Dame
Fortune ceased to smile on him in business.
* • * *
The licensing of gold-buyers has not panned out
up to prospects in the West, and the licences will most
likely be abolished. The Mint will, in that case, be the
sole buyer, with the banks (and it is suggested also the
post-offices) acting as agents for it.
# * « *
The Anchor Consolidated, Tuckanara (W.A.),
one of the Zeb. Lane-Frank Gardner cats, was handed
over to tributors at the beginning of June, after two years
of fruitless work. Although no stone was in sight and
the workings were dangerous owing to mine being flooded,
the 23 tributors, backed by 20 wages-men, have already
got out and treated 300 tons of stone for 300 oz. Tribute
is for five years, so the men seem to have a good thing on.
The wages-men are being paid £4 13s. 4d. weekly—con-
siderably more than co. paid, but the men must work —
there’s no boss so strenuous as a working-man.
* * * *
Three “seemingly valuable” discoveries have
been made in the Associated (W.A.), the most important
being at 1000 ft., where they are supposed to have struck
the continuation of the rich Northern Associated-Oroya
shoot.
* • * *
The 10 cats held in W.A. by the Standard
Exploration Co. were recently offered by tender, and
(barring Hannan’s Golden Treasure, consisting of 81 acres,
adjoining the Golden Horseshoe on the south) have been
acquired by Birkbeck, Hoffmann and Jowett. Of the
litter, the Duke at Broad Arrow, Golden Crown at
Kanowna, Golden Dream and Golden Group at Kalgoorlie,
Mainland Consols at Lake Austen, Wealth of Nations and
Wealth of Nations Extended at Dunsville, and Paddington
South at Paddington have all been practically abandoned
as worthless. Paddington Consols at Paddington is
about the only mine that will be operated on by B. H.
and J. This property is equipped with a 40-head mill,
which used to treat about 4000 tons of 12-dwt. ore
monthly till it was closed down 18 months ago by the
Whitaker Wright smash. The buyer of the Golden
Treasure has not transpired, but it is probably the
Golden Horseshoe co. or Kaufman. No details of the
deals yet made public.
• * * •
Golden Horseshoe (W. A.) last return of 16, lOloz.
from 1044 tons included 254 tons of 12oz. ore, despite
which the average grade showed a decline of lOdwt. per
ton. Value of yield at £3 12s. Bd. per oz., £58,500, which
should show a profit of at least £30,000, or (say) 2s. per
share. Mine still opening satisfactorily, and, so far as
one can judge, can maintain present returns for a long
time.
* * * *
Golden Link Consols, Kalgoorlie (W.A.), a
rehash of four wild cats, has again come to the end of its
tether, and is in such sorry plight that directors have to
raise the wind for a further flutter by issuing £90,000 of
10 per cent, debentures at a discount of 25 per cent. This
means raising money at 12J per cent. Co. holds “ invest-
ments,” alleged to be worth £62,852, but, as these con-
sist of scrip in its offspring, Chaffers Extended, said
investments as an asset are an absolute farce. The raison
d’etre of the recent “discovery” of the 12ft. lode, worth
2oz. 4dwts. per ton on the Eclipse line, is now apparent;
100 tons of this wonderful stone is now being treated at
the Croesus battery, and the result will be awaited with
interest, if not with anxiety. Every effort will doubtless
be made to get at least one good return.
• * * *
Long Reef Co., Lennonville (W.A.), first)
crushing of 915 tons from Galtee More lease, recently
acquired from I. V. Kearney for 16,600 reserve shares, and
£3400 cash, returned 45Soz. Co. previously held these
leases under option, but abandoned them, it is alleged,
on the recommendation of their engineers, only to re-
buy them at about three times the original figure. As
the stone only averages, lOdwts. the purchase does not
look a good one.

Associated Northern, Kalgoorlie (W.A.), hav-
ing resumed shipments of rich telluride ore to smelters,
some good returns may be looked for in the near future.
A specially picked parcel of 7 tons shipped to England
yielded 19760 z. gold. There is some talk of erecting *
plant on this mine, but nothing definite can be learn •
Strenuous efforts to bring about amalgamation are still
being made by the Brown Hill authorities.
Ida H., Laverton (W.A.), had a full run with
its treatment-plant last month, and produced 1028 oz.
from 850 tons, as against 7300 z. from 606 tons during May.
Cause of small May return was a break down. ims co.
has an issued capital of only £54,000, on which a 10 per
cent. div. was recently paid, and a further early discn iu-
tion is now looked for. Co. is controlled by John
Haddington, the “ Boulder King.”
Great Boulder (W.A.) is pushing on its no 8 ! 11
shaft, and very shortly exploration at 1000 ft. will be
initiated. Mine on the whole is opening well, but the
exploratiomof the continuation of thelvanhoe lodes from
Jansen shaft, on the north block, seems to be resulting
in the fiasco the diamond-drill foretold 18 months ago.
Unless the installation of the Merton furnaces materially
increases returns and reduces costs, divs. are unlikely to
show much, if any, enhancement.
v i* * *
According to its latest published return, the
Kalgurli (W.A.) cleared a monthly profit of £6OOO. This
result was, however, brought about by the practical
abandonment of development, as, out of total costs of
£6846, development figures for £237 only. As 3160 tons
were treated for 33050 z., the amount expended on de-
velopment is Is. 6d. per ton only, or one-fifth of the
Ivanhoe figure. R. S. Black, who long figured as manager
of this mine, has resigned, and Frank Moss, who really
managed the mine, now gets the sole credit or discredit.
The W.A. Mining and Oil Corporation seems to
have struck good “ile” in its 640-acre patch, Warren
River, near Cape Leeuwin. Boring operations were con-
ducted to a depth of 525 ft. through a formation said to be
very similar to the famdus Texas oil-field, But let the
discovery be as good as alleged, and what will be its
value ? Absolutely nil. Why ? Because .W.A. members
have combined with N.S.W. members in the. Federal
Parlt.,and have decided that the Standard Oil Co.. of
America shall have undisputed monopoly of the Australian
oil trade.
* * * *
The owners of Payne’s (N.S.W.) process for
treating copper ores by leaching have applied for patents
all over the world. The secret of the process is in the roast-
ing of the ore—the complete sulphatising of the copper.
Patentees are satisfied that they have overcome this
difficulty, and talk about crushing and treating any class
of copper ore for less than the value of a unit of copper
per ton, with the advantage in the case of auro-cupriferous
ore that the tailings will be left in a perfect condition for
the recovery of the gold by amalgamation or cyanide.
They have arranged for the use of N.S.W. Govt, metal-
lurgical works at Clyde, and will give public demonstra-
tions of the working of the process on bulk parcels of ore.
v • * *
Lord Nelson, St. Arnaud, is one of the standard
mines of Victoria. In a few weeks’ time it will get over
the £200,000 dividend mark. Zeb. Lane, sen., who has
managed the mine for nearly 20 years, assures share-
holders that it will pay divs. for years to come. Last
half-year 11,795 tons crushed for £24,421, or about £2
Is. 4d. per ton. Divs. paid £9OOO, or about 14s. 9d. per
ton. Working costs seem excessive. Cyaniding of tail-
ings does not show much profit—return, £1396; expenses,
£1309. With good equipment, mine should pay at least
10s. per ton more in divs. But an up-to-date plant would
mean a cessation of divs. for a few months, which share-
holders would not be likely to approve of.
» • * *
Quartz Hill Co., Chewton, Vic., last half-year
won £3950 worth of gold at a cost of £3159. It sold
4475 trust shares for £lB7O. As stated by the chairman
on 22nd July the co. had £l2Bl 3s. 9d. on hand, out of
which it declared a 6d. div., or £l2OO. The div. is really
being paid out of capital, for had it not been for the pro-
ceeds of trust shares there would have been no div., and
the co. would have been £670 in debt. The board “in
future intends to pay a considerable sum out of the earn-
ings to a reserve fund.” The odd £Bl 3s. 9d. is a good
round sum to start with.
* * * *
New Moou (Bendigo) record to the end of June :
Tons crushed, 267,345 ; gold, 104,7720 z. ; profit,
£145,075. Value of ore, £1 11s. per ton ; profit, 11s. No
call since 1890. Latterly co. has been paying better divs.
on 7dwt. dirt than it used to pay on 12dwts. Electricity
to be introduced for underground haulage to effect
further saving. Mine still in its infancy.

How little the Municipalities Act helps in
fixing the town-rate on mines is aptly illustrated by what
has this year happened to Broken Hill Block 14. The
municipal valuers assessed that mine to pay £358.. Under
threat of more law-proceedings, the council cut this down
to £215. The co. took an appeal to the local court, which
cut that down to £56. Of couree where such differences
are possible law-fights are certain.

GLA[?]GOW.
The statue raised to the memory of the
late Baronet Clarke, of Victoria, the man who
was made famous by inheriting nearly all his
father’s money and spending some of it, has been
dumped on to perhaps the best site in all Mel-
bourne—at the main entrance to the Treasury
Gardens, top of Collins-st. The female figure in
the memorial is a fine, vital piece of work, carved
with the delicacy and strength that Mackennal so
signally possesses. It is not too great praise to
say that the sculptor has managed the drapery as
few modern artists have ever succeeded in doing.
The back of the girl is, perhaps, a trifle under-
modelled, and the figure of a boy seated at her
feet is not ideally successful. Mackennal’s effort
to secure an original pose leads to a clumsy appear-
ance from certain positions. The drapery, how-
ever, atones for all. The whole is a work of art
in the best sense of the word ; and, under the
circumstances, more’s the pity. It is a standing
jibe against Melbourne, this marble to a man who
was a man of money and nothing else. Mac-
kennal has not carved a single dollar into his
group to show that the stone is raised to the ever-
lasting glory of Spot Cash. At least, the first
baronet’s bust should have been hewn from gold-
bearing quartz.
BEFORE THE TAILORS HOARDING.
Liz.: “ Say, is there really blokes like that? ”
They put the other Clarke, the State Governor,
up to orate at the undraping of the statue of
“ Big ” Clarke 11. His Ex. knew nothing about
the deceased baronet, who died long before his
arrival here, but he dripped out what had been
Eoured into him with quite the touching air of one
ereaved, and enlarged copiously on the de-
parted’s many noble attributes —among them the
fact that he “ patriotically maintained half a
battery of horse-artillery.” Half a battery ! —how
ignoble do fractions look in a panegyric! Peacock,
K.C.M.G., also spoke a piece, mainly about the
benefioences of the gone-before baronet, whom
hardly anyone on earth knew anything about.
After which the company departed, leaving the
statue to be a marble mystery to a generation that
will never realise why so large a rook should have
been thrown at so small a man.
• • * «
Re the reeent Redfern (Sydney) shooting
ease. The statement that the police have dis-
covered the identity of one of the desperadoes from
the detective office’s collection of photographs is
somewhat discredited by the remarkable readiness
of people in general to identify any photo., or
any person— for that matter. Present writer was
interested in & case in which half-a-dozen people
were shown a heap of widely-dissimilar photos.,
and, though the verdict was in no ease unanimous,
every individual photo, was identified by some
of the number as being that of the person
“wanted”—one man, indeed, thus “identifying”
every photo, of the pile.
Re the recent shooting of two policemen in
a Sydney suburb. The policeman’s revolver is a
foolish weapon. It is generally used to frighten a
man who is running away. If the law-breaker has
a pistol himself and doesn’t run off, the policeman
has no chance with his “gun,” for the criminal,
knowing what to expect, is ready first. And,
since the latter knows that, if caught, his shot, no
matter what the result, will mean hanging, he
may as well shoot to kill. It is all one price, so
long as the law idiotically punishes a constructive
attempt to murder as if it were murder itself.
Such a law, in fact, is in itself an incitement to
murder.
• • • •
A pitiful sequel to the murder of Constable
Guilfoyle, by that desperate pair of coiners, was
the end of Joseph Heaps, a young fellow who,
whilst travelling the country in search of a living,
was surrounded by a party of scared bucolics,
under the leadership of Constable Madden, and
shot dead last week, at his camp-fire, on suspicion
of being Guilfoyle’s murderer. This tragedy hap-
pened in the Queanbeyan (N.S.W.) district, and
it is very hard to see how, even in Queanbeyan,
there could have arisen any suspicion against poor
Joseph Heaps, seemingly a thoroughly decent
fellow and the chief support of his widowed
mother.
He the shooting of Joseph Heaps, in mistake,
by some members of the police party, here is an
excerpt from an interview with the slaughtered
man’s mother:
. J° e was his father’s favorite, and when he was dying
in the hospital he specially asked for him. His father is
buried at Rookwood, and I would like him to be buried
there too. The police wanted me to let him be buried at
Queanbeyan, as there would be a lot of expense in bring-
ing the bodj' to Sydney. They asked me if I had enough
money to pay for it, but I said I hadn’t. I think they
ought to bring him down, don’t you ?
Tho meanness, the callousness of it! N.S.W.
owes a substantial money grant to the poor woman
as compensation for the loss of her bread-winner,
and should pay it without heckling.

The classics have provided instances of
man’s conspicuous devotion to his mother-in-law,
but a discovery of such mixed relationship in a
high circle of a certain Australian city comes as a
shock to most people. In this case the wife is
deserted for her youthful and more attractive
mamma. The husband is a leading light in his own
spheres. And now the worm turns —towards the
law-courts.
