Yankee missioners Torrey and Alexander
have evidently got Hobart Mercury on their side,
and the poor old Devil is having a bad time in
consequence. Sample of Mercury reporting of
the variety-show run by the pushful gents with
the twang:
HOBART UNITED MISSION.
The Drama of Life in Three Acts.
The three meetings conducted by the American
evangelists yesterday were undoubtedly of the highest
order. At the noon day meeting in the Town Hall Dr.
Torrey continued his series of addresses on “ Why I
believe the Bible to be the Word of God.” We are of the
opinion that no more able defence of the Sacred Scripture
has ever been heard by a Tasmanian audience. ... A
magnificent congregation of men and women listened
with rapt attention to Mr. Alexander’s marvellous singing,
and.to Dr. Torrey’s clear-cut and telling discourse on the
subject of prayer. . . . The evangelists have made church-
going at all hours the most popular business in this city.
Mr. Alexander began to handle his vast choir with
exquisite skill, long before the service actually began. The
charm, and wonder, the skill and beauty of these choir
practices are things that will be for years and years trea-
sured as blessed memories in many a heart and many a hom e.
Christianity had its incarnation in the realm of music. . .
The preacher’s delineation of the desolation of a soul in
sin struck clean through into brain and heart. We saw
aged men bend their heads in confusion, as though they
had been there. We saw young faces appalled, as if by
the dreadful consciousness that they were hastening there.
Torrey’s definition of sin is “ Doing what you please.”
“ Whosoever wants to have his own way is a sinner.” The
end of that way is desolation, absolute' and awful. It is
the process that pulls a man’s coat from his back and
puts it on the publican’s back. It is the process by which
a man ultimately qualifies himself for “ A hog-tender to
the devil.” . . . The last scene was full of pathos and
beauty, representing the repentance of the prodigal; the
white-haired old man shading his eyes from the gleam of
the setting sun, as afar off on the horizon he sees the
wanderer returning ; the glad, grand embrace of love and
the kiss of peace : the smothered confession : the music
and the glorious revelry, above which sits Love in robes of
kingly splendor crowned with joy. The great congrega-
tion of more than 2000 human souls sat with bowed heads
while Alexander’s song, “ Where is my wandering boy to-
night,” thrilled, surely, every heart not yet dead to all
virtue.
For a secular paper this is distinctly not too bad.
Similar vermilion-tinted “reports” appeared in
the Mercury right through the Torrey season, all
of them more or less definitely suggestive of the
conversion of the editorial “we.” Folks were
amazed, and it was nightly expected that
Proprietor Davies (that isn’t his turf-name) would
rush to the penitent form and empty all his Bay
Eagle steeplechase winnings into Parson Torrey’s
capacious hat. But no such thing happened, and
Hobart is now divided as to whether the Mercury
has been “ pulling the public leg ” or whether
Torrey has been clever enough to jerk the
Mercury’s respected limb to make it dance like
that. As Torrey is understood to have done time
as a journalist the odds are on the latter propo-
sition.
Horrible thought! Torrey and co. are coming
to Sydney. Can the foregoing artfully-written
paragraph have been fixed up by their press-agent?
QUITS: A SALT CHESTNUT
ILLUSTRATED.
SQUAREHEAD SAILOR (throwing down a sovereign
so hard that it rebounds): “ Ya! yump, you
English one poun', yump! Das times was
many I yumped for you.”
A romance of real life that came rather
rough on the other woman. A wife from one of
the other States lately engaged a private detective
to search for the husband who deserted her 15
years ago. She had a mind to serve the long-lost
sinner with divorce papers, if he were discover-
able, but her scruples—she being R.C.—troubled
her considerably. In fact, the lady was still
hesitating at the divorce question when her agent,
after careful search, came along" with the news
that she was a widow of four days standing. The
missing man had been run to earth in the most
solemn sense of the term. He was buried the day
before. Death had dissolved the marriage, the
widow’s scruples were at rest, her husband had
died intestate, and she bore away his estate —
worth £2ooo—from right under the nose of the
unlawful relict!
* • * *
When all is said and done, the average
gentle shepherd misses a splendid lot of opportuni-
ties for getting into the Divorce Court. Probably
he is the most tempted mortal on earth, if he doesn’t
happen to be repulsively ugly. The actor, artist,
or any other sort of recognised Bohemian (includ-
ing the young man who calls for the rent) is a
particular mark for the advances of the
everyday flirty angel, whereas the parson
has to suffer the temptations of a particu-
lar tribe of Delilahs and Potiphar’s wive 3
who lie in wait for him alone. And the
women who wouldn’t think of “falling” unless
the Church were concerned in the tumble, are
probably a more dangerous class than even the
moderately serious sisterhood that inclines towards
medical men. Of course no journalist can speak
positively on this point. One must be right in
the know— i.e., in the “ cloth ” —to be an authority
re the wiles of the ladies who want their cup of
salvation tea to be sugared with a little bit of sin.
* * • *
Ten years ago, on July 3, the big three-
months’ strike began at Broken Hill, and the
bitterness of it is still in many mouths. Even
now there is no party cry half so effective among
Broken Hill laborites as ‘ ‘ Remember ’92 ! ” and
every few weeks somebody or other, who has got
into a hot argument, spends half-a-crown in
asserting, in the local paper, that he didn’t “ black-
leg ” during the trouble. To accuse a Broken
Hill mine-workman of that is still to accuse him
of worse than anything which the law punishes.
• * * *
Ifc was the long arm of coincidence which
lately guided the Broken Hill Miners’ Association
to send in their claim to the Arbitration Court on
the 10th anniversary of the start of the great ’92
strike—July 3. Defeated in the strike, the con-
tract system was forced on the men, and a wage-
cut followed. The men’s application is for a new
contract agreement and a return to the wage-scale
of ’92. The co.’s have been consulting with a
view to hitting back with a petition to cancel the
registration of the Miners’ Association, on the
ground that it has rules of a character similar to,
but more obnoxious than, the political rules of the
A.W.U. Meantime the Miners’ Association is
hurrying up with a revision of its code.
• • • *
T’other morning, as a Lavender Bay (Syd-
ney) ferry-steamer drew up to Circular Quay, a
boy, jumping not wisely but too soon, took a
bath ; whereupon a man cast himself—hat,
umbrella, pipe, morning paper and all-over the
side and fell in right on top of the boy as he
rose for the first time. The two disappeared,
then rose again ; and the man, whose natatory
powers were not equal to his pluck, was seized by
the boy, and towed by the hair of his head to the
wharf-side. Amidst great applause from the
spectators, the would-be rescuer was landed in
good order and condition, but very wet, and minus
his dignity, hat, paper, pipe, and umbrella.
Certain forenoon trams from
Sydney suburbs into the city
might almost be called “ hospital
trams,” so many children do they
carry who are being escorted to
“the ’orspital” by women who,
for pure love of the thing, have
taken charge on behalf of parents
—mothers are seldom there.
These women generally get talk-
ing in a strain that freezes the
veins of their unfortunatecharges.
“Takin’ ’im inter th’ ’orspital,”
asks one woman. “ Yes e’s got
somethin’ hup with ’is heyes.
Come ’ere, Tommy, an’ show the
lydy yer heyes. I don’t think
’e’ll git hover th’ hoperation,
meself.” “Oh, ’e’s goin’ under
a hoperation, is ’e? Pore little
chap ! You’ll bear it like a man;
won’t yer, Tommy?” Tommy,
then made to realise the situation,
whimpers dolefully. “ Pooh ! that’s
nothin’,” says his sympathiser;
“look at this ittle boy, ’e’s got to
go through a worse one.” “Wot’s th’ matter
with ’im ? ” asks Tommy’s guardian. “ Oh, ’e’s
got something wrong with ’ees bowels.” And
then follows a cheerful dissertation on entrails,
which makes two wretched kids wish they’d never
been born.
• * • •
An interesting document is a circular by
Mr. F. Webster, sec. of N.S.W. “Royal” Agri-
cultural Society, appealing for subscriptions for
“ a testimonial of a lasting character to the Hon.
Sir John See, K.C.M.G., M.L.A.” Sir See is
president of the Agri. Society aforesaid; he is
also Premier of the State ; and that possibly helps
to explain why the Society is allowed to hold an
area of public park and to spend a huge public
subsidy, simply for the purpose of catering for the
public amusement in a not-too-creditable fashion.
But why should the public—already victimised
by the mis-use of its park and of its subsidy—have
the salt rubbed in by a begging appeal for guineas
for the rich John See ?

There was a delightful omen at the peace-thanksgiving

to give thanks.

S.A. military officers and men are wroth
with the manner in which they are being treated
by the haughty Hutton. The companies are
gradually growing very thin at parade. The
unceasing flood of new rules and regulations
causes much irritation, but the force resents most
bitterly the practical dismissal of local drill-
instructors and the invasion of teachers from
other States nearer the Iluttonian eye.

There was a very remarkable defence to a
Ballarat (Vic.) maintenance case the other day.
The girl’s yarn was that, she was a domestic ser-
vant in the house of an elderly Christian with an
invalid wife and grown-up family. He seduced
her, she said, and the intimacy continued
at his house and elsewhere in Ballarat. In
Feb, 1901, they went to Melbourne together
and occupied the same room. A year later she
gave birth to a baby. Her elderly friend had
meanwhile helped to get her into a refuge, from
whence she wrote him, under an assumed name,
but couldn’t get him to take up the responsibilities
of an illegitimate father. The girl’s chief witness
was the shepherd of the Bible Christian Church
which had brought all parties concerned into
communion. The shepherd said “he had seen
undue attention paid by defendant to plaintiff.”
Then the elderly Sunday-school teacher told his
yarn. He denied having been amorous with his
handmaiden, but admitted that she went to Mel-
bourne with him. He didn’t want to travel per
same train as the spinster. It was she 'who took
him in hand, saying “I’m going to stay where
you are, and nowhere else.” They found accom-
modation at a small hotel where there was but one
spare bedroom, with two beds in it. He lay down
on one bed, clothed and in his moral mind, whilst
the damsel lay on the other. No impropriety
occurred, and at break of day he arose and left
his persistent young friend sleeping the sleep of
innocence. Some months later, when the forth-
coming event was being talked about, he protested
to the brethren that he had been the unfortunate
victim of circumstances. The case looked sus-
picious, but he couldn’t help that. The girl got him
alone in Melbourne and refused to leave him.
So far, things looked blue for the elderly pietist,
it being a maxim of law that if two adults of
opposite sexes sleep in the one room something is
to be pr-esumed (unless they happen to beman and
wife). But the next witness, a son of defendant,
aged 21, put a different complexion on things.
Sonny deposed that once when he was walking
home from Sunday-school with plaintiff she
amorously assaulted him, and the subsequent
results were sinful. Also an elder brother testi-
fied to having susoected the last witness of
dalliance with the damsel nine months previous to
the birth of her baby. Hearing all these things,
the Ballarat East Bench decided that the paternity
of this Sunday-school child was not proven, and
they advised the police to inquire into the
mysteries of the case, and discover who had com-
mitted perjury.
* * * *
The Ballarat Sunday-school superinten-
dent’s story of how he took the female teacher to
Melbourne and she wouldn’t leave him, although,
as a shocked superintendent, he wanted badly to
get rid of her, showed that he wasn’t a man of
resource. In a large city like Melbourne, if a
superintendent finds a Sunday-school teacher
persistently walking alongside him with sinful ends
in view, and he doesn’t want to call the police,
there are plenty of ways out of the difficulty.
The most gentlemanly way is to walk straight on
till he reaches St. Kilda, then back through Prahran
to Richmond and thence to Northcote, and,
if she is still along with him, to walk from North-
cote to Port Melbourne, and next to Footscray,
and if he hasn’t lost her by that time, to walk
from Footscray to Kensington, and North Mel-
bourne and Carlton and Brunswick, and then
make swiftly into Collingwood—by which time
an utterly wrecked teacher with her boots worn
off her feet will have fainted in the roadway and
been left behind. Another idea is to go into a
Turkish bath and start taking off his clothes.
If she is waiting on the pavement when he comes
out he can go into a barber’s and get his hair cut.
If she is waiting when he comes out with his hair
cut he can go into a hotel and start drinking. If
that doesn’t shake her off, the next idea is to go
into a dentist’s, and, if she accompanies him there,
to tell the dentist to extract four of her teeth.
Someone or other of these proceedings would
surely bring about a climax. Another idea is to
ride about the city in trams, and, if the lady
follows, make her pay her own fares till she is
“ broke,” and is at last put off the tram, or run-in ;
by getting off immediately the conductor has
collected the 3d. the cost of tram-riding can
be run up to 7s. 6d. an hour. Also, a superin-
tendent can simply run for it with his coat-tails
flying in the wind, and the Sunday-school teaching
lady left hopelessly in the rear. If he wants a
good start he can take the lady into a boot shop
and tell her to select a 275. pair, and fly as soon
as she has taken her boots off to try the other ones
on. He can walk through the West Melbourne
swamp, where she can’t follow him ; in fact,
there are lots of ways.
And this superintendent says that ho did none
of these things; he allowed the lady to follow
him to a hotel, and she forcibly stayed
all night in his room. He didn’t get out
of the room ; he stayed there too, and lay awake
all the time dwelling upon the horror of the
situation. He was a woefully helpless and un-
enterprising superintendent.

Rude man is interested to observe that
the fat woman of Sydney block has ‘ ■ stowed her
bingey”—per aid of the new Yankee “straight-
fronted corset,” a high priestess whereof, with the
most lovely twang ever heard, lately arrived from
the land of wooden hams, on a fat-compressing
mission. The fat woman’s front adiposity
has mostly disappeared, but where it has gone to
is an anatomical puzzle. However, from the
queer waddle she has taken on, it would seem that
the erstwhile fat party does not feel altogether
comfortable when she tries to walk.

“H.A.M.”: Some of Sydney’s most resi-
dential suburbs abound with crumbling houses
only a few years old. I know of a man who has
gone bankrupt because he couldn’t sell quickly
enough a lot of houses built on sand. Some houses
are built entirely of used bricks, and I know of
one cottage that is being built with a half-brick
wall. Badly wanted, an effective Building Act.
“It is said that marriages havp increased
greatly this year, as if the union of King and
country had been the means of inspiring other
unions.”
- Sub.
Unto matrimonial cares—
Scrubbing boards and kitchen chairs ;
I never had much leaning, if you please,
But a gleam of new romance
Comes my feelings to entrance
With the crowning of the King beyond the seas.
Bugler Love goes tootle-toot,
Beats my heart a proud salute,
And the windows of my soul illuminate;
Shows my cheek its loyal red
For my own particular Ned,
And our marriage is a Coronation FSte.
He.
When I hear the joy-bells ring
For a far-off Queen and King,
They lend a grander meaning to my bliss ;
And I feel my love expand
To the confines of the land
As I greet you with a large imperial kiss.
It makes our future seem
Like a vast imperial dream
Like a Pageant, a Parade, a Grand Review !
I was happy all jpy life,
Never wishful for a wife,
But I want to own an Empire, and that’s you.
Constance Clyde.

Somebody’s aide-de-camp made a parti-
cular object of himself at a dinner which some of
his sporty pals gave him a while ago- He brought
the festival to a close by walking along the full
length of the table and kicking all the glassware
and breakables off it. The hosts, it is said, paid
the hotel bill for the smashes of their brilliant
guest.
* * * »
A man who evidently repented at leisure,
and left room for others to repent leisurely also,
was Charles Gilheany, who died in Melbourne in
’97. His will was only lodged lately, there being
apparently an all-round gradualness, not to say
dilatoriness, about the Gilheany family. Most of
testator’s estate consisted of a few square yards of
land right alongside Goldsbrough, Mort and Co.’s
place in Bourke-st. This is now valued at £400, but
in the boom days G.M. and Co. offered £10,000 for
it. Gilheany refused, and waited long for the
offer to be raised. The rise never came ; but even
in death he never lost hope. His will specified
that a nephew was to have the rents arising from
the block for 50 years, but was not to sell it for
less than £10,000 plus £1000 for every year since
testator’s death, so that in 1947 he can take
£60,000 for the property. Gilheany doesn’t seem
to have cared what happened to the land after 50
years.

• * * *
The two Southern capitals have recently
supplied an electrocution-sensation apiece. In
Melbourne, a young fellow was aloft at 3 a.m.,
attending to an arc-light at the railway-station,
when a live wire came his way. He was found
hanging among the wires, quite dead. The
Hobart aff lir was equally startling, though the
victims were horses. One of the cross-wires of
the electric tramline broke adrift, and hung in a
loop over the roadway. The obstacle was not
visible in the dark, the coach-horses ran their
heads into the loop, and fell dead. Had the wire
hung a foot higher, it would have missed the
horses and—there were three persons on the box-
seat of the coach.

It is more than suspected that there is
often a lot of hanky-panky in
N.S. W. in connection with the
sale of property by Govt,
officials. A recent case : in
a bankrupt estate certain
goods, worth (say) £60,
were advertised to be sold.
Two persons anxious to
secure them, and ready to
bid up to £50, inquired as
to the time of sale, and on
turning up punctually
found that the articles had
been knocked down for £3
to a bidder two hours
before. There is strong
presumptive evidence there
of crooked collusion to sell
goods at much under their
real value, to the loss of the
State or of the creditors of a
bankrupt estate. The matter
should be brought before
Parliament.
Dissatisfaction with the management of
the Queen Victoria Scholarship for Women ia
raging in Sydney. The fact that the aid of mere
man was invoked at the initial meeting vexes the
soul of the New Woman. One of her speaks:
“The Victoria Scholarship which is in honor Of
a woman, intended For women, originated By
women-, by means of subscriptions From women,
should be done With the help of women only.
Speeches by a few sensible, sober women would
have been of more value than the drunken
maunderings of politicians.” The New Woman’s
efforts to reform the world are frequently inter-
rupted by a wild rush to shoo off the man who
wants to help reform. It is an illogical attitude,
betraying a consciousness of inferiority—this
jealousy of male assistance.
• * * *
Adelaide is very sorry to lose the Tenny-
sons. H. E. has only made one political mistake
worth mentioning in his term of office; and his
wife has been equally at home in agitating for (and
getting) a maternity hospital, or in putting the
blue ribbon round a Derby winner’s neck. Both
have been geniality itself to all comers. Not a
breath of scandal has touched Government House
under their rule; and this (though many of the
bygone scandals were very probably unfounded) is
more than can be said of any predecessors there
for 20 years or so back.

onaSydney har-
bor-frontage. A
certain Balmain
residence, for-
merly of much

ants, is let at a small rent, and when its

Then he turns his attention to the catch-

ing 100 dead rats was washed up—at a time when
rat-corpses were worth 3d. each.
9 * * *
The Brisbane project of a co., with £5000
capital, to run a motor-car passenger-service
between Brisbane and Rockhampton is an inte-
resting one. Present proposal is to run four cars,
each with a capacity of 16 to 20 passengers ; two
to leave each city daily. There is a vast virgin
field of usefulness in Australia awaiting the motor-
car, and, if the present proposal has any success,
other ventures will rapidly follow. A swift,
regular service of cars, of about 10 passengers and
mail capacity, running, say, from Bourke to
Moree or Narrabri via Walgett, or from Cobar to
Hay via Condobolin, or direct, would do away
with changing stations right through, and should
pay handsomely on an expended capital of £4000
or £5000.

A well-paid and highly-valued S.A. Govt,
servant, lately retrenched with many others, went

aimlessly along Port Adelaide wharf at midnight,
a cold and shivering imbecile. Which is the first
recorded blessing of the Almighty on the ruthless
slaughter of the Jenkins crowd.

A M.L. Presbyterian congregation decided
that the kirkyard would be the better for some
trees, and prominent members of the flock accord-
ingly donated young trees, to be planted as Coro-
nation mementoes. When the Jmgorations were
declared off, the elders were puzzled a bit about
the trees, but, after due consideration, they
decided to proceed with the ceremony “in memory
of the King’s illness ! ”

Bishop Goulburn deprecates the Australian
habit of calling the mother “old woman.” The
Bishop is quite right. None the less is it amusing
to notice the touchiness of the clergy on the
“reverence” question. If old women be shorn
of their dues of respect whatever will happen to
our ghostly comforters ?

“ While there’s life there’s a crib to crack.”
Edward Moore, aged 75 years, was lately sentenced
at Sydney, to four years’ “ hard ” for house-
breaking. At the outset of his career, in Victoria,
he got 10 years for highway robbery ; later on he
served two sentences of seven and eight years
respectively for robbery under arms ; after which,
in N.S.W., he, by various offences, compiled a
further gaol record of 20 years, apart from 12
months extra for attempting to escape. Early in
1901 the veteran retired to Rookwood Benevolent
Asylum, but, emerging on June 26 with an aged
colleague to view the Coronation celebrations, the
ruling passion recurred, and “ up ” he goes again.
Moore’s prison-career totals 45 years.
Vic. Acting - Commissioner of Railways
Fitzpatrick, in a recent report, proves that the
swagger military person—and, in a less degree,
the rifle-club person —have been defrauding the
State wholesale by abusing their “free-pass ” con-
cession. The Act provides that Every per-
son engaged to serve in the naval and military
forces, if in uniform, or carrying a rifle, shall be
conveyed free to and from all musters, drills,
parades, and rifle practice, over the Victorian
railways.” So whenever any unspeakable beer-
and-bluster warrrior wants to go anywhere on any
business, he first puts on his rifle and swagger,
and robs his country by travelling free. The
Commissioner shows that on such holidays as
Cup-day the fraud is at its worst.
“ Warree ” : The unemployed question is
reaching an acute stage on the Temora-Wyalong
railway-construction work. Crowds of men from
everywhere arrived on the ground in anticipation
of a job ; but the Owe’Sullivan ukased that pre-
ference should be given to men with electors’
rights for the local district. Then there was a
rush for “rights!” Next, a few trainloads of
Sydney unemployed, armed with ration-orders,
were dumped on to the spot. These had to get
work, but the others were not wanted. _ Conse-
quently, there are hundreds of men, many in actual
distress, begging for work, and the Owo’S. says he
cannot put any more on.

Jean Gerardy with his Stradivarius ’cello
opened to a poor house at Sydney Town Hall last
week. Musical stars are having a bad time here
financially. The Angel Israfel himself, who
must be something of a virtuoso as his heart-
strings are a lute, might draw a full Town Hall
these days, but just think of the Influence he
could command. Gerardy, coming on to the plat-
form, looks much younger than the flame-tinted
portraits of him that are stuck about the city •
leaving the platform he looks years older.’
Same with Max Schliiter, who, after he has played
a lot of his own vitality through his violin, looks
forty. Gerardy’s ears are set low like those of a
cocker spaniel, and are at an unusual angle : per-
haps this means ’cello genius. He has the true
musician s face with the full lips, deep-socketed
eyes, and prominent brows.
Miss Electa Gifford’s soprano is not as beautiful
as her figure. If it were like her shoulders and
neck it would be perfect. I like the little
olumber bong she gave for an encore—poppies
in it the swaying of the cradle, the rustle of the
mothers gown m the deepening dusk, Foote’s
Irish Folk Song” she also sang. Amy Castles
gave it to us frequently, but Miss Electa warbled
it with a difference. The three notes which Amy
used to sing so staccato Miss Electa lingered over
and let die slowly Her gown, around the
lower half of the skirt, was a fresco of silver
on black, and the artist’s intention was to
suggest a woodland pool by night, reeds and
grasses on the margin, big moths and glow-worms
on the surface. The intention did not mis-
carry. Herr Gottfried Galston, the pianist,
played Schumann’s Sonata in G Minor, which is
about as easy as Chinese metaphysics, and is
three-quarters of a mile long. So, after the
audience had listened intently for the first quarter
it began to cough and scrutinise each other’s
clothes, and wonder why musicians are never
bald. Ihe professional musicians present said
a , w “ ' vell P ] , a y ed - Herr Gottfried
pedals differently from other musicians whose feet
Ive noticed. He lifts his foot bodily, or, rather
tootily, quite level and very swiftly. Miss Gif-
ford s singing of the “ Mad Scene ” from “ Ham-
let at a later concert was really as perfect as her
ligure. And her gown was champagne shade with
woven gardens of pink roses.

An American lady visitor is just now paralysing
an Australian hotel with her splendid presence
and the gowns in which she drapes it. She is
very tall, very well-developed, about 40, with a
dead-white skin and black hair, and she sweeps
into the dining-hall with a Columbia-hails-Aus-
tralia sweep, trailing yards of crimson velvet and
priceless lace. One of her tailor-mades is a
cream cloth, embroidered with gold. But
the humor of the why of her being here:
‘‘ I spent two thousand pounds on clothes
for my wife in Paris,” explains the husband, “and
just then a relative of hers must go and die, and
throw her into mourning, so I said, ‘ Let us go to
Australia, where no one knows us, and your
clothes won’t be wasted.’”

1 heir Excellencies Rawson and daughter visited
the P.P.C. and D. Show which His Ex. opened,
to orchestral effects of cackling, crowing, trilling
and barking. . I should imagine that it was the
first show their Exes, have visited at which the
principals, and all the rank and file, wore their
own natural feathers and hair. In one case thecolor
was not real—anoriginally-whitefowlhadbeendyed
rose-pink and labelled the “Coronation Hen.” She
was very angry-looking a-nd was quite tired trying
to wash herself with the limited means at her
disposal—her beak and that silly little nib of a
tongue with which a hen is handicapped. The
Governor bought an Australian terrier, and Lady
and Miss patted many friendly bow-wows on the
organ of veneration. His Ex.’s father was a very
ooggy man, beagles being his fancy.
Two Missus and Mary Jane stories told by the
Missus : “I engaged a maid named Pearl, and as
I simply couldn’t ask a Pearl to fill the coal-
scuttle or holy-stone the doorstep I said, ‘I would
rather call you by some other name ; have you a
second one ? ’ ‘ Yes,’ replied the damsel, brightly,
my second name is Opal.’ So I stuck to Pearl.
At one time I had all but engaged a maid named
Hernnone, but upon asking her, ‘ Have you a
black dress, white caps and aprons?’ ‘Yes, I
have,’she replied acidly, ‘but I’m not going to
wear them. Ma didn’t christen me ’Ermione for
to wear a livery. 5 55
What is the idea of the hands-behind-the-oack
pose so much affected lately by large women and
thin women ? It suits only a very good, slender
figure, and does not harm the effect of a very
thin one, but for a large woman—and we have
such a lot of large young women, in every ball-
room—with very obvious hips, it is—well, there
is something about it that makes me think of a
whale s calf standing on its tail.
Writes my Brisbane correspondent:—
“To celebrate the first visit of Admiral Beau-
mont to Brisbane waters, Vice-Royalty enter-
tained at one of the old come-as-you-please, out-
door afternoon fetes. The Governor and his
sister-in-law, Miss Webb, received the deep curt-
seys of the initiated and the little pinchbeck bobs
of the novices, on the lawn. His Ex. cultivates
an altogether charming manner, seldom forgets
a face, and manoeuvres cleverly, when there is a
lapse of memory, to put on an old-friend air.
1 here is a great race for the epauletted attaches ;
two of Brisbane’s beauties annexed officers of the
last squadron, and ‘ there’s no saying where a

Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—

fookat, hides a soft heart under his buckram. In
the Town Hall, t’other evening, when he handed

hung down his head and winked the tears out of
his eyes when the ‘death-roll of honor’ was read
out, including the names of two little girls, one
aged o, the other 12, who had been drowned trying
to save baby-brothers. His own only chick is a
daughter, so no doubt pa has a soft spot for
youngsters of the ‘ coaxing sex,’ especially as one
of the gallant children was named ‘ Ethel ’ —his
own piccaniny’s title.

“Mr. and Mrs. Robert Harper had the luck to
travel to Vancouver by the same boat as the ex-
Gov.-General. Some of the smartest have never
forgiven the briskly-conversational Mrs. Harper

“ The nervousness of the politician at a ‘ social ’
ceremony is often thought to be surprising, though
it is a recognised characteristic of his twin-brother,
the stage actor. Puthim in his congenial position of
a political performer, and your M.P. has the con-
fidence, intrepidity, and self-assurance of an
Alexander. But introduce the petticoat element,
perfume the air with French floral essences, and
lo ! Alexander trembles at the knees ; his hands,
which rule continents, lose their confidence, and
his wits go wool-gathering. This was noticeable
at the Acting Gov.-Gen.’s swearing-in the other
week. Big, burly C. C. Kingston leaned on one
foot, then the other, and ended in a wide grin
at his own sense of awkwardness. The O’Connor
threw his Senatorial chest out bravely, but fid-
getted, and wore an air of painful restraint. And
so did most of the impatient orators of the House,
Alfred Deakin alone excepted. But what could
possibly trouble that self-contained young-old
man ? He has something of the parson in his
style, and the parson is always at his ease among
women.
“The M'Eacharn clan are forward with
Japanese furnishings and curios of all kinds after
their trip Eastward. How many more homes are
going to sacrifice their Louis XIII. cabinets and
Chippendale furniture for the lacquered and
much-gilded decorations of Japan? Out St.
Kilda way, and along the Brighton-road, ever so
many homes are filled with ‘ the latest novelties ’
from the land of the Cherry Blossom. The house-
holders deck themselves o’ mornings in kimonas
(the tinselled canvas ones); in the afternoon they
don (that is, when not going out or receiving) the
gorgeous, bird-colored, padded and quilted satin
kimonas—the most luxurious of fireside gowns.
Also, smart girls skewer their coiffures with Jap.
combs of all descriptions, decorate their wrists
with Jap. bangles, and their necks with Jap.
beads.
‘ The humor of the Japanese furnishing of our
social lights is that it delights in a profusion of
the cheap, the tawdry, and the glaring. It may
be the fashion of the Jap. bazaar in a cheap quar-
ter at Tokio, but certainly has no kinship with
the wonderfully-artistie interiors of the homes of
educated Japs. Those are marked by a restrained
aestheticism and a paucity of ornament, which, to
the European mind, looks like bareness. The Jap.
keeps his * curios ’ —not in his home, but in an
outhouse.
“ Celia Ghiloni, whose vulgar role as the
snarling * Old Dutch of the circus proprietor, is
a tremendous clnnb-down from lolanthing in
fairy splendours at her Majesty’s skittish show,
turns out in superfine style off the boards these
days. She lit upon a tea-room, the other day, in a
dream of violet glory. Her present part in the
‘ Circus Girl ’ is a strain on her voice, her wits,
and her presence of mind. The effort to sustain a
most unsuitable role is painfully visible, and
those who were rather taken with the Celia’s
fascinations, when, Aphrodite-like, she rose from
her watery bed, and waved her wand upon the
earth, squirm at the present sight of her in a
greasy velvet gown, a lop-sided island of a hat,
with flaring white feathers, and an Arriet-like
voice and harpy manner.
‘May Beatty tells interviewers and friends
that she feels sorry Melbourne gets but a vulgar
, impression of ner in the present performance.
Carmenita, ’ she says, was deep-down comedy
enough, but ‘ Fi-Fi’ is lower still, and blithesome
May prays for a really nice part to set her on
refined heights again. "Clowns—male or female-
all alike, want to play Hamlet.
“ While May Beatty’s boldness and brightness
nave grown apace with the nights of the Chinese
Honeymoon, Josephine Stanton languishes and
droops till her tired-feeling becomes positively
infectious. Even her pet warble about the ‘ Dainty
Darling Dimple ’ has waned in expression and
range. As the Princess, she bears a passing
resemblance to Ada Willoughby in the last
jewelled pantomime, but she gave us all Ada’s
effortless performances and none of the little
woman’s winsomeness. There was an indefinable
charm about Ada’s treacled tootle of ‘ My Daddy’s
a Gentleman,’ and as Josephine Stanton really
boasts a dainty, darling dimple of her own, she
should put some of its charm into her catchy
warble.
“Talking of the amateur ‘Runaway Girl per-
formance (and in stage-struck circles they are
still talking about it), the name-part was not quite
unsuccessfully grappled with by a Sandring-
ham lady, a sweet-voiced little thing. Miss
Abbott, who took Carrie Moore’s part, is also a
Sandringham product, a youngster of 17 or there-
abouts, and the best little dancer in a crowd whose
darlcing was a respectable all-round apology for
their lack of sound. Some of them couldn’t even
talk audibly. Miss L. Lugg, the pretty daughter
of a dentist, reproduced Grace Palotta on a
smaller scale, even to the cracked note m her
voice, and the accent. The notion of an imitation
Palotta straining after the foreign accent of the
original in this very English character is
surely the funniest notion that ever possessed an
amateur mummer mind.
“ Some amateur mummers scored conquests in
a recent musical show, and the engagement of one
of the leading brunettes is a result of her skittish
stage strut—in abbreviated skirts. On the whole,
the revelation afforded by the brief petticoats was
a disenchanting one, and the discovery was made
of famine calves in nearly every instance. The
only one of the co. with substantial supports used
to flaunt them before the other girls behind the
scenes, and leave the owners of the frail props in
high dudgeon. This is feminine nature all over—
vanity in excess matched by envy in excess.
“The path of a recent amateur Society show
was strewn with many thorns. The dress com-
petition was a sharp one. The frock of one dainty
Miss cost 18 guineas. This was a bad precedent,
for another good member with many physical
points was prevented wearing a serviceable but
cheap costume, on the score of incongruity.
Frocks were to be merely decorative, not practi-
cal, and all the pretty show-girls of the co. had to
scale the heights of extravagance, just because the
wealthiest girl had started off with the most
luxurious and useless chiffons.
A charmer who showed most expensive under-
frills was giving her wed ding-trousseau treasures
a preliminary airing. How does this strike the
sentimentalist ?
“Moral philosophers advance a reason for a
certain comic opera girleen, of no parts but what
the daintiest dressing gives her, securing all the
laurels of a big drum-banging show. They say
the tricky one has all her Johnnies, like so many
poached eggs, on toast, and delays her choice of
the lot to secure greater manifestations of worship
from the sighing swains who struggle for a mono-
poly of her smiles and affections.
“ ‘ Novel’ teas have broken out again badly in
Melbourne. Judge Molesworth’s wife negotiated
one the other day, at which Capt. Browning, the
tootling aide of Gov. Clarke, scored a point or
two. The chubby warrior appeared with a long
list of Argus birth-notices pinned to his coat.
All the girls stood round him, blushing and
giggling, and afraid to venture guesses over such
a delicate matter, till one rushed in with ‘The
Newcomes.’ She had guessed right the very first
time.
“Electa Gifford—the songstress of Gerardy’s
concert party—tells an interviewer that, while
visiting Samoa, she was asked by a native chief if
she and a girl companion were married. On
answering in the negative, the chief cordially and
instantly invited the two charmers to pitch their
tents in sunny Samoa. “He really wanted us to
stay,” ingenuously added the radiant Electa.
“The marriage of Miss Alice Climpton and Mr.
Clive Macartney, at St. Thomas’, Essendon, last
Wednesday, drew all the feminine flutterers of the
N. W. district. The bride is a daughter of one of
Melbourne’s rich millers, and the incidental splen-
dour was up to the high-water mark of fashion.
The pearl and ivory raiment revelled in mountings
of good lace and chiffon. Three good-figured brides-
maids showed how effectively ficelle lace shows
up on ivory silk foundations. A few blush-pink
roses peeped forth from dainty toques. Of course
the cascade dip of lace at the back was a feature of
each chic toque. All the guests sported millinery
brought up-to-date by streaming velvet ends down
the back.
“ A certain bright little comedienne does Col-
lins-st. occasionally with a wealthy American
resident whom the Society girls have failed season
after season to bait. The Johnnies of The Block
think it ‘ a case.’
“ Senator O’Connor took an affectionate farewell
of Acting-Prime Minister Deakin at the closing of
the Senate’s shutters for a month. Often o’ fine
mornings the Sydney Ajax takes a brisk walk to
South Yarra, there to enjoy his coffee-roll with
Alfred the Didactic, and chat over the day’s pro-
gramme. No cobwebs on their brains at 8 a.m.—
light consumers of victuals and viands, they revel
in sound hearts and strong nerves, and are equal,
either of them, to 16 hours’ work at a stretch,
allowing an interval for a tray-snack.
“There were ructions in a Collins-street tea-
room, t’other day, over the introduction to ‘the
staff ’of an ex-housemaid. A customer recognised
the Hebe as an old servant, and indignantly scat-
tered the news among the smart graces of the
tray. They formed a deputation to the pro-
prietress, declaring their disgust and refusal to
work with a ‘ menial ’; and, although she bore an
excellent character, she had to march off. Which
beautifully illustrates the chance that any woman,
however refined, has of rising in Society if ever
she commits The One Unpardonable Sin —that of
becoming a ‘slavey.’ That, after all, is the only
unpardonable sin in a woman.
“Talking of music. The washer-lady, the
mangier, and the multitude of stitching sisters
move Heaven and earth to have themselves or
their bairns taught the pianoforte. There is one
story in robust circulation to the effect that a
serving- woman offered laundry assistance in return
for the five-finger exercise on the Ronisch. That
about takes the palm. But everyone has personal
experience of the stitching-she who offers two
days’ needle-dodging a week in return for music-
lessons ; and the palmist, the pedagogue in petti-
coats, and the manicurist, who offer their services
for ‘ Nelly Bly ’ tunings and chromatic scalings.
An advt. in the Argus, the other day, held out
the enticing offer of poker-instruction in return
for music-lessons.

the capitals is run by high-collared youths, who
do the pining-in of corsage sprays and all that
sort of thing.

Next to the Insurance fiend ranks the
Typewriter man for cast iron nerve and per-
severance. A man has only to breathe that he is
going in for a machine when the salesmen swarm
round him like flies over beef. Brown will tell
him that a machine printing with a shift for
capital letters is only used by persons with weak
intellects, and that if he buys a double keyboard
instrument his troubles are for ever at rest.
Smith, however, scoffs at the double keyboard
notion and prophesies disaster of the most awful
description to the unlucky possessor. Then up
comes Green with a pad machine and anathema-
tizes all machines printing from a ribbon. The
bewildered buyer, driven to distraction, curses each
and all. The safe and comfortable course is to deal
with a house offering a variety of each principle ;
and anxious to give the buyer the type of machine
best suited to his special wants. The United
Typewriter and Supplies Go. of Sydney,
Melbourne and Perth sells the Yost, New Century
and Densmore, shift key, double keyboard and
pad machine, and they give their advice free, and
also a free trial of each kind of machine.*

PERSONAL ITEMS.
This time the Coronation is fixed for
Saturday. No more Friday luck !
The Bulletin regrets to hear it reported
that its old friend Gentleman One is missing with
the trust-money again.
Acting-Governor-General Lord Tennyson
completes hi® first half-century of life on Tuesday,
Aug. 12; Lord Ranfurly, Governor of M.L., will
be 46 two days later.
Colonel Tom Price, at just on 60 years of
age, goes to Queensland as Commandant, when
several long resident officials of Queensland are
getting the run for being at or about that period
of life.
The Tempe (N.S.W.) Refuge for Women is
rather proud of its oldest charge. When a girl of
16 she was an inmate of the original institution,
and now, at 85, the giddy lady again seeks its
shelter.
Apropos of a recent parsonical debacle,
something always happens to the man who abuses
Ihe Bulletin. That something also always hap-
pens to everybody is likewise a fact, but we ignore
that so as to argue like a parson in this case.
Two N.S.W. Supreme Court Judges
(Messrs. Cohen and Bring), on occasion, wear
hard-hitter hats. But you never saw His Honor
Mr. Justice G. B. Simpson in a hard-hitter hat,
any more than you ever saw him wearing a pipe
in his mOuth in the street.
Levy, M.L.A. for Fitzroy (Sydney), has
won an Australian record. Twenty-two motions
“ that he be no longer heard ” were carried in one
night’s sitting of the Leg. Assembly. He was
thus bowled out for two whole innings in one
night—on a wet wicket, though.
The estate of the deceased Westralian
Premier Leake shows liabilities, £14,714 ; assets,
£16,567; balance, which will probably be swal-
lowed up by realisation expenses, £1853. Once
again, politics don’t pay. A dead politician’s
chief asset is generally his widow.
Apropos President Baker’s wig portrait,
and the S.A. Leg. Council’s desire to have the
horse hair painted out, the portrait of Chief
Justice Dowling, in N.S.W. Supreme Court
building, originally had a wig, which was painted
out, as can be seen on closely scrutinising the
picture.
Interesting for the Adelaide unemployed,
whose promised winter employment was one of
the baits used by the Snow tramway crowd, to
know that all plans have yet to be made, and that,
meanwhile, Mr. Snow, who is represented as
having many private concerns, has set out for the
Harz mountains, in Germany.
Acting-Federal Premier Deakin will be
46 on Sunday, Aug. 3; Leader of the Senate
O Connor’s birthday is Monday, Aug. 4; “Rolf
Bold re wood ” will be 76 and Rep. Isaacs 47 on
Wednesday, Aug. 6; and Treasurer George
Turner will be 51 on Friday, Aug. 8, Rep.
Poynton being 49 on same day.
Mr. Fuller, M.H.R., who was lately bask-
ing in the moonshine of G. H. Reid’s notoriety as
he wobbled around Victoria, was worried at every
meeting by hairy and persistent individuals, who
requested George to “sit down an’ give th’ pup a
show.” Fuller got to be known all over the State
as “ole Gawge Reid’s dry dawg.”
Just as John Haynes was shaping, in
N.S.W., for another go at his ill-starred Capital
Punishment Abolition Bill came the murder of
Constable Guilfoyle. There really ought to be
an Act to prevent John Haynes proposing a Bill
for the abolition of capital punishment, on the
ground that he thereby promotes murders.
A MAN WITH AN EVIL
STAR.
“Some men are awfully unfortunate. You re-
member Smith whose wife died last year?”
“ Yes.” "Well, he’s got married again.”
New York Herald says that King Edward
and Salisbury don’t speak now owing to the
latter’s refusal to recommend Ernest Cassell for a
peerage. Last year a similar request was refused,
but Cassell’s promotion was again demanded by
the King on the ground of his recent munificent
charitable gift for a consumptive hospital.
It was actually cabled to Australia that
J. Chamberlain had sent a cheque for 50 guineas
to the hospital in which he was treated after having
been run down by a pro-Boer cab-horse. This
exhibit is evidently meant as a startling proof of
King Brum.’s munificence, but it reads like the
meanest bit of skite that ever travelled over a tele-
graph wire. What a crawler is the cable-man !
Brunton Stephens’ description (in a letter
to Geo. Essex-Evans) of his method of poetic
production :
I produced another stanza (of “ Convict Once ”) last
night. Sydney Smith hit off my case in describing
Rogers. “ When Rogers is delivered of a couplet he is
kept in bed, and straw is laid on the street, and the
answer to enquirers is that he is doing as well as can be
expected.” That’s me to aT,or to a straw.
Apropos of John Longstaff’s luck in his
commission to paint the King, supplemented by
an order for a portrait of Queen Alexandra. By
the Queen’s expressed wish the Australian was
brought to a Court at Buckingham Palace the
other night, and a week after the event J.L. had
not recovered from the surprise of seeing himself
in a black velvet knickerbocker suit and wearing
a sword.
“ William Lewis” writes :
Dear Bulletin, —You jeer at “Sir” John Jenkins, but
how about Sir Hiram Maxim? To my mind, no more
wildly funny combination has ever been conceived.
What a name for a comic opera ! How it conjures up
recollections of Sam Slick, wooden nutmegs, pork-and-
beans, buck-wheat cakes, and nasal twang 1 Sir Hiram
Maxim, Sir David Harum, Sir John Jenkins ! Shade of
Hosea Biglow ! where shall we find to-day the American
democrat of Lowell’s verse ?
The man arrested at Broken Hill charged
with the big opal mail-robbery is a “ whip ” of
fine busbmanship and unsurpassed know-
ledge of the country. He has been in the
backblocks for years—right back into the
days when the Barrier was the favorite
refuge of the hard-pressed. When the
miner and more or less civilisation came
along he spent most of the years crossing
mobs of moving cattle over the Darling
at Wilcannia. At that risky business he
certainly hadn’t an equal in Australia,
and probably hadn’t one anywhere else.
Mrs. Campbell Praed, in her forthcoming
book of Queensland reminiscences, should be able
to describe some exciting scenes. Her young
married life was spent on Curtis Island, and squat-
ting in those days yielded sensations. She is a
Murray Prior, and her kith and kin are still rooted
there. Her uncle, by marriage—Mackworth
Praed—won the Cambridge prize poem on Aus-
tralia over the head of W. C. Wentworth, of
Sydney.
Dr. Kennion, ex-Bishop of Adelaide and
now Bishop of Bath and Wells, is to be right-hand
supporter of the King as he walks up the aisle of
Westminster Abbey on the day of his Coronation
(Aug. 9). It is difficult to disagree with the
doctors-in-charge at this distance ; but the Aus-
tralian public is inclined to look upon this early
date for the Coronation as an injudicious rushing
of things, and to think that King Ned will need a
deal of support on the day.
In Federal politics George Turner main-
tains his State reputation as a grafter ; the clerks
in his department loudly bewail their boss’s insane
craving for business. Turner’s frugal mind, how-
ever, got quite a jolt when a bill recently came in
for tea-money for the hands he had kept late at
work. In his passion for economy, Treasurer
Turner may yet introduce the “ crib ” system into
his department, and thus make cold, proud, high-
collared civil-servants carry to the office billies and
nose-bags.
A statue inaugurated at Melbourne to
William Clarke ! Who’s he ? Who knows—
without a moment’s reflection, on tagging on the
“Sir W. J. , Bart.” The first statue that
Melbourne requires is to William Clarke ! So be
it. He almost chums with Khartoum Gordon,
another gentleman to whom Melbourne was bluffed
into rushing up a memorial. If you ask a William
Clarkite why the statue goes up, he will of course
reply : “Well, you see, Clarke —the Greatest One
—was so rapidly sinking into oblivion that some-
thing had to be done.” A monument to Higin-
botham would be superfluous.
“We have had Seddon, of Maoriland*
over here,” writes a S’African correspondent
to The Bulletin-
“He came to Vere-
reeniging and ad-
dressed the M.L.
troops. I was not
much impressed. He
is very noisy and
very frothy. The
words, ‘ I seldom
make a mistake’
were used frequently
in his speech. He
went with the flood-
tide in the martial
business, and is reap-
ing his reward. He
talks at the people,
not to them; and
has kindly fixed the future
policy of this country. He freely
offered to terminate the war at
any hour if the authorities chose
to follow his advice. I wish he
could have talked with Botha,
De la Rey, De Wet, and Co.
He is full of modesty. His
oratory ranged from Maoriland
mutton to the unity of the Empire. His style is
most grandiloquent, no matter what his subject
may be. . . . Yet in Johannesburg he was very
well received.”
His Majesty King Thomas Fall-off Brassey
at the Royal Colonial Institute, 11th March, ’O2 :
I have undying' recollections of the Hinchinbrook Pas-
sage, and my visit extended to the extremest point of
Thursday Island. Many of my Victorian subjects were
deeply interested in Queensland properties, and I admired
greatly the spirit of enterprise, &c., &c.
His Majesty possesses something else which is
undying—undying impudence. Memo : Why
spell his name with an “ e ” ?
Justice Pring, latest accession to N.S.W.
Supreme Court, dealing with an appeal by the
Railway Commissioners for a new trial, dropped
softly on his brother Judges with the remark :
Speaking for himself he thought juries were better
qualified to decide matters of this kind than Judges could
possibly be. Juries were, of course, not infallible, and
sometimes made mistakes, but upon the whole, and
especially in cases of this nature, they were more likely
to do justice than a Judge, whose mind to a certain
extent was narrowed by legal technicalities. »
“ THE BLIND SINGER”: A MELBOURNE
STREET SKETCH.
“ I arise from Dreams of Thee.”
Robert Richards, of Riverstone (N.5.W.),«
who died too young (19/7/’O2), was one of the best
and straightest of the meat and horse men in Aus-
tralia. Had he given himself wholly to one thing
or the other he must have gone far. His enter-
prise and practical knowledge, associated with
thorough scientific management, would have
placed the whole home and export business in
meat on a new basis. But he was content with
Riverstone, and didn’t get much beyond plater
business in racing.
A fine stamp of enthusiast died the other
day in Walgett hospital after a tree had fallen
on him —Jack O’Brien, bush-workers’ organiser.
With 255. in his pocket (his own and last 255.)
and a bike and a blanket, he set out two months
before last N.S.W. general election to get into
every little back camp in the vast and droughty
Wentworth electorate in the interests of the
Labor man. He neither hoped for nor received a
Eenny for that labor, or for other of equal value
e had done before.
Charley Warne, a full-blooded aborigine,
died t’other day at Kempsey (N.S.W.), at the
reputed age of “at least 118 years ” ! Half-a-
dozen aborigines, aged “at least” 110 to 125,
have died in the last two years. This mythical
longevity takes rise from the fact that no nigger
is certain of his real age, and from the equally
pertinent circumstance that at 70 or thereabouts
he withers and wrinkles up so remarkably as to
look a centenarian. If the just-departed patri-
arch was anyway near his alleged age, he was a
boy of four when Gov. Phillip put up his first
tent on the shores of Sydney Cove.
A team of veteran Boers would have given
great interest to the competition for the Kolapore
Cup. Grey-headed, coffee-drinking, phlegmatic
marksmen of the old school, that lay at ease behind
cover, and picked off the advancing rooineks,
one at a time. Shooting is a sport in which
excellence-cum-sobriety has a long life. Fergusson,
one of the victorious Australian team at Bisley
last week, was the winner of the Queen's Prize at
Wimbledon 22 years ago. Three other members
of the Eight are old enough to have sons as big as
themselves, and their success suggests that the
picture of the old man Boer and his deadly rifle is
not over-colored.
B. E. Minns’ many friends on this side
will be glad to hear that, though still under con-
stant surgical treatment, the New Englander’s
health is distinctly on the up-grade. “I had an
hour’s chloroform three weeks ago,” he writes in
his latest letter, “and am due lor another soon.
The surgeon is trying to mend the hole he had to
make in my side ; I fancy he’ll make a job of it
in one more go. I hate the idea of an operation,
but am never sick after it, unlike most people. . . .
Am kept busy, notwithstanding that, for nearly
four years now, I have had to be content with
what the editors here choose to send ; however, I
hope to be in a condition to ‘ buzz ’ round more
soon.”
Little Isidor Lissner, who died the other
day, was one of the most genial and popular souls
who ever stepped into the Q. Parliament, Came
to Bendigo in the fifties, migrated to Maoriland,
and finally returned to Australia to help open up
Ch. Towers, in ’73. Returned to Parliament for the
Kennedy, he was finally made MTlwraith’s Minis-
ter for Mines prior to ’93. Labor beat him
in ’93, and though subsequently returned for
Cairns his political mana was gone. Was fond of
dubbing himself “A bad speaker but a good
Lissner.” An old Parliamentary anecdote repre-
sents Lissner- as saying concerning certain kid-
napping outrages, “they vould make your hair
cold and your blood shtand on end.”
The Baronet Clarke statue stands just at
the entrance of Melbourne Treasury gardens.
Poor old S’William’ used to pass through those
gates early on most mornings in the week, making
a bee-line for the Masonic Club and a stiff
whisky-and-soda. From the Club to St. James’
Buildings, thence to the bank ; Australian Club
afterwards and home to Cliveden or Sunbury early
in the afternoon. Nothing about him to suggest
apotheosis or statue-raising. Just kindly and con-
siderate management of inherited wealth and
consumption of as much liquor as could be digested.
A tablet in the bank would have been right
enough, for in the crash of ’93 the baronet took all
the responsibilities of one bank and a big share of
two others on his shoulder without a murmur.
The shoulders were well padded—revenues for
decades averaged over £150,000 a year. Porters
and guards on the Sunbury line say the statue
ought to have been on the station. The Bart, was
a model to all who entered there. “Shaky as he
might be, said old Mac., “his fingers could always
find the road to the right pocket and pick out half
a quid when they got there. A man of that sort
is lit for a station anywhere.”
A look-back at the most shameful time in
the police, history of Australia does not make
ghosts of Dan Kelly and Steve Hart, said still to
be in the flesh. There never was sure evidence
that either of the two was killed. Father Gibney,
of Perth, was the only one game to enter the
house after it had been fired. He saw two bodies,
and by touching ascertained that both were dead.
After the fire the skulls and parts of the trunks
only were found. The armour they had worn was
in the same room, and the supposition was that they
had removed it and shot one another. Ned Kelly
was of opinion that they were not game for that sorb
of thing. And lie himself, as is well known, gob -
out, and could have got clear after the police sur-
rounded the place. His return was about the
toughest instance of matehood in bush history.
There were upwards of 30 people in the house
throughout the “siege.” Three were shot, and
the manner of their shooting is well known, bub
about the death of the two outlaws, if they died,
there has always been silence and mystery. There
was no very accurate tally kept of those who
entered the house the day arid night before.
At a Melb. annual Orange demonstration,
the other day, one speaker high in lodge authority
announced the death of Johnston, M.P. (“Johns-
ton of Ballykilbeg ”) Grand Orange boss in Great
Britain. “Sad news awaits us to-day,” said he ;
“ we have received a cable message announcing
the death of one of the leading members of our
institution—a man whom we have all respected
for years, whose works have endeared him to every
member throughout the world—l refer to” (he
lifted a scrap of paper, tried to puzzle out the
name scribbled on it, and after a lapse of a couple
of seconds said) “I refer to Mr. Johnston—Mr.
Johnston of —er —of ” (another pause and another
scrutiny of the paper) “of Killambeg, Ireland.
Mr. Johnston occupied a seat in the House of
Commons for—er- —more than ” (the paper was
again raised) “for 40 years. It is a great blow
when a gentleman so well-known as Mr. —er —er —
Johnston is tiken from our midst.” Nobody
laughed. Anybody who laughs at an Orange
meeting is at once identified as an emissary of the
Pope.

appointment, and, of course, the public believes it.

The J.P. again. “ Boyang ” writes :
Being delayed, t’other day, in a small N.Q. town, I
strolled into the local court-house and found that a Chow
was being sued for debt and that a lively argument was
proceeding between one of the magistrates and plaintiff’s
lawyer, who objected to the J. P.’s presence on the Bench
on the ground that he was an interested party. Finally
the J.P. unwillingly left the Bench, but promptly took
* ,,s Place at the lawyer’s table as advocate for the de-
fendant Chow! Yet those present didn’t appear to see
anything unusual in the occurrence.

11. M. Pibfc gives S.M. Herald interesting
facts about sheep-feeding in the drought. The
work on Burrawang is prob ibly the biggest of the
sort ever attempted. More than 100,000 sheep
are hand-fed at a cost of per week each.
They would about eat their heads off at that rate
in six months. But how would it be if they were
fed on station-made ensilage, instead of hay at
£6 a ton and wheat at 4s. Cd. a bushel ? The
daily consumption now is 23 tons of hay and same*
of wheat. The wheat would be equal to four
times the quantity of hay. For 100,000 sheep, say
400,0001 b. of ensilage a day. On one big A r ic.
farm ensilage has been put down at less than ss. a
ton and more than 10 tons to the acre. A 5000-
acre farm, on that basis, would give a food supply
for 250 days at a cost of <£12,500 (ton reckoned at
20001 b.). This year, taking Mr. Pitt’s figures as
correct, it will cost Burrawang a trifle over
£OO,OOO for six months.

hundreds of cows are kept in good milking condi-
tion by being fed on the despised prickly pear.
At Daandine station (S.Q.) the cattle are formed
into camps of 400 and prickly pear is fed to them
in troughs. There are six tanks to each camp, and
three men employed at the fires. No water

Died recently at Mareeba (N.Q.), Annie
Doyle (better known as “ Red Jack ”) who had
f >r years been well-known in out-back Australia.
Constantly travelling and never stopping long in
one place, there is hardly a far-out township or bad
track in Queensland that she hadn’t visited. As
a rule “Jack ” had a decent horse or two and was
ever eager to make a match. She made one once
at Cloncurry, with a Chinaman, for £10 —one mile
—owners up. The race came off on a Sunday—all
Cloncurry attending. It was a very close thing,
and in the struggle for home “ Jack’s ” hair (red —
wh )nce “Red Jack”) came down; ditto the
Ch nv’s pigtail. The lady won by a bare half-
length. She was a good “bushman,” and would
tackle any sort of work for a spell—her fancy jobs
being droving and cooking. Didn’t drink, but
could awear like a bullocky.

‘ ‘ Mor ” : Re rabbits climbing over netting-
wire (8., 28/6/’02): Between Orange and Welling-
ton (N.S.W.) once, I saw a rabbit running along a
wire netting fence, trying to get through. Came
to a cow lying alongside fence, and jumped on to
her. Fence was still too high, so bunny set to
scratching the cow, which thereupon got up, and
enabled him to top the fence. [Next!]

A true yarn of a “tight, place ” comes
from Albury (N.S.W.). A squatter shot at a wild
bull and missed. The bull came over in great
haste to ask the squatter what he meant by it,
and the latter, seeing the case was urgent, got off
his horse, and climbed up a tree. As he did so lie
dropped his gun.. In climbing up the tree his
foot got jammed in the fork of a branch, and lie
was left there. a prisoner. The bull then prodded
the horse which had its bridle ent tngled in a
branch, and finally it stuck its horn through the
bridle and left, taking the horse with it. The
squatter had an impression that he might leave
his bones up the tree, but after a while he got free
and limped after the bull, which he demolished
with a second shot. Next day, by way of cele-
brating his escape, he accidentally cut off two of
his toes with an axe, and as he had just recovered
from an attack of blood-poisoning when he met
the bull, he doesn’t seem, taking things all round,
to be a lucky pastoralist.
• * * *
“ Traveller” writes from Queensland aboyt
the prospects of that State’s “ great natural in
dustries ” :
This country is in a bad way. The dairymen from
Brisbane to Toowoomba are practically wiped out; rem-
nants of once-numerous herds still lingering on upon
straw-chaff-cum-molasses, prickly-pear and boiled sugar-
cane. If rain does not come within the next two months
there won’t be a beast left in this district, because it will
be impossible to drive them to and fro for water. The
wretched skeletons stand in the same place all day long
waiting to die. A man at Rosewood, who six months ago
had 100 cows, has now but 25,14 having died in one night.
Another, after 22 years’ farming, has now nothing to show
for it but a mortgage ! Cookies who liaoc any hay and
straw are selling at £lO and £4 per ton respectively, and
letting their own cattle take their chance.
From Ipswich to Toowoomba, thence to Pittsworth,
Leyburn, Inglewood to Goondiwindi, the country is
a mere desert of dust, misery and dying cattle.
Horses follow you and whinny pitifully ; one to which I
gave a “ johnny ” followed me for five miles. The trees
are dying, and dead marsupials are everywhere ; the
only thing that appears to flourish being the crow —which
seems to revel in all the horror. Near Goondiwindi I
met a man with 4000 sheep out of 40,000 with which he
had left Deniliquin (N.S.W.). He had sold 3000 en route,
but the other 33,000 had perished 1
Teams are now making for the stations laden with
straw chaff, but without green stuff the cattle must die,
as this kind of tucker forms a ball in the stomach as hard
as iron. With no crops this year an I the cattle all dead,
the farmers of this part of Queen hand have an awful timr
fccf:- v diem. /
“Nobaii” : I
had got into camp
and was hobbling
out my horses when
a man came up,
wearing three coats
and two hats, one
over the other.
“Yell, I tink I
camp over dere,” he
said, pointing to a
dead leaning box-
tree. He made a
large fire on the
leaning side of the
tree, boiled his billy
and had a feed.
Then he made a
shakedown of leaves
just where the trunk
of the tree would be
likely to fall, as the
fire burnt through
it. Not wishing to
be delayed on an in-
quest I pointed this
out, and after much
persuasion he shift-
ed over towards
where I was camp-
ed. During the night
the tree fell with a fearful crash. I got up with a
start and found the other chap dancing round the
fire and clapping his hands like a Salvarmy cap-
tain. Then I noticed that the tree had fallen
within a foot of where he had been sleeping,
“You shifted back, after all,” I exclaimed.
“Yes,” he answered. “You tink you get me
over near you und rob me. I sleep vere I like.”
I cleared out as soon as I could catch my horses. I
afterwards heard that the crank was found boiling
his 'billy on the floor of a woolshed. The fire had
not quite eaten through the boards.
* » * «
If there is one class who should more than
another regret the happening of “tragedies” it
is the man who carries his swag. If one such
shows up in the affected district while the hue-
and-cry is on, he at once becomes the cynosure of
all eyes; weak-minded people rush inside at sight
of him and barricade the doors, while other luna-
tics fly to the telephone and call the police in
frenzied, broken gasps. He is dogged and way-
laid and variously persecuted at every turn; and
while innocently boiling his billy under a gum-
tree he is suddenly surprised by a rush of bobbies,
“laid on” by idiotic residents, who cover him
with guns and roar at him to “bail up.” The
police cause might be materially helped if some
hoodlums could develop enough common sense to
reason that a man is not necessarily a scoundrel
because he carries a swag.

“Ham”: Re a par. (8., 28/6/’O2). I
have known men kill a beast, and after cutting
off the required quantity of beef, effectively hide
the carcase thus : First, the defunct is buried in
the usual way; then a second hole is dug close by,
presumably for prospecting purposes, the dirt
being thrown carelessly over the place of the first
hole, thus giving the locale merely the appearance
of an abandoned alluvial shaft.

At last sitting of Condobolin (N.S.W.)
local Land Board Mr. E. M. Y. Curr, a squatter,
/ties, called by the Crown to give evidence as to the
capital value of certain lands which the Board was
asked to appraise, and on which the applicants
(selectors) thought the Crown placed too high a
value. Mr. Curr founded his estimate on the
basis of having one man and a boy to look after
an area sufficient (in that district) to carry 3000
sheep—about 9000 acres. He even limited the
estimate to two rations, stating that most of the
selectors round him were running their farms by
themselves assisted by a boy at about 10s. per
week. Several well-known graziers afterwards got
together and drew up the following programme of
a day’s work of the 10s. boy :
“ The Boy ” on our selection of 9000 acres.
1. Arrives at our selection
2. Goes to bed at eight o’clock
3. Rises at 3 a.m.
3.30 a.m. 4. Runs in the horses (one wild)
3.45 ~ 5. Feeds the horses
4 ~ 6. Saddles the boss’s horse
4.30 „ 7. Lights the fire
5 ~ 8. Milks the cows
5.15 ~ 9. Sets the milk
6.35 „ 10. Prepares breakfast (cooks, &c.)
7.5 „ 11. Has breakfast (standing)
7.20 „ 12. Feeds the fowls
7.35 ~ 13. Gathers the eggs
7.50 „ 14. Feeds the cows
8.5 „ 15. Takes yesterday’s milk to pigs
8.45 ~ 16. Stirs up broody hens to make room for
♦ laying ones
9 „ 17. Takes calves away from cows
9.15 ~ 18. Feeds cats and dogs
9.30 ~ 19. Sharpens odd tools required
10 ~ 20. Outs firewood
10.15 ~ 21. Feeds poddy calf
10.45 ~ 22. Sweeps out the house, scrubbing it once
a week
11 „ 23. Makes the beds, &c.
12.15 ~ 24. Prepares dinner
12.30 ~ 25. Feeds horses and unsaddles horses
12.45 „ 26. Has dinner (standing again)
1 p.m. 27. Banks up fire before going out on run
1.15 „ 28. Saddles horses
1.20 ~ 29. Starts feed pigs and fowls, when “ boss ”
orders him to protect northern boundary
fence from bush fire on a neighbour’s, six
miles away, with strong wind blowing in
direction of homestead
8 „ 80. Returns home after assisting in stopping
bush fire
8.15 ~ 81. Feeds pigs, fowls, &c.
8.30 „ 32. Feeds horses and unsaddles them
9 ~ 33. Milks cows
9.15 ~ 34. Sets milk
9.30 ~ 35. Makes butter
9.45 ~ 36. Runs in the calves
10.15 „ 37. Prepares tea
11.45 „ 38. Has tea (still standing)
12 ~ 39. Washes up tea utensils
1 a.m. 40. Kills pig and cuts it up
2 „ 41. Kills sheep and cuts it up
2.15 ~ 42. Looks to horses
2.30 ~ 43. Discusses matter of Going on the Land with
the boss, and the boss eventually per-
suades him, at 2.46 a.m., to form a Union.
After Union has been formed, and again
dissolved, on the boss’s resolution, finds
it is time to milk the cows.
44. In shearing time, as he has to muster
sheep for shearing, pick-up, wool-sort,
roll, press, keep pens full, act as tar-boy,
sew up bales, brand bales, keep returns,
return shorn sheep to paddock, load
waggon, take wool to railway station,
cook for three, &c., we have allowed 34
hours a day in order to give him neces-
sary time to complete his duties in a day.

“ M'unga ” : Re “ Burketown citizen’s”
statements (8., 14/6/’O2). An exposure of treat-
ment the aborigines have received and are still
receiving would “stagger humanity”; but no
man really “in the know’’ would dare to show
things up. In ’93, a station-manager in W.Q.
brutally murdered a black because he thought
the man was trying to entice away one of his (the
M.’s) “stud” gins. At the request of an eye-
witness (who could not write), I wrote to the
district inspector of police, giving all particulars.
The inspector, however, merely sent my letter
back to the manager, with the result that I had
to clear out of the neighborhood. As for “Burke-
town Citizen’s ” squatter who boasted of riding
down aboriginal men and outraging the women —
that, in some parts, is quite common. One squatter
in his youth invariably cut the women’s throats
afterwards, and the only thing that this brute
cared for was a dog that acted similarly with
female dingoes. A lovely pair 1

“ GS-.T.” : That “ Ragged Thirteen ” yarn
(“6xB” B. 7/6/’O2) corresponds in Queensland
with the Speewah wool-shed “pitch” of N.S.W.
It is used to gull the town bushman, and dates
from the time of Kimberley rush. In ’B3 or ’8413
men started overland from W.Q, with a few
horses and very little capital but plenty of what
they called “knocker.” At each station they
struck they demanded rations, and if police had
been plentiful in that part they would never have
reached. Kimberley. One of them afterwards made
a big rise at Coolgardie, and would not to-day
care m uch to be reminded of the incidents of that
trip.

“ Wilgar” : Re horses eating sheep-dags

eaters. Some people who keep a cow make no
provision for her maintenance, and much that is
fit only, for the manure-heap thus ultimately
resolves itself into “ fresh milk ! ”

Kuppers Elberfelcl Lager.
A Melbourne pressman, during the Royal
visit, went to tlie transport to interview one of
our dusky “fellow-subjects,” who was alleged to
be the crack rider of all India. The scribe asked
a gorgeous be-whiskered nigger on the deck if he
could direct him to Khan Mahomed. “Cer-
tainly,” was the reply; and, while seeking their
man, the suave darkie talked affably to the re-
porter in perfect English, telling him all about
their trip, and the high appreciation they felt for
Australia. Then suddenly he drew the pressman
aside, and said: “Look here; if you are writing
anything about us, I would feel obliged if you
would say that Jumbo Singh is the handsomest
man in the whole procession. I’m Jumbo Singh,
you know ; and I’d like you also to comment par-
ticularly upon my uniform. This is all real gold ;
this embroidery cost 100 rupees, and this scarf is
one of only five of the sort in all India.” “ But,”
ventured the rep., “aren’t the rest dressed like
you ? ” “Not a bit of it,” said the other. “ Their
uniforms are only bad imitations of mine—didn’t
cost a quarter a 3. much ! This belt, now, is a
masterpiece of Indian art ; but don’t forget to say
that it is the sole property of Jemadar Jumbo
Singh. Then just look at these shoes! ”
“But,” interrupted the reporter, “ I must really
get hold of Mahomed Khan.” “What do you
want him for?” asked the other. “Well, I
believe he’s the best rider in the Indian army,”
ventured the newspaper-man. Jumbo Singh
laughed with huge contempt. “Look here,” he
said, “don’t you bother about Mahomed Khan.
He’s not half the rider that I am. True, he has a
following of low-caste coolies who believe in him,
but if you are dealing with the very aristocracy of
horsemanship just you come to me ! Also, if you’re
writing anything about swordsmanship I am the
one you should come to. Not a man in the Indian
army can touch me at that! And, as for shooting
” “But,” protested the scribe, “you see, our
office in its ignorance told me to see Mahomed
Khan, and I’ve got to obey orders.” “Just as
you like,” remarked the darkie loftily. “ But you
tell them, if they put in that stuff about me, I’ll
buy a thousand copies and post them all over
India.” Kipling wasn’t far out with his “half
devil, half child.”
