The Oldest Jewelleiy House in Sydney, 23 HUNTER STREET.
In Frank Thornton’s Dressing-
Room.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Come in, old chap ! By Jove, isn’t it cold 1
Shut the door! Thanks, It is cold i I didn’t
believe it could be so cold in Sydney ; can’t get
warm except when I’m at work. Yes, I get plenty
of work in this part. It’s the heaviest part I’ve
ever played in my life, and I’m ‘Facing the
Music,’to the tune of dropping lOlbs. in weight
the past three weeks. Made the mistake the
other day of giving a matinee —to a full house—
Oh, no ! I don’t want to publish figures—but I did
open iny eves when Mayne brought round the
total. But that’s not it. The matinee knocked
me out. After it was over, I crawled home at five
and went to bed until half-past six.
“ How is it that people so closely associate
me with the ‘ Private Secretary ’ and
‘Charley’s Aunt’? T’other day I was stand-
ing in the street with a ladv, and some-
one close by said ‘that’s the Private Sec-re-
tary an’’is bloomin’ lionali ’ '1 he success of my
life was as Mousta, a humpbacked, one-eyed
monster, in W. S. Gilbert’s blank verse tragic
play ‘Broken Hearts.’ But people don’t know
that--they always associate me with the ‘ Private
Secretary.’ Irving lent me a dagger to use
in the part of Mousta, and when I was on, Ellen
Terry herself brought Irving along, saying ‘Gome
and see this little comedian play tragedy.’ After
the performance Irving said to me, ‘ You’ie wasting
your time in comic opera,’ And he asked me to
keep the dagger as a memento.
“The Australian audience is the most critical
and appreciative in the world, and the best
audience I've played to. And it’s a public that
forms its own opinion, and sticks to it. For
instance, ‘The Bookmaker’ received excellent
notices from the Australian press everywhere.
It was practically boomed by newspaper criti-
cisms, but the Australian public didn’t fancy it,
so wild horses wouldn’t draw them into the
til 'atre.
“An actor who is original, and who makes
suggestions to a manager, is a very valuable
acquisition to a company ; and a girl who has
initiative quickly rises in the profession. When I
was young I was as full of zeal as 1 am now ; and
I sometimes made a suggestion to W. S. Gilbert,
although be was apt to resent it. Here is an
instance, from the rehearsal of ‘lolanthe’: ‘I
would like to make a suggestion, sir.’ Gilbert
(savagely) : ‘Aren’t my lines good enough, sir?’
Thornton: ‘Yes, sir; but the line, “the sheet
slips demurely from under me,” is a bit awkward,
sir.’ Gilbeit (overwhelmingly) : ‘Why, sir?’
Thornton : ‘ I have to make a face like this when
I say the sheet shbllips, sir.’ Gilbert : ‘ What
would be better, sir ? ’ Thornton : ‘ The sheet
glides, sir.’ Gilbert (with loud pomposity): ‘My
id rds, if you please, sir I—my wok os ! ’
FRANK THORNTON.
Photo, by Talma, George-street, Sydney.
“ The ‘Private Secretary ’ as originally produced
was a failure. The star part of the piece origin-
ally was Gibson, the tailor, who wants to get into
society, and the piece was guyed and booed on the
first night. But Beerbohm Tree scored so great a
hit with • his creation of the Secretary, that
that part was made the star part and' to-day
we play it with the make-up and mannerisms as
created by Beerbohm Tree.
“Nearly all actors and actresses are content at
rehearsal, to merely speak their lines. I have the
greatest difficulty to get my company to act at
rehearsal. When I’m rehearsing I act—act to the
empty theatre. And after I’ve let myself go, I
sometimes overhear my company in the wings
saying ‘Look at the governor ; how can he do it ;
isn’t he worked up ! ’
“The opera ‘Pinafore’ is another instance of a
frost becoming a great success. It was produced
in the month of May at London Opera Comique,
and was. such a dire failure that the co. agreed to
accept two-thirds salary until the next opera
(‘Pirates of Penzance’) was ready. Shortly the
news came along from America that ‘ Pinafore ’
had caught on there amazingly, that dozens of
co.’s were playing it. With that the English
public awoke to the merits of ‘ Pinafore,’ and con-
sequently the piece ran a year and 11 months to
enormous business. A two years’ run I —yet on
the first night the English crowd jeered and
hooted, and after Josephine's song they yelled,
‘ Oh, take it away ; there’s another shop ballad.’
“ The most sympathetic actress I ever rehearsed
with was Lucia Harwood. She used to play Ruth
Rolt to my Dick Phenyl. In rehearsing the scene
in which she says farewell to Dick she would be
THE SHEPHERD-STREET TRAGEDY.
The two suspected men who are now being hunted by N.S.W. police.
crying, and so would I. I’d turn to tier and say,
‘ Stop it, Lucia, you fool ! you’re blubbering ; ’
and Lucia would reply, 1 Look at yourself ; you're
tbe fool; iou began it.'
“ A recent instance of a failure being changed
into a success was ‘The Manualvres of Jane’ at
London Hay market. Jones is the author. At
time or first production, the play, ‘WhatHap-
pened to Jones’was running at the Strand. On
first night of ‘Jane' the audience oooed and
hoote 1. Jane,' played as a serious comedy, went
dreadfully flat. The gallery jeered at every turn.
‘Oh, take it away!—what’s happened to Jones.’
Maude, the actor-manager, then hit on the ex-
pedient of broadening Jane out to a tarce, with
the result that a play tnat was dead on trie first
night ran ior over a year.
“ Then tnere was Kitty Grey, a comedy pro-
duced not long ago by George Edwards at the
Vaudeville. Magnificent cast—no go . He turned
it into a musical piece, and ever since ne has been
coining money with it m London and the pro-
vinces.
“One of the very smartest things of the London
stage was done at the Comedy Theatre at a
matinee of one of Charles Hawtrey’s plays. The
audience was much taken by the leading man’s
clever burlesque of Charles Hawtrey’s acting, and
applauded vociferously time after time. At the
close the leading man pul ed of? his make-up, re-
vealing Charles Hawtrey himself laughing at the
bewildered audience. ‘ Ladies and gentlemen, I
am very glad that my imitation of myself has
pleased you so well.’
“Some actors and actresses get very big salaries
in London, but even first-class people get much
smaller salaries than one would expect. One of
the chief reasons is that so many younger sons of
swells are now turning to the stage. They look
well, speak well, and as they get an allowance
from the paternal estate they can afford to play
for next to nothing. Besides that the profession
is so fearfully overdone, although in London they
have a standing population of some six millions
and a constant floating population of a quarter
million —250,000 people always ‘doing London.’
“ Vo, I haven’t seen a doctor about my loss in
weight. For us actors there’s no tonic like the
footlights, no doctor so good as Doctor Theatre.
“I vvill tell you a story of stage superstition.
Mr.D’Oyly Carte and I had a pleasant chat one
day, each of us sitting comfortably ’in an arm-
chair, and while enjoying a good cigar we came to
a very amicable agreement. The agreement was
that I was to stage-manage and produce ‘Princess
Ida ’ for Mr. Carte in America, on terms
which we fixed to our mutual satisfaction. And
we further pleasantly agreed that the contract
should be signed on the morrow, and that I should
take my departure by the morrow’s midnight
train from Euston. Home I went, packed up
trunks, sweated, bustled, worried, grafted, got
ready. Then early next morning, with serene
feelings and a smiling face, I rattled in a cab
down to Mr. Carte’s office. Self-congratulation
rose within me as I reflected that the signing of
the contracts would occupy but ten minutes,
and that then I would have the rest of the
day to spend in the way that I liked best.
The doorkeeper stopped me. ‘ Beg pardon, sir ;
Mr. Carte is very busy now, sir, and cannot see
you.’ For a moment the news knocked me over,
then I knocked thedoor-keeperover, jumped up the
stairs and into D’Oyly Carte’s office. ‘ D’Oyly ! ’
I shouted, ‘what’s the game?’ He looked up
from his desk and said very affably “ There is no
game ; I will meet you at the train and sign the
contract.’ I answered: ‘Rats ! If you’re doing
to sign the contract why not do it notv?’ Rising
with dignity he said very solemnly and collectedly,
‘My dear Thornton, don’t get excited ; I’ll meet
you at the train and sign the contract .’
“At 15 minutes before midnight I arrived at
the railway with my bags, my trunks, my boxes,
my portmanteaux, my hat-boxes, my macintosh,
my great coat, my umbrella, my goloshes, my
orange, my biscuits, my tea-bottle, my milk-
bottle, my cushion, my fur-cap, my warm slip-
pers, my cough-mixture, my wliisky, my cigar-
box, my cook, my valet, my groom, my bootblack,
etc., etc., etc. And damme if there was one
sign of Mr. D’Oyly Carte. I began and kept at it
for ten minutes. As I stopped momentarily to
get breath, [ heard a soft, suave voice purring at
my side : ‘My dear Thornton, don’t swear ; my
wife has come with me to see you off.’
“ ‘Oh, no, D’Oyly : I’m not off ! ’
“‘My dear Thornton, don’t make a scene;
kindly get into the train and I’ll sign the con-
tract.’
“‘My dear D’Oyly, I will get into the train
after you have signed the contract, and it’s now
two minutes to 12.’
“Mrs. Carte got hold of my right arm, Mr.
Carte took mv left, and partly by entreaty and
mostly by shoving they got me into the carriage.
“ The clock began booming the midnight twelve.
“‘Sh-h-h! dear Mr. Thornton,’ purred Mrs.
Carte.
“ ‘ Sh-h-h be blowed,’ I said : ‘look here, D’Oyly,
if that contract’s not signed I’ll get out at Liver-
pool and come back. ” Bang! went the last
stroke of midnight. Shriek went the train
whistle. D’Oyly Carte whipped the contracts out
of his pocket, Mrs. Carte fetched an ink-Dottle
. and pen out of her pocket, . and we were off;
I in trie carriage, D’Oyly Carte and Mrs. Carte
running along the piatform, Mrs. D’Oyly clutch-
ing ner skirts up viitn her left hand, and
keeping stride with ner nusband. So for thirty
yards we sped along, Mrs. Carte holding tne ink
bottle m tier right Hand, D’Oyiy Carte signing the
contract, then scrambling me the pen, I signing
the duplicate. Throwing the duplicate to them I
hung out of the window and shouted, ‘Why the
devil didn’t you fix it up before?’ And D’Oyly
Carte propping at the edge of tue station with red
puffing cheeks, with Ins panting wife beside him
clutching the ink-bottle in her right hand and her
skirts in her left, mapped his hands, one each side
of his mouth, and shot oack the answer through
the distance, above the roar of the train, “BE-
CAUSE I NEV - ER SIGN A CON -
TRACT ON ERI - DAY-Y-Y-Y ! ! ! ’ ”
S. A. MILLS.

POLITICAL
POINTS
erdict of the colonial division of the
Imperial Conference—Better Not.
It looks at present as if the Federal tariff
is going to be put through very easily after all,
despite the yelping of half-a-dozen “ powerful ”
dailies.
They call the Senate’s tariff “requests”
the Dobson-Fraser suggestions, for nearly every
division was carried with the aid of one or other
of t! lose two wobblers.
Action is threatened against certain
M’s.L.A. of N.S.W. who refuse to pay income-
tax. They haye nothing but their Parliamentary
salaries and contend that these are untaxable.
A study in bathos and anti-climax at the
Canada club dinner :
Laurier : Canada can be the Empire’s granary.
Barton : And Australia the Empire’s Butcher.
Seddon : And Maoriland can supply the Empire
with butter and cheese.
While Adelaide has handed over her tram-
way system to a syndicate, Chicago, the second
city of America, has, by an overwhelming vote,
declared in favor of the municipal ownership of
street railways and of gas and electric-lighting
plants.
THE OUTBREAK OF THOMAS.
RAILWAY OFFICERS.
PLATFORM UTTERANCES.—FORCIBLE STATEMENT
BY MR. BENT.
“....was most atrocious. I remarked that if the
man who divulged the figures were in the military forces
he would be shot. As to the speeches, lam not going to
bother over every whelp that likes to talk.”
If T. Bent had his way the above might, in a
slight degree , picture a Minister of Railways'
attitude towards State employees.
Why shouldn't the Australian Common-
wealth, or the States, own a line of passenger and
cargo-steamboats just as they own the railways?
Such ownership would be a safeguard against
such a Morgan invasion as that which bids fair to
cripple England’s oversea-trade.
Queensland democrats have always had
good reason for insisting on an absentee tax, but
never a better one, and never one so easily grasped
by the general intelligence as the present financial
condition of that State. The Queensland ab-
sentee is the white-haired boy of long years of
bad legislation, and it will be a crime if, in the
present whipping of the cat, he is allowed to go
free.
If N.S.W. Advances to Settlers Act be, as
desired, amended to be also an Advances to
Wtrkers’ Act, the redundancy and surplusage of
the title will weigh it down. Why not call the
nnderfaking a State Loan Office, or an Industrial
State Bank ?
Concerning the famous and smellful Sea-
forth estate purchase. It is now a year since the
property—purchased at two or three times its
value by Queensland Government —was thrown
open to purchasers, and not a soul has applied for
an acre of it yet!
The impassioned loyalty of a certain
member of the See (N.S.W.) Ministry in recent
times makes it worthy of notice that just seven
years ago, when the question before N.S.W.
Leg. Assembly was whether Federation should be
under the Crown or under a Republic, that poli-
tician voted for a Republic. Guess his name !
The finest instance of the “geographical
Freetrade” that has been so much in evidence
during the Senate’s consideration of the tariff
occurred on the very last daw, when Gould and
Neild, who pose as absolute Cobdenists, stood up
valiantly for the highly-protected N.S.W. tobacco-
manufacturing industry. The tobacco trade is an
influential one, and politicians have always dealt
tenderly with it. And hereby hangs a tale.
Crouch, M.H.R., asked in the House the
other day whether the Government would arrange
for the popular election of a Governor-General,
under section 2 of the Constitution Act, and the
Reps, roared as if it were the most absurd
paradox. This acceptance of conventions as laws
of God is the sure indication of the truly con-
servative mind, to which even a disease becomes
respectable and desirable if it is of long standing.
Speaker M‘Court t’other day dismissed,
as informal, a petition presented to N.S.W.
Assembly, on the ground that said petition was
typewritten. Parliament, generally the birth-
place of liberal reforms, is conservative to the
point of idiocy on matters affecting its own
fashions and modes of procedure. Witness the
absurd gyrations of Black Rod, the trotting-in at
the right moment of the Mace, and other similar
mummeries.
Why Minister Kingston falls in, is through
his plain, blunt idea that laws were meant to be en-
forced—not to roost for purely ornamental pur-
poses on the dead limb of the statute-book. The
law prohibiting the importation of goods made by
convict labor was approved almost unanimously,
but the first time it was enforced (in Kingston’s
own State and against his own personal friend)
quite a storm fell on his head because he didn’t
withdraw the charge.
The emphatic negative given to the
British-Cabinet proposal that “the colonies”
should provide an English army reserve, shows
how well politicians recognise that the Jingo
sentiment of the last few years is merely a surface
feeling, and that if you scratch even a Melbourne
Mafficker you’ll find an Australian beneath. Only
the blood-drunk Seddon favored the proposal;
Australia, Canada, and S’Africa said “No,” and
it was at once dropped.
Discussing the question of the Common-
wealth entering into some partnership with the
Imperial Govt, as regards the navy, Melb. Akgus
writes
The feeling of attachment and unity is growing on the
side of tiie colonies ; it has progressed wonderfully of late
years. The one thing calculated to chill and damp it
would be the proposal to seal a bond appraising the cash
value of the colonies’ fidelity to the empire.
Just so. Vociferous loyalty is one thing, and
payment in coin is quite another.
Q. Govt., in deference to the mandate of
a number of sky-pilots who have just taken a sham
referendum, has made a modification (per
regulation) in the Education Act to allow clergy-
men to give religious instruction during the first
half-hour of school time. The Q. Education Act
expressly specifies that only secular instruction
shall be given during school hours, and this new
regulation (without any statutory amendment) is,
without doubt, absolutely ultra vires.
Queensland Government seems desirous
of taking special vengeance on Hansakd. One
reporter at £3OO per annum has been dispensed
with ; £5OO per annum is to be taken off the re-
maining seven; and into the bargain a 10 to 15
per cent, reduction all round is yet to come and
then an income tax on the top of it all. Parlia-
mentary reporting (especially in Committee) is to
be much curtailed—to the great joy of certain
Brisbane dailies who ask with charming in-
genuousness—“ Why can’t people be content with
Press reporting of debates?”
Victoria cuts off the sixpence a week rise
formerly granted to its poor six-and-sixpence a
day bobby after two years’ service, but ne’er a
word about reducing the pay of its State Governor,
or extending the. land-tax—which at present only
touches properties over 640 acres in extent and
then is only levied on any value over £2500, so
that all Melbourne is exempt —in order to square
its ledger. Queensland takes 5 per cent, off the
charwoman at the public offices, takes 10 per cent,
off salaries over £lOO, and 15 per cent, off salaries
over £450 —but it exempts the Governor (<£sooo a
year and allowances for looking at half-a-million
people) also the Chief-Justice (£2500 a year) and
the Puisne Judges (£2OOO a year). And not a
word about a land-tax there either ! Australia
fools away nearly three times as much per in-
habitant on its Governors as Britain does on its
royal family and all the royal family relations.
And when hard times come it would rather
retrench the poorest charwoman than touch its
sacred mock royalty.
“HE GIVETH HIS BELOVED PEACE.”
How is India represented in this great
London Imperial Conference ? Three hundred
million ardent loyalists left out, while a represen-
tative of five millions, another of three and three-
quarters, and another of less than one, are settling
the whole hash. Is the entire Empire bent on
securing the prosperity of India, too? What a
noble aim to secure for India that legislation
which is the best for India ! But the representa-
tion in London only consists of two or three
tiddlywinking little potentates, who are absorbed
in eating, drinking, and the ballet. Not a word
about India at the Conference. You see, it is
Bull’s slave region.
King Ned’s invitation-card to a Corona-
tion dinner on sth July, 1902 (reproduced by
several London daily papers), showed, in a
finely-executed design, a sturdy black clasping
hands with a fair white woman. Unconsciously
thus was shown the aim and the weakness of his
Empire. The Black Man and the White Woman
will never unite in amity, for it is against Nature
and her inexorable laws ; when, in spite of Nature,
degraded individuals commit the sin of race-mix-
ture, the punishment is great and follows unto the
fourth generation. But the British Empire—
mostly a nigger Empire—has to either foster that
mixture or face disruption.
It was just about the time that the Corona-
tion fell through that the Boer gave up his alleged
great joy at having become a British subject and
became grumpy and unfriendly again. Edward
VII. was supposed to have had a great deal to do
with the making of peace, and it was only an act
of common kindness to persuade him that he had
made a real peace. He doubtless wanted to be
able to say and to think on Coronation day, that
he reigned over an empire that was not only peace-
ful but cordially friendly. So the ruling powers
in S’Africa, having absolute control over the
cables, supplied a friendly, not to say wildly
enthusiastic, Boer to order. He was a temporary
enthusiastic Boer warranted to last (by cable) till
Coronation day, and he fell through again imme-
diately after that date.
Victoria discovers that £150,000 of its
“ permanent and reproductive ” public debt is
represented only by a squad of worn-out en-
gines slowly oxidising in the sheds at
Newport. If each one had been broken
up when it became worn out the value
would have had to be charged to
working expenses. As working ex.’s
looked bad enough without that, the
ancient wrecks were preserved in a
corner and kept in the balance-sheet as
genuine assets, and they are there yet.
Once more the good old “ permanent and repro-
ductive ! ”
Having kicked the hated Trenwith, and
with him the rest of the Peacock crowd, out of
office, Symes Age was at latest engaged in
shaking the Kyabram incubus oft' its shoulders.
Asa preliminary it jumps on poor old ‘ ‘ Sam ” Lan-
caster, president of that belated gang of political
aspirants, and trie other “bat-eyed Conservatives
of the Citizens' Reform League.” Mr. Lancaster,
the Syme paper remarks, “has no standing as the
leader of public opinion, and any prominence he
now enjoys comes to him purely because of the
movement with which he has the good fortune to
allyshimself.” And the whole Kyabramapootra
agitation was merely run by a lot of male hens of
the Lancaster type, who thought they saw in it an
exceptional opportunity of worming into the good
graces of the mighty David, and thereby getting
a lift into Parliament. Lancaster’s nasty slap in
the eye but presages the general belaboring of
Ben Goddard, John West and co., and it is
tolerably safe to say that when David divides the
sheep from the goats next election time, those
petty schemers will all be among the goats.
A fact to be remembered always in connec-
tion with the question of land-resumption for close
settlement is that, under good management, the
merino will pay just as well as the man, and, as-
the absentee knows, he is not half the trouble.
Ercildoun and Dowling Forest (Vic.), under
respectively the late Salmon Wilson and Bart.
Clarke, showed this. On each estate is at least.
20,000 acres of first-class agricultural land. The
Wilson lot, under sheep, was run by a manager
and about six hands. Clarke had 68 tenant-
farmers, with their families and at least two
hands apiece the year through.
The shabby little faking tha~ has been
done over the N.S.W. State’s Sydney land-
resumptions is a bigger misfortune than it looks.
The political swindle by which all the rents that
the Government could lay its hands on were
treated, and are still being treated, as revenue,
while the corresponding liabilities are charged to ■
loans, will probably, in the end, burden this State
enterprise with half-a-million or perhaps a million
more debt than it should carry. That means
from £15,000 to £40,000 a year more interest,
according to the price paid for the money.
The probable result will be that the en-
terprise, which would otherwise have
paid handsomely, will show a loss, and
State - socialism will get a huge set-
back. The principle is good, but the
paltry crookedness of a Ministry whose
political systems of the three-card-trick
order would bring the best principle
into contempt. The Labor party could
have made the Ministry go straight, but
it didn’t; and there is no permanent suc-
cess for a party which won’t lift a finger
to prevent its own best ideas being thus dragged
in the gutter.
Sydney Telegraph weeps over the pro-
posed North Shore bridge : —
The Government knows all about the need for the
bridge ; no question as to the merits of the case will be
raised ; estimates of cost have been secured. But where
is the money to come from ? If that question could be
satisfactorily answered the bridge would soon become a-
fact.
Exactly. In a poor, small, commonplace spot
like England, cities just put up bridges and pay for
them out of the rates. A special rate of 2s. Or 2s. 6d.
in the £ levied on Greater Sydney for two years
would pay for the whole structure, and even while
the payment was going on the rates would be
lower than in any big English city, and less than
half what they amount to in some cities. And
the Telegkaph, which is so much excited about
following the English example in tariff matters,
never even sees a glimmer of this simple explana-
tion. True to its good old gospel of cowardice and
helplessness, it can only suggest sitting down and
wondering where to borrow the “splosh.”
Attorney-General Wise (who seems to be
the only N.S.W. Minister with any idea of reform
legislation) has personally drafted a new
State Children Amending Bill. At present in
N.S.W. mites of tender years are haled before the
beak, perhaps together with a miscellaneous lot of
drunks and vags. Three separate Ministerial
Departments manage the several institutions, run
by Govt, for their reception, and often, a child is
committed to the wrong sort of institution for its
particular case. Mr. Wise is going to create a.
board (or enlarge the powers of the present State
Children’s Board) to have the power of committing
a child direct to a place of safety or correction
without facing the ordeal of a police-court trial.
This is the most important point of the new Bill,
which deals with a class disquietingly on the in-
crease. * There is nothing novel about the humane-
scheme. Mostly, it is “lifted” from the S.A.
Act. Its realisation, however, looked hopeless
until Wise interested himself in it.. N.S.W.
politicians generally look for something more
showy than simply useful legislation.
“ Del ” : Demagogues are the tubercles of
democracies. Whether they be leather-sellers or
news-sellers it is our Cleons of whom it behoves-
us to beware. Though Athens graved Form from
Chaos, her demagogues dragged her to the
dust (for it paid them) and Greater
Greece with her. The daily news-
paper, by turns unscrupulous
in its own interests, and
flattering the people
through their pas-
sions, is the real
Cleon, the dema-
gogue of our
democracies.
QUIXOTE-SANCHO.
Steyn recalls Stein, the German patriot.
When Bonaparte crushed Prussia, at Jena, he
reduced the Prussian army to 40,000 men. Stein
invented the scheme by which this number of
men was trained afresh every year. Thus Napo-
leon laid the foundation of Germany’s military
greatness.
>
END OF THE “ DAILY MAIL” OFFER
The Daily Mail (London) announces in our
columns to-day the final withdrawal of its
“Encyclopaedia Britannica” offer in New South
Wales. This sale was undertaken a few weeks
ago by permission of the London Times, and the
period of the contract having been reached, no
further books can be sold after to-day.
We understand the sale has been most
successful, exceeding that even of the Times
distribution of the same work two years ago. A
novel feature of this new offer is an extra volume
compiled especially for the use of students and
others, and entitled “A Handbook of Systematic
Readings in the ‘Encyclopaedia Britannica.’”
This volume forms a 26th and is sent gratis with
all orders for the “Encyclopaedia.” Much com-
ment has been caused by the characteristic manner
in which this offer of the “Encyclopaedia Britan-
nica” has been placed before the New South Wales
public. This no doubt accounts for its marked
success. To-day is the very last day. See
announcement elsewhere.

Mr. PARK, of California dully, Bendigo,
Victoria, swears that
VITADATIO
HAS CURED HIM OF
Cancer in the Tongue.
To MR. PALMER—
I, Frederick Parks, of California Gully, near Bendigo,
in the State of Victoria, do solemnly and sincerely declare
that this is my Testimonial to you on behalf of mv cure.
I have been a sufferer with Cancer in the mouth for the
past six years. I went to the hospital, and the first thing
they did was to take the tongue right out. In two
months’ time, after the operation, the Cancer grew again ;
it got larger than before. The doctors said that nothing
could be done, so I went home and was put to bed. I
asked my wife to get me a bottle of VITADATIO. I took
half a bottle that night, and in three days I was able to
take oatmeal and sago, and have had no other medicine
but VITADATIO within my mouth. I can solemnly
declare that the world does not know the power of
VITADATIO. lam the the only one living 6Ut of forty-
five cases of Cancer treated last year, and can solemnly
swear that only for VITADATIO I would not be here now,
so I think you can guarantee a cure for Cancer, as mine
was a very bad case ; there being no hope, the doctors
said, and now I can take any kind of food the same as
before the operation. And I make this solemn declara-
tion, conscientiously believing the same to be true, and by
virtue of an Act of Parliament of Victoria rendering
persons making a false declaration punishable for wilful
and corrupt perjury.
F. PARKS.
Declared before me, at Bendigo, in the State aforesaid,
this 14th day of May, in the year of our Lord one
thousand nine hundred and one.
J. M. DAVIES, J. P.
For further particulars,
S. A. PALMER,

A resourceful railway official has just been
brought to light in Victoria. Through'the negli-
gence of a subordinate at a suburban station, this
masterful individual missed his last train home.
He did not adopt the miserable subterfuge
of other people who were “had” in the
same way, and either walk or take a cab,
but instead telephoned to the depht for a
special engine, which conveyed him home at an
excellent speed and in great majesty. Of course,
the mere annoyance thus caused to the engine-
driver and his yawning assistant, prevented from
getting away to their homes, didn’t matter in the
slightest. When found out, this ready-witted
station-master pleaded that he utilised the engine
as a common home-going passenger who had been
led astray by the default of a railway servant.
But the departmental chiefs decided that as he
was only a passenger at the time he should not
have appealed to himself as station-master for
redress, but should have gone round to his own
night-porter and laid his case before that tem-
porary despot. The comic opera hero, therefore,
had to pay for his engine-ride some of £2 10s.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Rawdon looks to have a considerable bit the
ou-r er ,° f . the wei e:hts with stable-companion Colonel
Shilmsk l in the Caulfield G. N. Hurdle Race. He beat
the Colonel by a head at Flemington, and is now pitted
against his imposing rival with another 21bs. in his favor.
Again, the fact that both horses will be so much more
Heavily burdened than before should tell in favor of the
one that is least “crushed” and has proved himself quite
’ l\ I t ? ot , B “P erior > to the other in stamina and pluck.
Still, Shilinski would look as well-handicapped as any-
thing in the crowd if Rawdon’s name were missing,
uiven good health and a safe issue out of all their jump-
ing practice up to the day (August 2) the pick of the two
cracks frpm the Miller stable will be a tempting first
favorite for the Hurdle Race. Meanwhile, this scribe
prefers Rawdon’s chance.
* • * •
Kiota, the own brother to Daimio, has got 9.8
only for the G.N. Steeple, at Caulfield, run on the 9th.
this is 21b. less than he carried over the Flemington
sticks without making a mistake, and, if the promising
young beginner can put on the necessary “ condition ”
between the two events, he must have a good chance.
Two of the horses that fell at Flemington promise to be
more dangerous over the longer distance and easier jumps
at Caulfield. Royal Mail, stable mate of Kiota, is under-
stood to be none the worse for his unlucky topple ; like-
wise Mystery, an old battler that has more than ordinary
speed, and was capable of beating Royal Mail over the
V.R.G. steeplechase course, a few months ago.
* * * *
The balance-sheet of the Adelaide Racing Club
shows that, for seven days racing in the year, it gave
£7425 in stakes. And, in connection with those occasions,
it received in tote commissions (7£ per cent.), £6332 ;
about £l5O more than the nomination and acceptance
fees, gate-money, catering, race-cards, &c., all put
together 1 The membership is 456, at two guineas.
• * * *
f Destroying of Q. nag Monseigneur at Ipswich
t’other day, consequent upon an accident, brings to mind
that his owner, Mr. J. M'Qill, of Blacklands, has had
much more than a fair share of ill-luck lately. Bucolic,
Notre Dame and North Australian were previous losses
this season, then promising colt Buckmaster went amiss
when most wanted, and, topping it all, many valuable
brood mare 3 perished through the drought.
* * * *
M.L. winning sires’ list this season is topped by
St. Leger, whose progeny have appropriated £ll,OOO, and
Mr. Stead occupies his usual place as leader of M.L. win-
ning owners with (up to last reckoning) £12,325.
New Australian racing season begins Friday
this week. Winning owners’ list for closing year is
headed by Mr. L. McDonald with £12,322, principally
through Wakeful and Revenue’s appropriations of Mel-
bourne and Sydney Cups and Newmarket Handicap, etc.
Mr. “ J. Baron” finished second with £4223—a big fall—
and Mr. Agar Wynne (£4112) third. Trenton and Gozo
are easily ahead of all other successful sires. ’Tis nine
years since any Australian owner scored as remuneratively
as Mr. McDonald ; in 1892-3 Mr. J. B. Clark’s nags brought
him £13,421. M.L.’s biggest winner (Mr. G. C. Stead)
handled £3 more than Mr. McDonald.
• • * •
V.R.O. balance-sheet is interesting. Stakes
distributed reached £48,904, as against £44,094 for pre-
vious year, and added money was £37,295 —nearly £5OOO
over that for preceding 12 months. Biggest rise was on
Spring meet, when receipts exceeded disbursements by
£9186. Far the greatest loss occurred in connection with
Autumn gathering, which depleted the bank account to
the extent of £3601. Year’s total profits were £10,138,
and losses £3905, leaving a net gain of £6228. All liabili-
ties paid, including £lO,OOO for improvements to the
club’s property in Bourke-st., Melbourne, Y.R.C.’s credit
is now £15,000.
* • * *
Mr. T. H. Smith, M.L.C., who died in Sydney
Sunday night, was among N.S.W.’s oldest racehorse
breeders and owners. Captivator, Anteros, Clayton
(especially imported) were his most successful sires.
His nags operated principally in the Clarence and
Richmond River districts, where he resided, but many of
them succeeded at Metropolitan meetings. Alderman,
Prince Edward, Prince Consort and Princess Arena were
among the best. The dead man was one of the pioneer
settlers of the Clarence, and had been identified with
pastoral interests there for upwards of 50 years.
« » * »
Fourth test—England v. Australia—began
Thursday and ended Saturday last, and was a most
remarkable match. Though it rained overnight and in
early morning, weather was fine when play opened before
17,000 people. Win of toss gave visitors a big pull, as at
the outset wicket was slow and easy, but became caked
subsequently. First partnership (Trumper and Duff)
yielded 173 before Trumper was bowled by Rhodes with
104 to his credit, accumulated in exactly lhr. 50mins.,
during which the pair hit up 168. Trumper’s score
included 14 fours, 3 threes, and 6 twos. Third wicket fell
at 179, and the fourth at 183. Hill and Darling kept
going till (when 256 had been notched) former was
caught; his contribution totalled 65, made up of 5 fours,
3 threes, and 6 twos. This combination had added 73.
Seven were down for 288. Darling was next out (caught
off a skyer). Hi 3 51 were put together in 95 minutes,
and included two sixes (both off Rhodes), and 5 fours.
The rest failed badly—seven of them only aggregated 17—
and the full total stood at 299. At the close of that day’s
play five Britishers were out for 70, of which Abel,
Palairet, Maclaren, and Ranji contributed no more than
15. Next morning Jackson and Braund put a widely-
different complexion on things by bringing the figures
to 185—a partnership of 141 for the sixth wicket,
which is one run less than the record. When the tenth
fell the English team’s total was only 37 to the bad.
Australia’s second time at the wickets saw the trio
(Trumper, Duff and Hill) who, among them, had totted
up 223 in the first innings, all out for 7, while six others
could do no better than 15. Gregory and Darling made
61 of the 86 scored by the whole side. With 124 to win
England had 2 out for 68 and 4 for 89. At one stage 16
were required and 5 wickets still remained to fall, but
Trumble and Saunders got them all in quick succession
for 13, thus leaving Australia successful by 3 runs.
Several hours rain the night before and some during the
Englishmen’s second innings played havoc with the pitch,
hence the collapse. The luck towards the end was all
with Australia ; any mistake by batsmen brought about
their discomfiture for a certainty. In England’s opening
innings Trumble bowled 4 for 75, Saunders 3 for 104 and
Noble 2 for 47. Trumble and Saunders had the honors
between them the second time, for the former sent 6 back
for 53 and the latter 4 for 52.
• * * *
Triumph of Australia over England by 3 runs
in fourth test is the narrowest margin recorded for these
contests. Nearest was Australia’s 6 runs’ success against
Shaw and Shrewsbury’s combination, at Sydney Cricket
Ground, in Feb., ’B5 ; and next was at ’Ome, on the Oval,
when, in ’B2, Australia came out best by 7. The “rub ”
of present tour comes Australiawards. Four of the. five
tests have been played—two were drawn and two won by
the visitors; the result of the fifth, therefore, cannot
affect the position.
•* * * *
Up to moment of writing, Australians have
played 24 matches in England, of which they won 14,
lost 1, and 9 were drawn. Prior to the latest (fourth
test), Trumper was much in front of batting figures, with
29 innings for 1319 runs, averaging 45"48. Duff and
Hill were closely bracketed, the former having 33
innings (5 times n.o.)for 998, averaging 35'64, and the
latter 28 innings (1 n. 0.) for 934, averaging 34'59. Arm-
strong’s 8 n.o. innings kept him in fourth position, while
Gregory, Hopkins, and Darling, with larger aggregates,
were within three runs of the Melbourneite. Hopkins’
and Darling’s total compilations were 636 and 635, and
averages 25'44 and 25'40 respectively. Duff topped the
bowling averages (accidentally) with lwkt. for 0 runs.
Trumble is really the leader, his record being 39 wickets,
390 runs, averaging 10’CO. Saunders, Armstrong, Noble,
and Howell followed as named, with little between them.
Bowling feats of Australian Saunders and
Englishman Rhodes, last week, were'food for talk among
cricketing folk. Saunders’ 6 for 9 on third day of the
Surrey match—the last four of which were gathered in
without a run being scored—brings to mind Yorkshire-
man Jackson’s performance in opposition to Australia,
early this season, when he also accounted for four with-
out having a run scored against him. Right through the
Surrey match nine wickets fell to Saunders for 91.
Operating for Yorkshire v. Gloucestershire, top-notcher
Rhodes got 5 for 22 in one innings and 7 for 36 in the
other—actually 12 for 58 ; less than five per wicket.
• • * •
Latest test saw Clem Hill the first (Australian
or Englishman) to aggregate 1500 for those fixtures.
When the match began, Maclaren only wanted five to
achieve that honor, but Trumble bowled him for one,
while Hill scored 65. Maclaren’s 35 in a second attempt,
however, put him 31 beyond the required figures, which
Hill exceeded by 16. Ranji., with 1489 opposite his name
now, should be the next to gain the honor. Trumble’s
105 wickets to date equals the records of Gillen, Peel and
Turner.
• • • •
First innings of test match introduced Trumper
as a record-holder, as his 104 notched on that occasion
makes the sixth century credited to him during the
present tour, and consequently one more than the
previous best (Darling’s) created three years ago.
Trumper’s five other hundreds were—lol against Notts,
121 against Oxford ’Varsity, 105 against M.C.O. and
Ground, 128 against Cambridge, and 113 against an
English Eleven.
v * * *
His 65 runs in the first innings of the fourth
test just completed the even 1000 for Clem Hill for the
tour. R. A. Duff beat him for the four figures by about
one hour. Darling, the next man, was over 300 behind the
1000, Trumper 300 and more ahead.
* * * *
“Sprat”: In giving results of cricket matches
in which first innings has been closed (as Australian
match against Gloucester) why not credit the victors
with the actual win: “16 wickets, and so many runs," in-
stead of “ One innings and so many runs ”?
• * • *
Cable seems to delight in sending details of
English bowling feats (against English batting) by way
of glorifying the Australian batsmen, for they almost
invariably make light of Lockwood, Rhodes, Hirst, and
co., on a decent wicket. If these men have “great days"
how is it that they never do remarkable things, without the
assistance of rain, in a test-match ? Tom Richardson, of
yore, was the last English bowler who could be trusted to
“ come off ” against the Australians every time, and that
was 10 years ago.
* * • •
Australia-Surrey match, finished Wednesday
last week and hampered much by rain, resulted in a draw.
Surrey batted first, getting 296, with Abel (104), Brock-
well (39) and Hayes (37) top scorers. Howell bagged 5 for
80, and Saunders 3 for 82. Second effort brought 111,
Hayward contributing 48 and Hayes 22. This time
Saunders was sensational, bowling 6 for 9, with Armstrong
next best—2 for 34. Initial essay of Australians produced
313. Scores were topped by Hill (90) and Trumper (85).
Next best was Duff’s 57. Second innings saw 1 (Howell)
out for 11 when play ceased.
* * * ♦
Both parents of crack bowler W. P. Howell
(with the Australian team in England) died at Penrith
(N.S.W.) within a few days of each other ; the mother on
Thursday last week, and father Monday this week ; latter
was 66 years old, and, weakened through influenza,
succumbed to shock.
* » « *
Cabled that W. G. Grace has completed his two
hundredth century in first-class cricket.
* • *
The onljr amateur cricketer who ever came to
Australia with an England eleven at his own expense,
and didn’t draw a sixpence for his trouble, was, as it hap-
pened, the most consistently useful man in the team.
That was Phillipson, a wealthy Northumberland sport,
who did nearly all the wicket-keeping for Stoddart’s first
XI. and helped them to win the rubber in the tests. They
didn’t expect to want the attached amateur in the big
games. A man named Gay was engaged—as an amateur
with “ exes ” paid—for the position of boss wicket-keeper,
but Gay proved a failure from the start, and Phillipson
came to the rescue. Put in real good work behind the
sticks and made a few runs, when they were most wanted,
in a rough, confident style.
* * • «
That Australasian premier sprint swimmer,
Fred. Lane, at Manchester (Eng.) won the British
(actually the world’s) 100yds. supremacy in 60sec. was
news indeed. Prior world’s best figures were Derbyshire’s
(against time), accomplished in '9B, and Lane’s (Austral-
asian supremacy at Wanganui, M.L., in Feb. last) 1.0 1-5.
Of these only the last given had recognition through
t’other performance not being achieved in a race. For
many years the world’s swimmers have been aiming at
what Lane has done, but most of them, as well as swim-
ming writers in different parts—particularly on the oppo-
site side—voted it an impossibility without artificial aid.
Other Australian, Dick Cavill, was second by the touch
only, which means that, so desperate was the struggle,
their bodies might have been level at the finish, but the
quickest to shoot his arm out and touch the end of the
basin got the verdict. Cavill, too, was probably inside
the previous world’s figures. Derbyshire (holder of the
title and the record for several years) finished third.
Race was contested in the Englishman’s native city, and,
as latest mailed advices indicated that he was swimmim'
up to his top form, the merit of Lane’s and Cavill’s feat is
much enhanced.
* * * *
Contention that published statements attribut-
ing defeat of Australian swimmers in the Half-mile,
500 yards, and Long-distance Championships of
England to climate conditions were so much
bunkum is further borne out by Lane’s latest
feat (100yds. in 60sec.), and Cavill’s almost dead-
heat, second. As was demonstrated in the 500yds., so it
is with the 100yds., neither ever swam as well in Aus-
tralia. Another big thing of the English swimming year
(the 220yds.) has yet to be decided, and, provided all
three line up fit, Cavill, Lane, and Derbyshire should
supply an Olympian contest never to be forgotten, and
on the 100yds. form they ought to finish as named.
• • * *
The brilliant win by the Australian Rifle Team
in carrying off the coveted Kolapore Cup with a record
score must be gall and wormwood to some gentlemen of
the N.S.W. Rifle Association. Those persons contracted
a severe attack of “feelings” because of some alleged
base action on the part of Victoria or Tasmania. In con-
sequence, the N.S.W. reps, in the team were withdrawn.
However, the rest of the Commonwealth rifle shooters
“ bucked up,” sent their men, and have won the Cup.
* * • *
Imperial challenge trophy, Kolapore Cup, the
big thing of international teams’ rifle-shooting, fell to
Australia last week with a record score of 770. Previous
best was mother-country’s 768, in ’99, which averaged 96
per man ; and this the Australians, at practice three
weeks before, tied. English team got away with the
prize last year, having an aggregate of 759, and amono-
them was the highest individual scorer in the competi-
tion. Canadian reps, were second, making 751 ; their
last win was in ’96. Britain has succeeded 23 times since
the Cup was given by the late Rajah of Kolapore, in ’7l.
A Victorian team annexed it in, the Diamond Jubilee
year, and Guernsey on the following occasion. In the
connection it’s worth mentioning that five of this year’s
victorious eight—all Victorians—previously lined up for
the same competition as members of the ’97 and ’9B Vic-
torian combinations. The first lot won with an aggregate
of 75, and the next team was only beaten by three points
—Guernsey scoring 744 to the colonials’ 741.

Australian rifle shots, J. T. Lake (S.A.) and

in the King’s match at Bisley (Eng.) t’other day Con-

“ Graphic ” match saw M.L. fore-front shot, Mr. Wmslade,

* * * *
World’s pug supremacy wa3 contested in ’Frisco
Friday last week, when ’Murkan ex-boiler-maker Jeffries
went through old-man Maorilander, Bob Fitzsimmons, in
eight rounds. Nothing else could be expected, consider-
ing the way the match was made and the manner in
which freckled-fisted Fitz. conceded everything to the
hairy Yankee. As the Maorilander crossed the continent
to fix things, it was plainly a case of “ needs must.” Also
difference in age was much against Fitz., who is in the
40’s, while Jeffries hasn’t got through the 20’s. It’s any
odds that the fighters share a big “ gate ” and money paid
for cinematograph-picture rights, &c. Previous battle
between this pair, indicated Fitz.’s downfall, as Fitz. has
always had to make his own fight, which might be all right
with men of Jem Corbett’s stamp, but with a steam-hammer
like Jeffries it is entirely different. Peter Jackson used
to assert that if ever Fitz. and Jeffries met again, the
latter would surely win. The dead black classed Jeffries
among the best heavyweights the world has seen.
» * • *
First genuine contest for Australasian light-
weight bruising supremacy will occur in Sydney four
weeks hence, between champion Jack M'Gowan and
“ Rollo.” A few months ago latter wasn’t considered
among Sydney’s third-raters, but someone gave him a
chance of a match with Mat Mitchie by posting money
and providing the wherewithal for training. The Mitchie
party, cocksure of success, went down badly, for Rollo
won all the way. rle is sturdy, steady-going, fights hard
and takes punishment as if ’twere food to him ; and is
remarkably quick with his head. Melbourne’s curly-
haired miniature giant will need all the stoush he can
gather to settle “ Rollo.”
• • * •
Wail about pugilism being dead in Melbourne
was not borne out by results of the Doherty-Felix crash
for Australian supremacy. The “gate” totalled some-
thing in the neighborhood of £l3OO, bringing big profit
to the enterprising individual who hung up the £l5O
purse and probably paid another like sum for rent and
fitting generally. Last week Armidale maintenance man,
Billy M’Coll (now a full-blown dispenser of grog at New-
castle) and champion Bill Doherty were matched to fight
in the old Sydney Exhibition Building on Sept. 3. At
least as big a crowd as the Melbourne gathering is
expected, but this time the pugilists will share the spoil
after paying exes. The throng which witnessed Felix-
Doherty struggle was probably the biggest ever brought
together by a glove-fight in Australia, bar that when
Goddard and Peter Jackson met in the Crystal Palace,
Richmond (Vic.), 10 or 11 years ago. Other enormous
crowds were those drawn by the Foley-Burke affair on
the old Carrington Grounds, Sydney, and the Slavin-
Burke match in Melbourne. Griffo and Billy Murphy
packed the old York-st. (Sydney) Club at £1 each indi-
vidual twice, while Griffo and Martin Denny and Griffo
and Jerry Marshall bruised before great houses at the old
Darlingliurst Skating Rink, Sydney. Peter Jackson and
Tom Lees (for the Australian championship) 15 or 16
years ago divided a gate totalling perhaps £9OO.
• * * *
A match ’tween a Victorian greyhound not
named and the N.S.W. top-notcher, Great Heart, for
£lOOO aside, is likely to eventuate if supporters of the
latter find the money. The unknown is generally believed
to be among Tony Lumpkin, The Hermit, or the dividers
(Verse and Planet) of the last Waterloo Cup. Though
only a puppy still Great Heart will make an old man
opponent. So far he has an undefeated record, and
among his successes this season were two Derbies and the
rich N.S.W. Challenge Stakes. Not in any single course
has this remarkably good hound been led to the game,
and only in one instance (then 1J points) has an opponent
notched anything against him.
¥ m * *
Publio interest in cycle-racing has been killed in
Sydney by the tactics of the riders. Now an effort is to
be made at its resurrection. The N.S.W. League of
Wheelmen announces a series of big meetings for the end
of this year, prize-money to run into four figures, and
“ Major Taylor ” and other big importations at vast ex-
pense, are coming from America and elsewhere. The
League will need a lot of luck to carry the enterprise
through, for cycling in Sydney seems to have slumped
past reviving. The Sydney Cricket Ground trustees have
long since repented of the construction of that eyesore of
a track round their splendid ground.
* * * *
Road-race test at Launceston (Tas.), Saturday,
to select a rep. for Dunlop Tyre Co’s Warrnambool to
Melbourne bike contest, saw 18 start and eight finish.
Winner turned up in limit man, A. Harding, whose net
time for the 120 miles was 7h. 11m. 165., which is a fresh
record. Previous best was F. Beauchamp’s 7h. 19m.,
accomplished May 24, ’97.
Sandow.
“ Fluffy ” to The Bulletin, from Perth, W. A. :
Sandow made his first appearance in Australia before
an audience which filled every corner of Perth Theatre
Royal. Sandow was in the second part of Harry Rickards’
show, Lotty being the chief attraction of the first part.
Lotty apparently represents the available best in woman
form, while Sandow is put forth as all that is ideal in
manly brawn. Sandow stood revealed on a pedestal
with a piece of leopard skin around his loins, and white
tights on his legs ; his magnificent torso, corrugated with
muscles, was in its buff beauty. There were a dozen
poses, and at intervals the pedestal revolved, so that the
serried ranks of muscles lying along his back were brought
to view. Sandow’s development is extraordinary.
• * • •
His face is intelligent and bright, his skin perfectly
healthy, his teeth white and regular (presumably
indigenous), his hair crisp and curly, and his moustache
trained skyward a Id that of William the Sudden. The
face is frequently ablaze with astonishment at Sandow’s
marvellousness. If possible, Sandow is even more amazed
at Sandow than is Perth. One was stricken into silence by
the array of muscles, at one moment in ambush, and at
another leaping to view at the will of the Strong Man.
With a great noise of breathing he deflated his chest,
then, drinking in the air in gallons, he “ expanded ’’ till
there was a united gasp from the audience at a present-
ment that might be called wonderful, but was not with-
out an element of repulsiveness. The posing in th*
limelight was, on the whole, the best part of the show.
The muscle was beyond cavil, the beauty and balance of
the man beyond praise.
• * * •
The scene changing, a counterfeit of the Coliseum was
presented. _ Half-a-dozen Romans specially imported
from Victoria Park stood in attendance, while a few girls
represented the beauty of ancient Italy. The Romans
were probably the smallest that never served in a fish-
shop. The muscles of their brawny legs—they were like
silken bands. Seeing that he supports six on his chest,
Sandow is wise not to have men about him who are fat.
For this act he came mincing on to the stage—mfncing
is the only word. Sandow takes very short, hurried
steps, appears quite stiff at the knees, and grimaces and
gesticulates all the time.
* * * *
No-.v he is going to lift the 1261b. dumb-bell. He comes
down the stage stiff-legged, and does it, clearly and deftly,
with both hands, from the floor, extending the arms slowly
overhead, and then lowering the dumb-bell slowly tf> the
floor, letting it drop the last six inches with a thump.
Then he goes behind the proscenium, so as to get one of
the 17 recalls announced in the advertising colum .s
to-morrow morning. Next he lifts the 2461b. dumb-bell
and minces down the stage. Grips with one hand this
time, bringing in the weight of the body and the leverage.
The mass of iron goes up with a jerk, is brought quickly
back, and thumped upon the floor. Cheers, and yet
more cheers. But the two-handed management of the
lighter toy is the more praiseworthy.
* » * m
Sandow was most insistent on the music being changed
for each new feat. The orchestral scores are very com-
plete and the strong man did not hesitate to give the con-
ductor very marked notice if the music did not change at
the right moment. The tearing of the card-packs, and
the dance of the biceps each had their appropriate accom-
paniment. The “dance” was done with hands clasped
behind the head so that the upper arm was raised and the
muscles in full view. Then, beneath the pink skin, the
flesh was seen to roll about, and to leap rhythmically—a
thing very weird to see.
* * * *
Some men, not very strong, can tear a pack of cards
across. Sandow does this, but he takes one of the halves
and tears that. Then he takes two packs, opening them
from what purports to be original packages, places them
together and tears them. Then he tears three packs.
The three packs are almost a cube, and it is little leverage
that seems available for the task. There is a charac-
teristic theatrical touch that comes after the actual
accomplishment. The tearing is done at the knee/ One
end of the cards is gripped between the right hand and
knee, while the left hand seems to do the tearing. Then
comes the wrench, and the thing is done. Only a few
shreds are left to be severed, but to do this the ends are
brought away from the knee. Sandow braces himself.
His muscles start. With a mighty effort his hands pull
apart, there is crash from the orchestra, and the deed, al-
ready done, is officially accomplished. Meanwhile, one
of the noble Romans has been tying the sundered packs
into bundles. Sandow throws these into the audience
for souvenirs. He throws with an awkward underhand
jerk.
• * * *
Sandow’s last acts are the Roman Column arid the
Tomb of Hercules. The six Romans stand on Sandow’s
chest in the Tomb act, and they do not subdue him. He
rests face upwards on his hands and feet, forming a table
on which platform the six Romans mount, taking with
them all the available dumb-bells and having a face-value
of, say, seven or eight hundredweight.
* * * •
The v\ hole show is a queer mixture of marvel and moon-
shine. The beautiful development of his body and his
enormous strength on the one side, and on the other side
a number of tricks that one expects to find among men
of the Samson class. But most play-actors find it easier
to win applause by bizarre effects than by genuine work.

So far, the Conference of Premiers has
made very little progress in the matter of the pro-
posed preferential duties. Things remain at this
point: That Britain wants Australia to give a
preference to its goods by reduced duties, while
Britain admits the goods of Australia on no better
terms than those of all other countries. Yet, as
we buy more from Britain than Britain buys from
us, it looks as if we had some claim for a preference
to make things square. One reason why “the
colonies” are comparatively unprogressive, and
aren’t in a position to rise up and lick the mother
country’s enemies as they might do, is that the
mother country seems to prefer buying from all
her outside enemies rather than from them.

“Alone,” re “ Galleryite’s ” criticism of
the Irvine Ministry :
“ Galleryite ” is, in one particular, manifestly wrong.
He says, “ Irvine is too haughty even to see his constitu-
ents when they come to the city. That is the way Irvine
and men like him demonstrate their superiority.” Now,
if that statement were strictly true it would be none the
less a compliment to the man, but it isn’t strictly true.
Any of Irvine’s constituents with any business worthy of
the attention of the representative of the whole con-
stituency can rely on seeing him, and getting courteous
consideration. But, whatever his political weaknesses, he
has the statesman’s abhorrence of the sneaking K.va-
bramapootra with his “political-influence” jobs, who
buzzes round the pandering “ roads and culverts ” type
of politician, till Parliament, instead of being an aggre-
gation of the country’s statesmen, is fast degenerating
into a society of glib-tongued log-rollers and mean-
spirited humbugs. In the eye of every Democrat the
comparative exclusiveness of Irvine and a few others is
at least a suggestion of honesty.
Chermside, of Queensland, is the only
Australian Governor who, on his own motion,
proposed a reduction of his yearly salary. From
the days of Gov. Phillip any suggestion (and the
suggestions were frequent) to reduce salary was
always determinedly opposed by the holders of
the office. Finally, the British Govt, laid down
what has had the effect of law since—that the
salary of no Governor is to be reduced during the
term of his office. If Gov. Chermside's voluntary
reduction proposal be accepted, the Colonial Office
may or may not interfere. It certainly will be
quotable as a precedent of a reduction taking
place during the term of occupancy ; but difficul-
ties can be avoided by the Governor accepting his
full salary and donating a cheque to the Consoli-
dated revenue. In a somewhat analogous case
Bruce Smith and other members of N.S.W. Leg.
Assembly, and others in Victoria, declined statu-
tory salaries, but in after years drew them as
arrears of pay.

When the Owe’Sullivan unfurled a banner
recently for N.S.W. railway men he gushed:
“Standards, banners, symbols, and devices have
always played an important part in the history of
humanity.” Not a doubt of it! Because human-
ity has been emerging from barbarism —from the
red-feather and tattoo stage. When men become
really civilised such things will be left to children.
The Purnim giant, the youthful 7ft. 4in.
Gippslander, who has been shown round the coun-
try as a startling natural product, has just applied
for an old age pension, although only 25. The
giant’s heart, it runs, has proved quite unequal to
the task of irrigating his system, arid the doctors
have ordered him on to his back for the remainder
of his days. The Purnim giant made his first ap-
pearance in history when his father was sued for
not sending his little boy to school. The little
boy was called into court, and was found to be
about 2£ft. higher than his father, and large enough
to overawe the Bench. Unluckily for the giant of
Purnim, Nature, in supplying his enormous bulk,
did not fit him with a pump to suit. That is very
often the way with the long man. It is his heart
that gives out first.
The death, at the age of 83, of William
Hanson, first N.S.W. Govt, printer, recalls the
early days of Sydney journalism, when S.M.
Herald had absolutely its own way, scooped in
the shekels, and kept the daily press on a dull
level of sanctimonious propriety. It was after
Henry Parkes had failed to make a success of the
Empire, as a second morning paper, that Samuel
Bennett, a good writer on the Herald staff, and
William Hanson, Govt, printer, tried their ’pren-
tice hands on the costly venture. After a
decade of wearisome and ineffectual battling,
Hanson, heavily handicapped by small means and
a large family, was beaten in the fight. By the
irony of fate, the turning-point in his partner’s
fortune arrived soon after, when the first paper of
its kind in the colony—the Evening News —was
started on the ruins of the Empire, bringing
Bennett his earliest stroke of luck. The estab-
lishment of a weekly journal, Town and Country,
combined with free newspaper postage, completed
his fortune, it being a common sight in the bush
then to see heavily-laden wagons, whose sole
freight consisted of Town and Country, being
delivered at the colony’s expense. There was a
flicker of light in Hanson’s later life, when, at the
age of 70, he compiled two books of reference,
the “ Pastoral Possessions of New South Wales ”
and a “Geographical Gazetteer” of the colony.
But he was practically ruined by the Empire, in
which he had embarked everything, and his life
petered out sadly to the end.
Civilisation largely consists of shifting great
quantities of stuff from one place to another, and the
man who takes such a contract will, if he is wise, use
light tramways to do it. With the’ordinary wheelbarrow
a man can shove about 1801 b. along in front of him. With
a tramline and a truck the same man could easily shift
about a ton. If he used a horse to the trucks he could
shift about 10 tons. Also, with the tramline wet weather
does not decrease the hauling power of man or beast as it
does on an ordinary wet, muddy road. Therefore, if
civilisation has placed you in a station of life in which it
is necessary to hump large quantities of material from one
place to another, see Mr. A. E. Leplastrier, manager of
the Central Tramway Co, 40 Hnuter-street, Sydney, and
he will fix you up with anything in the tram business,
from a wheelbarrow run on one rail up to the best patent
chaired tramline with palatial passenger carriages and
first-class steel mining-trucks.*

My Melbourne correspondent continues
“Why will the Art Gallery-girl persist in a
robe of an obsolete character ? She is ever the
same as the years revolve. Her style never alters.
She is the slave of a fixed idea—the aesthetic in
dress. Her hair is tangled till it looks like a
disreputable nest. A rose of many a
summer ago is caught in the hair-twigs above her
left ear. Her level figure, devoid of curve or any
suggestion of Vondulation naturelle, is swathed in
a faded pink or a slowly-dying blue drape. She
is anaemic and sad. So, too, is the average male
artist. After a casual glance round at the warped
brethren of the mahl-stick, it is a relief to rest
the eyes on the big, solid, healthy figure of sculp-
tor Summers. Why should Art, as a rule, put on
a lackadaisacal dying-duck-in-a-thunderstorm
pose?
“At last the secret leaks out why Mrs. Martell
sent in her resignation as the well-paid resident
surgeon of the Women’s Hospital. Mrs. Martell
was treated like a lady-help. She couldn’t take a
breath of fresh air without much detail of per-
mission. Her recreation hours were severely
limited, in consequence of the casual visits of
patients in their extremity. It was required that
she should be always on the spot, ready for all
emergency cases. She felt the strain of continuous
duty, but conscious that she had been warmly
welcomed into the institution by the Catmittee,
and received into the feminine fold with much
grateful mewing, worked on against all odds. At
last her husband interposed, offering to be on
duty while his wife took some rest, but this sug-
gestion the Catmittee squashed immediately and
put a resolute claw down on all infringements of
the most minor rules. So Mrs. Martell handed
in her notice of retirement.
“ A Melbourne August bride is preparing to
take Sydney by storm (she is wedding a well-set-
up widower of Harborville). The lace designed
for the bridal toilette has been made by a sister,
and is claimed by a big chiffon establishment to
be the most exquisite work ever turned out by an
amateur needle-dodger. Yards of this precious
cob-web finery drape the skirt; more of it forms
the vest and sleeves.
“ The dress-builders of the day are raising their
charges immoderately, on the plea of the extra
labor of the needle involved by present fashion of
dresses. The smart society girl who once ran up
her own frock is unable to emulate the elaborate
modes of the day, and the home-made dress is
no longer a possibility. So the dress despots
are making life a vale of tribulation and tears
in a depressed community.
“ A girl is doing Collins-st. these days with an
ebony cane. Every now and then a girl breaks
out with the stick craze, but finds the fashion
doesn’t prosper much. One who leads her Pomera-
nian poodle by a silver chain is a bigger draw.
“The small sister of Amy Castles is being
coached diligently in French, German, and
Italian, preparatory to starting on a singing tour.
The fair, pretty, pert young ‘ Dolly ’ is a smarter
girl than Amy —more precocious and more appre-
ciative of this world’s possessions.
“ A certain prominent official gives private
views to his friends of the golden gewgaw presented
by the Ducal Atom last year. ‘Come to tea,’ he
says, ‘and I’ll show you the Duke’s present to
me.’ The tea he gives first, and the private view
after. This has been his chief amusement ever
since this State lost the fragrant presence of
Royalty. Only womenkind go, and even they
giggle inordinately. The faded flowers of crawlty
are not sentimental —only funny.
“Both the Melb. morning papers have paid
‘An Australian Girl in London ’ the compli-
ment of copying her article, ‘ The Omnibus Top,’
from the (London) Daily Mail. The writer,
‘M.C.E.’, is Mrs. Elkington, wife of Dr. ‘Jack’
Elkington, who is now in London studying small-
pox at headquarters, to qualify for a S.P.H.
degree. Mrs. Elkington, a sister of Mrs. Norman
Lindsay, is a clever, attractive girl who talks well
and writes as naturally as she talks.
“Mr. Justice Williams’ imposing daughter
sports a most artistic winter garb these veiled
days of frost and calm. It is a cinnamon-brown,
concertina-like velvet,- made with a very pouched
blouse and the long, plain, trailing, germ-collecting
tail. With it the theatrical-looking charmer
sports a jaunty toque of plateau shape, veiled in
cinnamon tulle, with delicate pink flower borders
about the rim and spreading over the crown.
The brown autumn tint is very telling.
“The ‘kitchen tea’ is catching on like Kya-
bramapootra reform. The idea, that must surely
have orginated in a poor quarter where the ddt-
less bride was in quandary to start the time-pay-
ment home, has set smart Society by the ears,
and every swagger bride these days approves of
her friends furnishing her kitchen in this hoydenish
manner.
“A whole ball was spoilt _ the other night
through a small and trivial mistake. A box of
floor-wax, looking like the legitimate face-powder,
but really composed of candle-grease and other
slippery stuff, was left on the dressing table of the
cloak-room. Girls excitedly rushing in, and
peeling off cloaks and wraps, made a dab
at the powder-box and whitened their cold
red noses. The stuff stuck horribly. It had
a strange feeling and a stranger smell. The
girls turned to each other asking the reason,
and then commenced furiously rubbing it off—or
rather in. The effect was ludicrous. Others who
had rouged their faces before coming washed the
hateful stuff out with their vexed tears. The
dressing-room presented a strange scene, and red-
nosed unhappy girls faced the music in sulks, and
in earnest prayer that the night would pass quickly
and end their humiliation.
“ An evil form of the three-quarter coat, which
seems to be one of the possessions of every happy
home, is the latest white lustre and linen variety.
The long, loose hang of the white linen thing,
with its two side slits, resembles so closely
a certain male garment of the common or garden
variety, that even the hardy annual sort of
woman blushes at the sight of it. At a recent
race-meeting a well-advertised widow wore such a
garment in green cloth, over a gorgeous black-
and-white frock, and mere man wanted to know
in his brutal fashion why she covered such a
wealth of material with a man’s shirt.
“Letters from Alice Crawford give glowing
accounts of the happy family relations existing in
Wilson Barrett’s co. The big bouncing Alice had
one grave fault which her friends were ever
striving to correct in her. She had no ‘ applica-
tion,’ and did not (they say) ever do herself and
her talents justice. What she learnt, she acquired
in a hasty way, and was a young person much
given to lounging luxuriously and lending a hand-
some ear to the encouraging eulogies of her
friends. But her recent letters show an emulative
spirit. Alice Crawford has the necessary tem-
perament, and a rather magnetic presence.

IN calling your attention to the “ Daily Mail ” final withdrawal of the “ Encyclopaedia Bkitannioa ” offer in New South Wales, we have but little to add to what we have already said in these columns.

and to these we have replied : . . .
(1) The full 26 volumes are forwarded immediately upon receipt of the order, or within 24 to 36 hours. Stock is carried temporarily in Sydney. . , , • ,

at nearest railway and steamship agencies. , , ,
(3) The books weigh 2101 b., and are carefully packed in London in a zinc-lined or tar-paper-lined case. The bookcase weighs about 851 b., and the carriage is about the same as that ot a set o books.

sence of means of communication with the guard'
of long-distance Vic. trains by the recent sen-
sational trouble with a suicidal madman, who
wished to jump out. The passengers right along
the tram were helpless spectators of what threat-
ened every moment to be a ghastly horror; but
the guard and the men in charge of the engine
were quite unconscious of events until a gang of
line-repairers happened in sight, just in time to
stop the tram and prevent the madman’s brains
being dashed out against a post. On some of the
Vic. lines, trains, running for full-hour stretches
without stoppage, are without any means of
drawing the attention of the guard when anything
goes wrong.
A Melbourne father appeared in the dock,
the other day, charged with manslaughter of his
daughter, aged 10. The child had been ill in bed for
13 months, and the evidence of three doctors was
absolutely horrifying. “ She was in a low condi-
tion and filthy and neglected. . . Never saw such
a number of bed-sores on any human being before.
• • •. She must have suffered untold agony for a
considerable period. . . She was extremely ema-
ciated and extremely dirty. . . Nails of hands
and feet were fully an inch long and grown into
the flesh. . . The child was covered with dirt
from head to foot, and the dirt had to be scraped
from her ; the hair was full of vermin.” At the
hospital it took four hours to cleanse her. Certain
medicos advised her removal to the hospital, but
another, since dead, was against it, and as the
child herself disliked the notion, nothing was
done until too late. The father, who was dis-
charged by the Judge’s direction, seems to have
done all in his power except to personally attend to
the little one’s physical needs ; that he left to his
eldest daughter, aged 27 (his wife was dead). The
child apparently died through dirt, neglect, and
through everybody’s business being nobody’s busi-
ness. Brethren, Brother Snufflebust will now
take up a collection for the heathens in China,
who abandon some of their new-born girl-babies.
At the Sydney Cyclorama, old Jerusalem,
after years of service, has given place to the old
Battle of Gettysburg, and the tin thunder that
expressed Hebrew alarm now comes in useful for
war effects. It is a pale green and yellow
battle, with real bottles in the foreground
and a painted haystack in the middle dis-
tance. Most people go to a Cyclorama feel-
ing annoyed because they are sure they will
be able to see through the illusion; they leave
feeling equally annoyed because they can’t. The
point where the real and unreal meet may, of
course, be distinguished by sharp-sighted persons.
Nevertheless, the pale, green hills have the neces-
sary distant look, and the misty American sky
extends far over what ought to be Redfern rail-
way station. Twice a day someone sings the
usual war-ballad about the soldier who says
“Mother,” and a lecturer makes remarks
on the leaders Lee, Ewell, &c. Jackson
wasn’t there, being unavoidably dead. Jack-
son was the Confederate leader who didn’t
shoot the aged lady for waving the wrong flag
over him. In all ages certain women have been
much praised for saying “ Shoot, if you will, this
old, grey head” (or young head), under circum-
stances when it is certain that the head in question,
being feminine, won’t be shot. These cases are
then instanced by lady lecturers as showing that
women are as brave as men. The Cyclorama con-
cludes with some ordinary illusion effects and a
cinematograph, mainly of a bucking horse, which
sometimes bucks on the top of the canvas and
sometimes plunges underneath, owing to apparent
insobriety on the part of the cinematograph.
The greatest relief has been furnsihed
mothers by the introduction of the
Baby Jumper. It works on springs,
and the slightest movement of the
child causes it to rock, and the in-
fant goes off to sleep—in fact, one
father describes it as saving wages
of two nurse-girls. It is on sale
at Lassetter’s, Hordern’s, Farmer’s,
in Sydney; at Finny Isle’s, in Bris-
bane ; and at Tye’s, in Melbourne.

New Zealand Loan and M. A. Co., Ltd., reports
—for week ended July 26th—that wool has been in
steady private demand at same old prices. At London
July sales quotations remained unchanged; merinos
firm ; crossbreds in better demand. Sheepskin supplies
are very heavy, but the majority are skins taken from
starved sheep. Market firm for fine-woolled, but weaker
for coarser and rougher sorts. No shipping inquiry for
tallow, which nevertheless maintains its prices. Strong
inquiry for kangaroo, wallaroo and wallaby skins. Swamp
wallabies made very high.’ Possums fell from6d. tolsd. doz.
Lightweight hides much neglected, supplies from starved
cattle being large. Fairly animated demand for other
descriptions and weights at about last week’s rates.
Hoofs and horns in active demand. Horsehair advanced
Jd. per lb. Wheat still ranges from 4s. 4d. to 4s. 4Jd. in
the city, while the co. has paid the Sydney equivalent of
4s. sd. to 4s. 6d. for several parcels in the country for
stock-feeding purposes. Maize still selling at 4s. lid. to
6s. per bushel, but considerable shipments from Argentina
are expected to cause a slump. Flour : On actual sales
best City Roller is worth £9 10s., and other brands
£8 10s. to £9 ss. per ton ; Manitoba, £l2 10s.
While in S’Africa, King Seddon did some
tall talking; his continual reiteration of “we
intend to present you with a fleet of steamers to
open up direct communication between S’Africa
and Maoriland,” made the Natalians espe-
cially very angry. They resented the pat-
ronising manner of. the “ great Imperialist,”
and at one meeting an old man asked
him in Taal if the steamers were to be
presented to the Govts, of Natal and Cape
Colony, and did the M.L. farmers and manufac-
turers hope to make any profit out of the presen-
tation. Dick squelched this ignorant disloyalist
by starting to belch “ Gorsave ” in his most terrific
basso, and, after that, there were no more inter-
ruptions. He made a bellowing pandemonium,
and called it approval.

Henry Irving and Ellen Terry, it is said, have deter-
