“ W.K.H.”: “ Who were the three Lelliots who are now
appearing in America, and are said to be Australians ? ”
The Lelliots came from Deniliquin (N.S.W.) Several of
the sons showed great musical talent, as boys. One of
them was a clever violinist, and another was a very fine
trombone player. I heard some time ago than the
Lelliots were in America. The boys were carpenters by
trade, but all the family were very musical.
If Mrs. Pat. Campbell comes to Australia under engage-
ment to Musgrove (she is not coming under engagement
to Williamson, anyhow) what will the lady of the highly-
strung nerves produce for her trump-card ? She has three
or four brilliant successes to her name, and several
failures. Mrs. Tanqueray and Magda are her strongest
parts. There might be some money in “ Magda,” because
an artistic and whole-hearted treatment of the character
in which Nance O’Neil bluffed Australia with her huge
self-assurance would be a revelation. But “ Tanqueray ”
has been played again and again, by the Broughs, to
moderate business.
Kelly, employed as “ the Terrible Turk ” in Williamson’s
“ Circus Girl,” at Melb. Majesty’s, is an ex-policeman, and
the largest on the job. He looks quite 7ft. high along-
side Lauri, and probably weighs little less than 30st.
He is, perhaps, the nearest approach to the giants of the
fairy tales that Victoria has produced, and has taken
prizes in his time as a thing of beauty. His first appear-
ance on any stage was at a Bendigo carnival, where he
took the cake for beauty, grace, and style in a handsome-
man competition, winning on points of weight and
measurement.
S.M. Herald stated last week that Melba would cer-
tainly be re-introduced to the Australian public at Mel-
bourne Town Hall, and not at the Princess’s Theatre or
the Exhibition Building. All the Bame, S.M.H. doesn’t
know. It merely surmises.
“ Melb.” : The amateur “ Runaway Girl,” on the press
verdict, was excellent —so it was. Book Arcadian Cole’s
monkeys turned loose on the stage. All monkeyed.
Everyone of them was a mere mimic’s mimic, imita-
tor of the player at Her Majesty’s. The ghosts of Carrie
Moore, Florrie Young, Lauri, Ward, Vernon, Bantock,
and so on down to the humblest. Three months’ inces-
sant devotion to this balderdash can only come to such a
result. Fathers and mothers tearing their hair.
“ T.D.” : One thing I notide about Piddock, the bari-
tone of “ A Chinese Honeymoon,” is that he’s a genuine
worker ; it seems difficult to get tenors and baritones of
that stamp nowadays. They mostly stand round when
not actually singing or talking, strike attitudes, and
endeavor to look pretty. But Piddock, and Kenningham
of the rival show, both set good examples to their col-
leagues by keeping on the move, and for that reason they
are worth much more to the management than the help-
less personages aforesaid.
“ E.S.” : Music teachers of Melbourne are recommend-
ing their pupils to copy the American soprano, Electa
Gifford, as to methods of breathing. The sages of the
singing world say her method is perfect. No effort, cer-
tainly, is apparent. Her throat is small and graceful. It
is better to watch that than her mouth when she sings,
for her beauty is completely sacrificed in the engineering
of those artistic vocal passages, and her small mouth (its
brevity is a blessing) forms loops, and circles, and slots,
and egg-shapes as she sends out her notes.
“ Vul.”: Re Cinquevalli’s unique feat of catching a
heavy cannon ball upon his neck from a dizzy height. He
tells an American interviewer how he learned the trick.
“ I began learning with a wooden bail, weighing about
lib. Even then it often knocked me unconscious. But I
kept on practising, and gradually increased the weight of
the ball. Yes, I catch it on the back of my neck. Of
course, if I made the least mistake there would be a bad
accident. Afterwards, I throw the ball into the air and
catch it on the edge of a plate. I can do the same feat
with an egg. I practised the trick tor nine years before I
performed it in public.”
The reason pianist Lachaume could not be persuaded
at the last moment to accompany Gerardy to Australia
was that his wife objected either to part from or travel
overseas with him- a state of things that bodes badly
for Lachaume’s future as a musician and a man of soui.
Canby was lucky in picking up, instead of Lachaume, the
businesslike Gottfried Galston, probably the most un-
affected star pianist yet seen in Australia. Galston goes
far to disprove the apparent belief that the strength of a
musician, life Samson’s of old, lies in the possession of
a huge thatch.
Alfred Harding, of Melbourne, cut his
throat in the City Baths. In choosing such a
place, Alfred showed consideration and good feel-
ing, but the job was not a striking success. He
lost a good deal of blood, but did not find himself
dying as readily as he had expected. Growing
weary of waiting he tied up his neck with a
handkerchief, went home in a casual sort of way,
and after mooning about the house for a while
went to bed. This happened on Sunday, and
Harding turned up at Melb. Hospital on the
following Friday, and presented himself for -
repairs. It was found that the main artery had !
been cut half through, but that the bleeding had
been stopped almost miraculously by a little con- I
gealed blood in the wound. It took a few yards
of material to sew Alfred up again, and now he is '
fit to fell trees.

WHAT THE WORLD’S SCIENTISTS SAY-
THAT half of the nervous affections from which people suffer arise through imperfect and insufficient nerve force. Rob the body of animal magnetism, and paralysis and dis-
order of the chief functions follow. Pluck a flower —it withers. The principle is the same. It is one which involves Death. _ . .
Physicians of the widest experience and highest attainment declare that where nervous disorders arise from structural changes in the great nerve centres, the treatment
which lends itself with direct adaptability and possessing most curative results is that of Electricity combined with medical treatment. Many eminent professional medical experts
assert this fact —that where nervous exhaustion and functional disorders are present, symptoms show themselves in sleeplessness, great muscular debility, headache, backache, mental
incapacity, loss of appetite with dyspepsia, and constipation, weariness or a condition of weariness and unrest. The intensity and variety of these symptoms indicate how greatly
the system is affected.
Electricity adopted judiciously with restorative medicines have powerful reconstructive influences. An electric “ Invigorator.” worn around the loins for a few hours'
nightly will effect a great and lasting change by pouring into the nerves that necessary vitality which being absent forms the nucleus of disease.
Read the two following testimonials from persons who have suffered and tried the treatment. Then if you are ill, consult the Specialists and speak likewise.

“D.” : Ee the purloining of Bulletin in
transit through the post: Speaking from actual
P.O. experience, I don’t think the B. is often
actually stolen, although it is very commonly
delayed. A dull dinner-hour—nothing to read—a
B. -within reach of your hand, and you with the
right to open any newspaper to see that it doesn’t
contain written matter —and “ where are you?”
I mail my copy to my father, and he gets it (33
miles to travel)—three days afterwards.

Mr. W. G. Hearne.—Dear Sir,—l am writing to tell you
about the wonderful cure your medicine has effected in
my case. About three years ago I began to cough. At
first the cough was not severe, but it gradually got worse,
and I became very weak and troubled with night sweats,
pain in my chest, and great quantities of phlegm. On
several occasions there was blood in the expectorated
matter. I had been treated by a doctor, who pronounced
myrease to be Consumption, and various other treatments
had been tried, but without benefit. It was at this stage
that I heard of your Bronchitis Cure, and sent to you for
a'course of the medicine. When it arrived I was too ill to
leave my bed, but I commenced taking it at once, and
gradually improved. lam glad to say that the two lots
of medicine you sent have effected a complete cure, for
which accept my very best thanks.—Yours gratefully,
J. BLAIR.

Your letter and Bronchitis Cure to hand quite safe.
I am sure you will be glad to know that your Bronchitis
Cure has quite cured me. I was very glad when it came,
as I was suffering from a severe attack of Bronchitis at the
time it arrived. I had sent for my own doctor, but had
not had one night’s rest for a week. I started taking the
Bronchitis Cure three times a day as directed, and was
very much eased at once. At the end of a week I only

HER DAUGHTER HAD BEEN VERY ILL.
SPITTING UP BLOOD.

A strange story of the suddenness of a,
panic-stricken Melbourne drapery firm. First of
all the firm (Foy and Gibson) sold a bag to a com-
plete stranger and took payment by a cheque for
£1 signed “Brown.” When the bag and the
alleged Brown had departed, somebody suddenly
rushed to the conclusion that the firm had been
swindled, and a young-man fled into the street to
seize Brown. He found Grim Wood instead, and
accused him of being Brown. Grimwood, in-
stead of casting the young man into the middle ot
next week, went with him to the shop, and
was there enthusiastically identified as Brown. A
detective was called and decided, on seeing Grim-
wood’s writing, that he was almost certainly the
man who wrote out the alleged Brown’s cheque.
He then arrested, the protesting Grimwood tor
“stealing a bag”—he had no bag about him, am
there was no sign of one being dropped in the
street—and ran him in. Later on, a detective
went out to inquire at Grimwood’s house, but
he was bundled off the premises. It took
Mrs. Grimwood four hours interviewing seven
different J’s.P. before she got her old man bailed
out. Next day Grimwood was remanded for
a week. And about this time the excited drapery
firm discovered that Brown was an affluent' citizen
whose cheque was quite good, and that Grimwood
was also a decent and “substantial” citizen who
couldn’t be arrested with impunity. The large
firm which made such a large row about
a small cheque is now so subdued that
either Brown or' Grimwood could buy an
umbrella there on credit any day. But as an
eSsay on the value of identifications the whole
business is instructive. If Brown’s cheque had
proved had, and if Grimwood had been a man
without property or influence, he might have got
12 months or so quite off-handedly, by reason of
the cheerful readiness of everybody to recognise
him at first sight. Many men have got more than
that under similar circumstances.

ALONG BACK IN THE BEGINNING OF THE EIGHTIES
Ilerr Rassmussen introduced his noxv world-famed Alfaline Herbal Remedies to the Australian public at 547 George
Street, Sydney. Australia was then flooded with American importations and proprietary remedies, manufactured

responsibility, and very often without giving them fair value for same.
HERR RASSMUSSEN BROUGHT ABOUT A REVOLUTION
by straightforward and fair dealings with the public, offering to stake his reputation and future if his remedies
were not as efficacious as he claimed them to be, accepting all responsibility, and willing to abide by the people’s
verdict.
THE HAPPY RESULT IS KNOWN TO EVERYONE.
Over twenty years have passed away, hundreds of remedies have come and gone—many of them were forced on
the public in numerous designing ways—thousands of pounds spent on advertising same with the assistance of the
world’s grea est experts in laying traps for the unwary ; but the public would have none of them, and they have
disappeared as suddenly as a flash of lightning.
“THE ALFALINE REMEDIES EXCELL ALL OTHERS,”

have retained the goodwill and confidence of the Australian public, and have become famous throughout the

Melbourne, Brisbane, Adelaide, .Wellington, N.Z., and in West Australia at Perth, Fremantle, Italgoorlie and

201 Bourke Street, Melbourne
Lost.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There were three of us—Caddy the musician,
Bourke, and myself. Caddy was a thin, small-
sized man, and I was no giant, but Bourke was a
fine, big, upstanding fellow with broad shoulders
and a long black beard of which he was inordi-
nately proud. Sometimes, when in a mood of
romantic reminiscence, he would caress this fall
of hair with both hands and remark: “This is
what makes them leave their situations. ” He had
been captain in a dragoon regiment at one time
and had sold-out. That was in the good old days
when a commission had a cash value in the market.
There appeared to be good old days almost every-
where at that period, and so when Bourke arrived
in Victoria bearing introductions to the right kind
of people he found no difficulty in obtaining an
excellent Government billet. He lost it on
Black Wednesday—that Day of Judgment for so
many high-placed Victorian civil servants. Then
he went into partnership with a contractor, and,
as he knew nothing of business, and the contractor
knew more than enough for two, he lost his capi-
tal, and took to the bush. Not with a gun, how-
ever, but with a swag. That was how we made
his acquaintance. Caddy and I were sundowning
our way from Melbourne to Queanbeyan, where
we hoped to find a friend who owned a gold-mine.
We met Bourke on the other side of Albury. He
was on his way to Urana to get a job at burr-
cutting. But his heart was not wedded to the
prospect, and he agreed to throw in his lot with
us and become one of the partners in the gold-
mine, if there proved to be one.
So we found ourselves leaving Tumut with two
large loaves, some tea and sugar, and a leg of
mutton. We had to buy these, as there is a pre-
judice in the towns against sundowners. Caddy
was the smallest man of the company, but he
carried the biggest swag, because he hated to pass
by anything he saw lying around loose, and
which he thought might come in handy later on.
So it happened that when we left Tumut his swag
was increased in weight to the extent of six or
seven pounds by the addition of a shovel-blade
wfjich he picked up on the road. “ It will make a
fine frying-pan,” he said. Bourke looked at me,
but neither of us smiled. If Caddy chose to
make a pack-mule of himself by carrying luxuries,
why should we discourage him ? The benefit
would be ours also.
* * * *
I remember well the last homestead we called at
before we were lest to civilisation. It was a cattle-
station, and, from the munificent manner in which
we were treated, it was evident that sundowners
were rare birds of passage in that part of the
country. They brought us out roast beef on a
lordly dish, and home-made bread, and tea and —
O Heaven, were we awake or dreaming ? —Cake !
Then they filled our bags as full as they would hold
and we went on our way, leaving blessings behind
us, That night, as we sat before a blazing log in a
little green gully between two hills, and smoked
and yarned and felt at peace with all created
things, two horsemen rode up and asked us for a
pannikin of tea ; and, when they departed, they
left us a cake of tobacco about the size of a mile-
stone.
* » * *
We had been told that at a certain point, where
a great tree stood towering high over its fellows,
a blazed track began. We were to keep to this
track until we came to some other conspicuous
landmark—nature of which I have forgotten.
Then we were to branch off upon another track
and keep to that until a third striking feature in
the landscape arrested our attention. I forget
this feature also. This was a sign for us to take
yet another track, which would eventually lead us
to a homestead, at which we could make inquiries
for further tracks and features. We followed our
instructions exactly, as we thought. But when
you tramp to the top of one mountain to find a
higher mountain in front of you, and range beyond
range looming in the distance, and every mountain
and every range looks much alike, the results
are apt to be confusing. We met with many
mountains, many tall trees, many tracks which
all died away. Then we met many trackless
mountains, and at last, when we got to a mountain
that looked precisely like twenty-four previous
mountains, we had consumed the last of our pro-
visions. Fortunately we had tobacco. The time
was near when I would regret that I had never
learned to chew. Night fell, and we made a fire
and boiled the billy. We had tea, which was
another comfort. But we went to sleep feeling
very hungry. In the morning when we awoke the
mountains were all around—
Ohin on hand,
Like giants at a hunting lay.
But we thought of the old Scotch saying, “A
stoot hairt to a stae brae, ” and we faced them once
more. Once or twice during that long and dread-
ful day we came across something that looked like
a track, but when we followed it up it led nowhere.
The strain told first upon Bourke, probably be-
cause he was the biggest man, and had the greatest
burden of flesh and hair to carry. Moreover he
was the largest eater, and depriving him of his
breakfast meant depriving him of a great deal
more food than a smaller man would have lost
under like circumstances. As night fell again he
became taciturn and gloomy and lost all pride in
his beard. I slept soundly enough for the first
two or three hours, and when I awoke the others
seemed to be sleeping in a troubled, uneasy way.
Bourke was cursing somebody in his dreams. I
made-up the fire and went to sleep again. It took
a lot of trouble to worry me in those days. But
when the sun arose, and we saw the mountains
looking down upon us in silent scorn, and locking
us in. from the world of living men, we fully
realised for the first time that we were Lost.
Another day passed. Bourke by this time was
morose and sullen, and refused even to make re-
marks about his military career.
All at once we found ourselves standing above a
pleasant little green valley with a stream running
through it and—most delightful sight of all—-a
hut at the bend of the stream. We tumbled
rather than walked down the side of the hill. And
when we entered the hut it was empty and the
fireplace cold! The place had been, probably,
untenanted for years. We camped there all the
same, and I awoke early in the morning to find
Bourke sitting up and looking at us with a canni-
bal glare in the eyes. A cold shiver went through
me as I thought that I might be eaten by a British
officer in these solitudes, and I went out and tried
to kill a fish in the creek with a stone. I did not
succeed. Then I found a tree with congealed
gum in crevices of its bark. I ate some of the
gum, and was sorry afterwards. Subsequently
we saw a sheep and tried to catch it; but it was a
fast sheep, and left us far behind.
The day wore on, and at last we found ourselves
on a flat beside a great river. Faraway in the
distance what seemed to be smoke was rising.
“ There is a house there, or a camp anyhow,” I
said.
But Bourke was sour and unbelieving. He said
it was mist from the river, and throwing his swag
down, said he would go no further. He said he
preferred to die just there. Gaddy was more
hopeful. But I, who had said that the vapor in
the distance was real domestic smoke, felt in
honor bound to prove my assertion or fall in the
attempt. So I left Caddy to look after Bourke
in his last moments, and take any dying message
he had to give. And I pushed on.
It was real smoke—and it rose not from one
house, but from two. I went back and told .my
mates. Bourke put off dying for the occasion,
and that night I knew for the first time in.my life
that there is a pleasure to be found even in indi-
gestion.
D.
A Strike at The Golden Yell.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The boss gazed at the typed sheet of the com-
pany’s note-paper that crackled in his fist. Then
he scowled like a walking Day of Judgment at the
approaching procession, and kicked his dog amid-
ships. “ Damn the Governor —and the directors
—and all Creation ! ”
Peters had just come up the shaft for a breeze.
“What’s up, Tom?” he queried, compassionately.
Matthews buzzed round. “What’s up! Every-
thing’s up ! Here’s the worm of a chairman
fetching the Governor and about 50 other claw-
hammered swells to inspect the show at half an-
hour’s notice — that’s what’s up ! Here’s the
poverty-stricken, Heaven-forsaken show all tails-
up with care, and that push of wasters coming to
crawl all over it.”
Peters eyed the impending array of visitors, and
blinked sadly fora minute. Then he got back to
the shaft and signalled for the “murderer.”
He got out at the 300 ft., wandered along the
drive into the underlay, and sang out to the
nearest toilers:
“Hey! you fellows! Get your hair brushed
an’ your dress-suits on ! Visitors ahead ! ”
A large man in a green-and-yellow shirt leaned
a 12-inch “ tom ” against the world, and gazed
at Peters tiredly. “Throttle all visitors,” he
said, in a foot-sore voice. Peters raked his head
with his fingers extra-thoughtfully.
“ Lessee,” he muttered in the quiet fastnesses of
an old winze. “Tom don’t want them pot-hatted
coots to get past the brace nohow. It ain’t the
show’s receivin’-day. Everythink’s in a mess,
and there ain’t been no carpets laid along the
drives for them howlers to walk on, nor nothink.
There oughter be a way o’ blockin’ them for him,
somehowruther. ”
Suddenly “’Ooray,” he shouted; “ ’Ooray !
there’s a strike on ! ”
He got out of the winze into the old drive,
where a score or more of practical geologists were
hard at work. “I say, you coves, Mr. Matthews
don’t want that buckram-shirted tribe to git below
the surface. He’s showin’ ’em around on protest
up on top now. Sling down your tools the whole
lot of yer, an’ up the shaft like lightning ! Say
you want another bob a shift. Say you want
creation, an’ the ventilation’s rotten, but say an’
do somethink ! Matthews ’ll love you for ever if
you do, an’ I’ll get him to split the difference and
make it a sprat—the rise, I mean.”
They were dumb for a minute, and Peters re-
addressed the man in the green and yellow shirt:
“Go on, Seymour; you take the lead an’ do the
spoutin’. Up the blow-hole with you ! ”
The shirt grinned loudly. “I’m your Moses!
Chuck away all them scientifik implemence, you
coves.”
The coves smiled; Peters nodded at them
gratefully, and then bounced off along drive and
crosscut to spread the glad tidings.
« * • •
Manager Matthews glanced wearily from the
clattering “30-head ” to the visitors. The chair-
man —a fat, sprawling stack of arrogant loquacity
—caught Matthews’ glittering eye, and thought
the light in it arose from gladness. “ Ah, yes,
Mr. Matthews, his Excellency will now go below.
You have everything all rights I presume?”
Matthews smiled,, with a struggle, then —
“ Hello ! what the ”
Seymour, in his W ;ird shirt, was standing by
the poppet-legs with a dozen other strange clayey
men. As Matthews spoke, up came the “delivery-
cart” with another contingent.
The manager eyed them dumbly for a second,
then his tongue slid into gear again.
“What boiled Hades game’s this? What
the devil are you doing up here? Your shift’s
not up! ”
Seymour gazed at him, a Niagara of a grin
streaming down his face.
“I know it ain’t, boss. It’s a strike. We
want you ter pay us a blanky bob a shift less, Mr.
Matthews. We all feel that you’re payin’ us too
much at present, sir, an’ the company’s gettin’
nearly old enough for a pension, an’ can’t afford
such wasteful expenditure.”
The chairman moved forward and waved his
paunch at Seymour in a dignified manner.
“You—you insolent dog ! Get off ! ”
Matthews, seeing the joke rising like a sweet,
dim star ahead, interrupted, as scores more of
dirty, mullock-marked geologists kept tumbling
up the shaft.
“Oh,” Matthews said, with intense gravity,
“you want a shilling rise, eh? Supposing you
get it under the ellr ? ”
Seymour smiled, and expectorated out across
the continent. “We’ll kick back, an’start on
him ! ” indicating the company’s chairman.
Mr. Colliber’s cheeks flamed red and his paunch
surged about like a ship at sea.
“Sack every man of them, Matthews,” he
thundered. “ Discharge every man jack of ’em.”
And the visit of inspection ended with this in-
dignant outburst.
■» * * *
Matthews stood in the Palace bar, a brandy-
and-soda in his hand, and gazed lovingly at
Peters. Peters had his hat on his eye and quoted
from the West Australian
The s’rike wish ’curred arr the Gold’n Yell mine las’
week, Whilst zexlncy the Gov’nor was payin’ the com-
p’ny’s proppery private visit, ’as—we un’stand—been
brought to a sarrisfac’ry ’elusion. All the men ’ave gone
back arr an increase sixpence shift. Mirr Matthews ”
Peters laughed hilariously and dropped the
paper heavily on the floor. “Nice li’l s’rike,
warn’ it, Tom ? ” he queried.

An interesting domestic story :
While the plague was rife in Sydney the servant of a
lodging-house keeper took mysteriously ill one morning.
Lodger No. 1 looked at her and ran upstairs to lodger
No. 2, who claimed medical knowledge. “ Oome down,
Dick!” cried he “and have a look at Jessie. I’m sure
she’s got the plague, and I kissed her only last night 1 ”
Dick investigated, and in turn sought the householder.
“ I say, Bill, you’d better look after Tom. The slavey’s
got the plague and he was kissing her last night.”
“ Great Scott! ” exclaimed the boss, “ then I’m in for it,
too ! I kissed her myself last night! ” Dick sniggered,
then suddenly grew grave. “ You weren’t kissing your
wife afterwards, I hope,” he said.
Decided by the magistrates at Millicent
(S.A.) that a man could not recover damages from
two others who had burned him in effigy opposite
his own shop-door, “the Court being unable to
see that he had sustained any damage. ”
At new London Hospital the operation-
rooms are so fitted that no speck of dust can get
in.—
The air supply is drawn from the garden by a glazed
tunnel. It is then driven by means of a large electric fan
through a canvas water screen and cotton-wool filters,
after which it passes into a heating chamber, and is
warmed by steam coils. Every operation takes place in a
kind of glass case, in which everything is sterilised.

PEARS
Ideals.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Ship went sailing to the West,
And left behind the haven warm,
The little town in garlands drest,
The little port secure from storm.
In vain the white-armed women strove
To keep at home their seamen bold ;
They would not stay for lips of love,
They would not stay for lure of gold.
For they were of the roving brood,
Whose hearts may never anchored be—
But time the heaving of their blood
Unto the heaving of the sea.
And some were young, with sky-wide scope
Of vision, full of day-dreams rare ;
And some were old, and their last hope
Was pledged on this adventure fair.
The wind that bore them from the land
Died on the sea with heavy moan,
As one who, in the desert-sand,
Dies lost and friendless and alone.
The moon shone like a scimitar,
The banner of the sun. was furled ;
The ship was like a little star
Lost in a strange, green-water world.
Then rose a vessel on the sea,
With silver sails set falcon-wise.
Her captain cried, “ What men be ye
What is your port and merchandise ?
The captain of the ship spake free—
With cheeks afire and flashing eyes. ■
“ High hearts, and hopes, and dreams,” said he,
“ These be our only merchandise.
. “ The port to which we sail to-day,
And all days, lies below the West—
O captain, canst thou point the way
Unto the Islands of the Blest ? ”
The Corsair captain cried, “Depart 1
Follow thou still thy foolish star—
And yet an arrow in thy heart,
This hour, for thee were better far.”
The ship sailed over that strange sea— • -
The Wind of Joy her mainsail fills—
Down violet valleys of the sea,
O’er silver-plumed and flashing hills.
“ O captain, whither are we bound ? ”
A young man asked with eyes a-stare—
An old man said “ Wouldst thou be drowned,
O fool, on this adventure fair?”
Avose a splendid caravel
Upon the blue horizon’s rim,
Breast-heavy, with a milk-white swell,
And from her deck there rang a hymn.
And on her deck there stood a priest.
“ What port is yours ? ” the captain cried.
“ The Blessed Islands in the East,”
The tall, far-gazing priest replied.
The captain laughed ; the crew with smiles
Watched that vain ship upon its quest—
For well they knew the Blessed Isles
Were far and far within the West.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
Big Bush Lies.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ The Lord sent them a strong delusion, and they
believed a lie. ” It is difficult in any other way to
account for the tales that are told of the Bush of
Australia. Begin where you will—the travelling
swagman for bedrock —and whenever people talk
about him they lie or repeat lies. Big John
O’Shanassy (Vic.) minted a long-lived lie with his
phraseaboutthem “walking about looking for work
and praying to God they may never find it.”
Governor Du Cane, of Tasmania, capped that
when he told a London audience that “on many
big Australian stations the cost of feeding travel-
lers was more than all the other expenses put
together.” “Loafing round the huts nine months
in the year and striking as soon as work comes
on ”is another big lie, authorship obscure. “ Take
up a bit of land and you’ll be independent
in five years ” has been very often said;
how often realised? “If you would only save
your money, you could be taking a trip home
instead of carrying that,” is a lie the agriculturist
has told the swagman thousands of times. “Get
the unemployed people on the land,” John See
says in N.S.W. to-day, as J. B. Patterson said in
Victoria years ago, and the one and the other, we
must suppose, under the same strong delusion.
The truth about all these things sounds just
about as strange to-day as did the tales Caroline
Chisholm told about the treatment female immi-
grants got in the bush 65 years ago. But still the
men who govern the country ought to face the
truth. How can public opinion (necessary pre-
cedent of satisfactory legislative action) be formed
on the pastoral-labor question while the delusion
holds ? Most of the authors of the classic lies are
dead, but the lies live on. What is the popular idea
of the swagman but a sordid sort of Satan going to
and fro in the land, devouring all he can and
doing nothing? The truth is, in the Eastern
States alone, there are from 30,000 to 50,000 swag-
men without whom squatting, as we know it,
could not be carried on—say 40,000 men, who do
not average three months’ work in the year each,
who cannot get more because there is no more for
them ; who, if liquor was knocked out of the land,
could not do more than keep themselves on the
plainest food and the most economical allowance of
tobacco ; who can never think of marrying; to
whom the word home has no practical significance.
Most of them drink the bulk of their earnings.
If they did not, it would be bad for the industry
with which they are associated. A sane and sober
man would hardly carry a swag the second year.
Every squatter and station-manager, clear of
delusion, must know, alt that. Yet who ever
heard a squatter speak of a swagman but as a
burden and a pest ? Governor Du Cane did but re-
Seat what he had been told in the bush. If he had
oubted and asked for evidence he certainly could
not have been furnished with proof that one station
to Australia ever spent £5OO in the year through
travellers’ rations. Think what it means. Allow
2d. for a pint of flour, 2d. more for tea and sugar,
and, to be liberal, another 2d. for meat. (Not
one station in fifty gives a ration of that sort.)
Then the cost per head is 6d., and it will take 40
every day in the year to total only £365. Is there
now, was there ever, a station in Australia
averaging 40 travellers a day throughout the
year? Half of that number would come nearer
the estimate of the biggest and most liberal, and
stations of that cut will require 100 rouseabouts
through their six weeks of shearing. In return
for rations given they have those men fit and
orderly and free of all cost at their hut doors
when wanted. What would it cost to bring them
out from town, and what would town men be
worth when brought out ?
- The farmer’s delusion is grosser than the squat-
ter’s. His harvest-work runs from three days to
three weeks. In the old hand-binding days, the
biggest chance he ever offered to a man to get “a
trip home ” was a shilling an hour, with tucker,
and that for three weeks at the longest. He does
not need much casual labor now, and a few hands
employed regularly may average £4O per year and
found. It takes a lot of years and a lot of saving
to make an independence, or even get a start, out
of that. And yet the air is thick with lies about
the farmers’ labor troubles and the fine field the
bush offers to deserving immigrants.
The politician’s lie about placing people in the
bush—planting them there by some means, and
according to some precedent never fully described
—does not merit much notice.. Nothing ever came
of it yet, and it has been tried in four States.
Nothing much has come out of it in the world:
The general order seems to be, amongst all pro-
gressive peoples, at least, that the cities snail
constantly be recruited from the country districts.
Our quidnuncs of the “sound economics” cult
would reverse all that. And they are the people
who, whether they believe them of not, pro-
mulgate the biggest bush lies of all. Theyjtell us,
bawl it at us unceasingly, that, on the bush alone,
do the cities and all citizens live. The bush is the
place where money is earned and made naturally.
They insinuate that Australian cities are warts on
the corpus of the bush, though they themselves
never get off the warts. Do they ever ask where
all the" money came from that has gone to
make the bush barely habitable, and, as an in-
vestment, one of the poorest outside Rhodesian
mines? Was the loan and mortgage money made
out of the bush or out of any so-called natural
(that is primary) industry in any land ? Is not all
the capital which goes into our bush-development
lent us by those whose revenues come mainly
from manufacturing and distributing the
raw materials we and other like nations pro-
duce ? The man in the Big Bush who grows or
digs may get enough to live meanly, and borrow
enough to carry on with anxiety. The fellow who
handles what is grown and digged will wear the
purple and fine linen always, and do the enter-
taining at home, and the pawnbroking abroad.
Poor old lied-about bushman! Master of the
sources. Lord of the springs of wealth ! Try the
searchlight on him, and he is sadly disappointing.
He knows —90 per cent, of bushmen know —that
the bush is a good place to get out of if any decent
chance offers. And the “sound economist’s”
bush —all-providing, all-supporting—is just about
as real as O’Shanassy’s pray-to-God swagman, or
Du Cane’s squatter, or the farmer’s hands, who, if
they looked after their earnings, might be all
going to the Coronation.
The truth of the matter, the facts of bush life, are
pretty well-known to men who are bred in it.
And what brings them by thousands every year
to every Australian city? Simply such convic-
tions as these. A trade, and regular work at that,
made at union wages, is worth more than the
average 640 acres of land available for selection
in any Australian State. So is a Sure billet at 7s.
a day, or a clerkship at £l5O a year. It is hard
reasoning on a cash basis, not silly hankering
after city life, that brings the young bushman to
town. Admitting their calculations to be right,
and they are easily figured-out, if one regular job
is worth 640 acres, a concern employing 3000
hands would be as good to the State as near
2,000,000 acres ; and one manufacturing city with
100,000 workers worth more than all the Crown
lands remaining in the biggest Australian State.
So our great primary-industry field dwindles
in value and prospect whenever we come to
consider it in comparison with other possibilities.
But we could treble the value of the golden fleece
if we resolutely took its manufacture into our own
hands. And why not? Because we are told
that our destiny is in the “natural industries ” of
the Bush. No other reason than that lie which,
as has already been said, caps all that is told of
the details of the life. When indeed the whole
business is dragged out into the light of common-
sense the old squatter apologists appear as
commonplace street-corner or post-and-rail liars,
while the “sound economists” show as Mun-
chausens.
F.M.
“ Melb.” : They are trying to divide this
city into Orange and Catholic factions. Infatu-
ation. The flank is turned. The bulk of the
population says “ A plague on both your houses ! ”
The poor old ostrich with its head stuck in the
sand ! Orangemen bottle themselves up by the
three thousand in Melb. Town Hall, and shout
“Great is Diana!” Then the Romans have a
similar bottling, and shout for some other Dinah.
Mayor Sam, who lets the hall to either, just about
represents the average public.
A military correspondent writes :
The real Commandant of the Commonwealth forces is
not General Hutton, but Joseph Chamberlain. Natur-
ally, the General would be prompted in every detail that
Could be imagined before he left England, but circum-
stances have arisen, that even the foresight of Chamber-
lain did not provide for, and decisions have had to be
delayed until the receipt of instructions from the genuine
Commandant. When Gen. Hutton assumed office in Mel-
bourne he apparently looked with a kindly eye upon the
rifle-clubs, and there seemed to be every prospect
that _ the excellent system that has prevailed
in Victoria would be adopted throughout Australia.
Then came a series of curious delays, and now just
enough of. the mask has been allowed to drop to indicate
that the rifle clubs will have no earthly chance of con-
tinuance under the old system. Gen. Hutton has let it
be known that he will have none of any such organisa-
tions which are not portion of the military forces and, as
such, under military discipline. In the old days the rifle
clubs of Vic. were amenable to military discipline. Then
they numbered some 2000 men; now they are ten times as
many. The. club-members’objection to being part of a mili-
tary force is plain. They desire the liberty of rifle-practice
at such times as they wish. They do not want drill or
uniforms or bumptious ornamental officers. If
they did, they could join one of the military corps.
But the rifle-club is for the man who desires to
be a good shot, without aspiring to military glory.
Gen. Hutton at first didn’t object to this. But the
local soldiery of the Commonwealth, thanks to Big.iohn
Forrest’s Defence Bill and Hutton’s administration,
exists, not for the-defence of Australia, but for England’s
purposes. The rifle-clubs (with 20,000 members in
Victoria alone), if they are not amenable to military
discipline, may, when the time comes, be Australian,
and not English. From a Chamberlain point of view,
that would be distinctly undesirable ; so the rifle clubs
must go.
